Fourth Figure Conjunctions with Particular/Negative Disjunctives
Author lists additional conjunctions for the fourth figure when disjunctive parts are negative or particular.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the number of valid conjunctive syllogisms in the fourth figure when the disjunctive parts are particular negatives, particular affirmatives, or when the disjunctive itself is particular.

Core Idea
The fourth figure yields 24 conjunctions when variations in disjunctives are included.

The author has already listed four conjunctions for universal affirmative disjunctives. Now he expands to cover cases where disjunctive parts are particular negatives, particular affirmatives, or universal affirmatives (with categoricals changing), and finally when the whole disjunctive is particular. This systematic enumeration shows the thoroughness of Islamic logic in covering all valid forms.

Guided Path
1
Recall the base case
Remember that when the disjunctive is universal affirmative (e.g., 'Every B is J or every D is J'), there are 4 valid conjunctions in the fourth figure. This is the starting point.
2
Identify the three variations
The author lists three additional scenarios: (1) disjunctive parts are particular negatives, (2) disjunctive parts are particular affirmatives or universal affirmatives but with categoricals restricted to universals, and (3) the disjunctive itself is particular.
3
Note the number of new conjunctions for each
For (1) there are 4 conjunctions, for (2) another 4, and for (3) twelve conjunctions. These are 'parallel' to the original base conjunctions.
4
Understand the conclusion type in the third variation
When the disjunctive is particular, the conclusion is 'mutlaqa 'amma' (absolutely general), meaning it is true at all times (perpetual) rather than a quantified statement. This is because the particular disjunctive does not guarantee a universal result.
Compare with the base case where the conclusion is a universal categorical.
5
Compute the total number of conjunctions
Add the base 4 to the additional 4+4+12 = 24 total valid conjunctions for the fourth figure. This matches earlier statements about 24 conjunctions.
The author leaves detailed derivation to the reader, indicating it is easy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كانت أجزاء المنفصلة جزئيات سوالب فقد يأتلف منها مع الحمليـات اقترانات أخرى أربعة موازية لهذه الاقترانات. وإن كانت أجزاء المنفصلة جزئية موجبة أو كليات موجبة فقد يأتلف منها مع الحَمْليات اقترانات أربعة، الحَمْليات فيها كليات موجبة أو كليات سالبة. وإن كانت المنفصلة جزئية فقد يأتلف منها مع الحمليـات اقترانات أخرى اثنا عشر موازية للاقترانات الكائنة من المنفصلة الكلية الموجبة والحَمْليات، إلا أن النتيجة فيها تكون مطلقة عامة.
And if the parts of the disjunctive are particular negatives, then there may be combined from them with the categoricals another four conjunctions parallel to these conjunctions. And if the parts of the disjunctive are particular affirmatives or universal affirmatives, then there may be combined from them with the categoricals four conjunctions, the categoricals in them being universal affirmatives or universal negatives. And if the disjunctive is particular, then there may be combined from them with the categoricals another twelve conjunctions parallel to the conjunctions arising from the universal affirmative disjunctive and the categoricals, except that the conclusion in them is absolutely general.
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage provides further enumerations for the fourth figure. After the initial four conjunctions where the disjunctive parts were universal affirmative, the author now considers variations. First, if the disjunctive parts are particular negatives (e.g., 'sometimes some B is not J or some D is not J'), then there are four additional parallel conjunctions. Second, if the disjunctive parts are particular affirmatives or universal affirmatives (but now the categoricals must be universal, either affirmative or negative), another four conjunctions arise. Third, if the disjunctive itself is particular (e.g., 'sometimes either some B is J or some D is J'), then there are twelve conjunctions parallel to those of the universal affirmative disjunctive, but with the difference that the conclusion is 'mutlaqa 'amma' (absolutely general), meaning it holds without temporal specification (i.e., a perpetual proposition). This shows the systematic enumeration of all valid forms for the fourth figure, totaling 4+4+4+12 = 24, as mentioned earlier. The author leaves the detailed derivation to the reader, indicating it is easy.

Key Terms
جزئيات سوالب
مطلقة عامة
منفصلة جزئية

Key Takeaway
The fourth figure's valid conjunctions are systematically enumerated as 24, covering universal and particular disjunctives with various categorical combinations.

Mixed Syllogisms with Multiple Predicates in the Disjunctive
Author expands on mixed syllogisms with multiple predicates.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the conditional conclusion of a mixed syllogism when the disjunctive parts have different predicates, using the first figure example as a model.

Core Idea
Different predicates in the disjunctive parts produce a conditional, not categorical, conclusion.

In previous lessons, mixed syllogisms involved disjunctives with a shared predicate, yielding a categorical conclusion. Now the author introduces a more complex case: the predicates differ. This changes the conclusion to a conditional (if-then) statement. In this lesson, we will walk through the reasoning for the first figure example and understand how the conclusion is derived.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
Locate the disjunctive premise and the categorical premise. Here the disjunctive is 'Always either every B is J or every D is H' and the categorical is 'Every J is A'. Note that the disjunctive parts have different predicates (J and H).
The disjunctive is universal affirmative in both parts.
2
Analyze the disjunctive structure
The disjunctive is exhaustive: if one part is false, the other must be true. Since the predicates differ, the conclusion cannot directly combine them; instead, we reason hypothetically.
3
Derive the conditional conclusion
Negate the part that has no sharing with the categorical premise (the part containing H). Then apply the categorical premise to the other part (containing J) to get the consequent. The conclusion is 'If it is not that every D is H, then every B is A'.
The antecedent is the negation of the part with H; the consequent is the result of a first-figure categorical syllogism: every B is J and every J is A, so every B is A.
4
Generalize to all figures
The author states that in all four figures, when the disjunctive parts have multiple predicates, the conclusion is an affirmative conditional. The antecedent is either the part that has no sharing or its negation, and the consequent is the result of conjoining the categorical with the other part or its negation.
The conditions for each figure remain the same as when the predicate is single.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كان المحمول فى أجزاء المنفصلة متعدداً، فالحَمْليات أيضاً إما متحدة أو متعددة، فإن كانت متحدة فهي أيضاً لا تخرج عن الأشكال الأربعة، ولنفرض الكلام إذا كانت أجزاء المنفصلة متحدة الكمية والكيفية - وأنت تعرفه في المختلط منها - والحكم في شروط كل شكل، وعدد اقتراناته كما مضى إذا كان المحمول اء الانفصال واحداً، غير أن النتيجة هاهنا في جميع الأشكال لا تكون إلا موجبة مقدمها إما عين الجزء الذي لا اشتراك فيه أو نقيضه، وتاليها نتيجة اقتران الحَمْلية بعين الجزء الآخر أو نقيضه، ونحن نذكر مثال الاقتران الأول من كل شكل، وأنت تعرفه في الباقي؛ إذ هو سهل الاعتبار: أجزاء متصلة: الاقتران الأول من الشكل الأول: من كليتين موجبتين وأجزاء المنفصلة كليّة موجبة، كقولنا: دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما كل "د" "هـ") و(كل "ج" "أ") ونتيجته (إن كان ليس كل "د" "هـ") فـ (كل "ب" "أ") لأنه إذا لم يكن (كل "د" "هـ") فـ (كل "ب" "ج") وكان (كل "ج" "أ") فـ (كل "ب" "أ").
And as for if the predicate in the parts of the disjunctive is multiple, so the categoricals are also either uniform or multiple; if they are uniform, then they also do not depart from the four figures. And we posit the discussion when the parts of the disjunctive are uniform in quantity and quality—and you know that in the mixed ones from it—and the ruling regarding the conditions of each figure and the number of its conjunctions is as before when the predicate of the disjunction is one, except that the conclusion here in all figures is only affirmative, whose antecedent is either the same part that has no sharing or its negation, and its consequent is the result of conjoining the categorical with the same other part or its negation. And we mention an example of the first conjunction from each figure, and you know the rest, for it is easy to consider: continuous parts: the first conjunction of the first figure: from two universal affirmatives and the parts of the disjunctive are universal affirmative, like our saying: 'Always either every B is J or every D is H' and 'every J is A', and its conclusion is 'If it is not that every D is H, then every B is A', because if not (every D is H), then every B is J, and it was that every J is A, so every B is A.
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage addresses a more complex case where the two parts of the disjunctive have different predicates (e.g., one says 'every B is J' and the other 'every D is H' instead of both sharing the same predicate). The author states that the categorical premises may be uniform or multiple, but if they are uniform, the same four figures apply with the same conditions. The key difference is that the conclusion in all figures is now a conditional statement (not a categorical). The conclusion takes the form: 'If not the part that has no sharing, then the result of conjoining the categorical with the other part'. In the example of the first figure, the disjunctive is 'Always either every B is J or every D is H' and the categorical is 'every J is A'. Since the disjunctive parts have different predicates (J and H), the conclusion is a conditional: 'If not every D is H, then every B is A'. The reasoning is: if the second part 'every D is H' is false, then the first part 'every B is J' must be true (since the disjunctive is exhaustive), and by the categorical 'every J is A', we get 'every B is A'. The author leaves the remaining conjunctions to the reader, noting it is straightforward. This demonstrates the flexibility of the mixed syllogism framework when the predicates differ.

Key Terms
محمول متعدد
نتيجة شرطية
مقدم وتالي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Conditional with 'if...then'
C) Disjunctive
D) Particular negative

Key Takeaway
When the disjunctive parts have different predicates, the conclusion of a mixed syllogism becomes a conditional statement whose antecedent negates the part without sharing and whose consequent follows from the categorical premise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the disjunctive: 'Always either every act of negligence (B) is sinful (J) or every act of care (D) is permissible (H)' and the categorical: 'Every sinful act (J) is prohibited (A)', derive the conditional conclusion.
?
For the second figure, if the categorical premise were 'Every A is J' instead of 'Every J is A', would the conditional conclusion change? Explain why or why not.

Third and Fourth Conjunctions of Compound Syllogisms
Defines the third and fourth conjunctions and their variations based on categorical quality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the third and fourth conjunctions of compound syllogisms based on the quality and quantity of their categorical premises.

Core Idea
Compound syllogisms combine a disjunctive with categoricals; varying categoricals yields multiple valid conjunctions.

The author builds on the basic form of compound syllogisms by varying the quality (affirmative/negative) and quantity (universal/particular) of the categorical premises. This yields distinct conjunctions with specific conclusion patterns. Understanding these variations is crucial for recognizing valid argument forms in Islamic logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Recall the basic compound syllogism structure
A compound syllogism (اقتران) consists of one disjunctive premise (منفصلة) and one or more categorical premises (حمليات). The conclusion follows from the combination.
2
Identify the variation in the third conjunction
In the third conjunction, the categorical premises differ in quality: one is affirmative and the other negative. The author gives the example: "Always every B is J and every D is J, and every A is B" (affirmative) and "No R is D" (negative).
The conclusion mirrors this mix: "Some J is A and no R".
3
Analyze the fourth conjunction
In the fourth conjunction, the categoricals are both particular affirmative. The conclusion is also particular: "Some J is A and R".
4
Generalize to other variations
The passage lists additional conjunctions: if the disjunctive parts are particular negatives, four parallel conjunctions; if particular or universal affirmatives, another four; if the disjunctive is particular, twelve conjunctions with absolute general conclusions.
The systematic enumeration exhausts possibilities based on the quantity and quality of the disjunctive parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاقتران الثالث الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن الحَمْليات مختلفة الكيفية، كقولنا: (دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما كل "د" "ج" وكل "أ" "ب") و(لا شيء من "ر" "د") ونتيجته (بعض "ج" "أ" ولا "ر").
الاقتران الرابع الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن الحمليات جزئية موجبة، ونتيجته (بعض "ج" "أ" و "ر").
وإن كانت أجزاء المنفصلة جزئيات سوالب فقد يأتلف منها مع الحمليات اقترانات أخرى أربعة موازية لهذه الاقترانات. وإن كانت أجزاء المنفصلة جزئية موجبة أو كليات موجبة فقد يأتلف منها مع الحمليات اقترانات أربعة ، الحمليات فيها كليات موجبة أو كليات سالبة. وإن كانت المنفصلة جزئية فقد يأتلف منها مع الحمليات اقترانات أخرى اثنا عشر موازية للاقترانات الكائنة من المنفصلة الكلية الموجبة والحَمْليات، إلا أن النتيجة فيها تكون مطلقة عامة.
The third conjunction: the conjunction according to its condition, except that the categoricals differ in quality, like our saying: (Always every 'B' is 'J' and every 'D' is 'J', and every 'A' is 'B') and (No 'R' is 'D') and its conclusion is (Some 'J' is 'A' and no 'R'). The fourth conjunction: the conjunction according to its condition, except that the categoricals are particular affirmative, and its conclusion is (Some 'J' is 'A' and 'R'). And if the parts of the disjunctive are particular negatives, then from them with the categoricals another four conjunctions parallel to these conjunctions may be composed. And if the parts of the disjunctive are particular affirmative or universal affirmative, then from them with the categoricals four conjunctions may be composed, the categoricals in them being universal affirmative or universal negative. And if the disjunctive is particular, then from it with the categoricals another twelve conjunctions may be composed parallel to the conjunctions composed from the universal affirmative disjunctive and the categoricals, except that the conclusion in them is absolute general.
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage defines the third and fourth forms of compound syllogisms (اقترانات) that involve a disjunctive premise (منفصلة) and categorical premises (حمليات). The author builds on previous conjunctions but now varies the quality or quantity of the categoricals. In the third conjunction, the categoricals differ in quality: one is affirmative, the other negative, leading to a conclusion with both affirmative and negative parts. In the fourth conjunction, the categoricals are particular affirmative, giving a particular conclusion. The author then generalizes: if the disjunctive parts are particular negatives, there are four parallel conjunctions; if particular or universal affirmatives, another four; if the disjunctive itself is particular, twelve conjunctions with absolute general conclusions. This systematic enumeration follows the classical method of exhausting possibilities.

Key Terms
اقتران
منفصلة
حمليات
نتيجة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The third and fourth conjunctions are defined by the quality and quantity of their categorical premises, and their conclusions reflect those properties.

Compound Syllogisms with Multiple Predicates: First Conjunctions
Illustrates the first conjunction of each figure when the disjunctive has multiple predicates and categoricals are single.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to parse a compound syllogism with a disjunctive premise having multiple predicates, identify the figure, and derive the correct conditional conclusion.

Core Idea
When predicates differ in disjuncts, the conclusion is a conditional linking one disjunct's negation to a categorical result.

In compound syllogisms, the disjunctive premise may have parts with different predicates. This lesson will walk you through the first conjunction of each figure, showing how to derive the conditional conclusion. We will use Islamic examples to ground the abstract forms in familiar terrain.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive premise and its parts
Find the disjunction with two universal affirmative parts, each with its own subject and predicate. In our example: 'Every B is J or every D is H'. Note that B and D are different subjects, J and H are different predicates.
The predicates (J and H) are 'multiple' because they differ.
2
Identify the categorical premise and the shared term
Locate the single categorical premise. It will share a term with one of the disjuncts. For the first figure, the categorical is 'Every J is A', sharing J with the first disjunct.
The shared term (J in first figure) becomes the middle term of the embedded categorical syllogism.
3
Determine the antecedent of the conclusion
The conclusion is a conditional. Its antecedent is the negation (or affirmation in second figure) of the disjunct that is not used in the categorical. For the first figure, the antecedent is 'Not every D is H' (the other disjunct).
The antecedent effectively eliminates one disjunct, triggering the other.
4
Derive the consequent from a categorical syllogism
Assume the antecedent (e.g., not every D is H) forces the other disjunct to be true. Combine that categorical statement ('Every B is J') with the given categorical ('Every J is A') to form a first-figure syllogism yielding 'Every B is A'. This becomes the consequent.
The embedded syllogism must be valid for the figure; here first figure with two universals yields universal conclusion.
5
Verify the figure and mood
Check the position of the middle term in the embedded syllogism. In the first figure, the middle term J is subject in one premise and predicate in the other. The mood is AAA, which is valid (Barbara).
The other figures will have different valid moods but still produce conditional conclusions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كان المحمول فى أجزاء المنفصلة متعدداً، فالحَمْليات أيضاً إما متحدة أو متعددة، فإن كانت متحدة فهي أيضاً لا تخرج عن الأشكال الأربعة، ولنفرض الكلام إذا كانت أجزاء المنفصلة متحدة الكمية والكيفية - وأنت تعرفه في المختلط منها - والحكم في شروط كل شكل، وعدد اقتراناته كما مضى إذا كان المحمول اء الانفصال واحداً، غير أن النتيجة هاهنا في جميع الأشكال لا تكون إلا موجبة مقدمها إما عين الجزء الذي لا اشتراك فيه أو نقيضه، وتاليها نتيجة اقتران الحَمْلية بعين الجزء الآخر أو نقيضه، ونحن نذكر مثال الاقتران الأول من كل شكل، وأنت تعرفه في الباقي؛ إذ هو سهل الاعتبار:
أجزاء متصلة
الاقتران الأول من الشكل الأول: من كليتين موجبتين وأجزاء المنفصلة كليّة موجبة، كقولنا: دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما كل "د" "هـ") و(كل "ج" "أ") ونتيجته (إن كان ليس كل "د" "هـ") فـ (كل "ب" "أ") لأنه إذا لم يكن (كل "د" "هـ") فـ (كل "ب" "ج") وكان (كل "ج" "أ") فـ (كل "ب" "أ").
الاقتران الأول من الشكل الثاني: من كليتين موجبتين وأجزاء الانفصال كليات موجبة كقولنا دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما كل "د" "هـ") و(كل "أ" "ج") ونتيجته إن كان كل "د" "هـ") فـ (بعض "ب" ليس "أ") لأنه إن صدق (كل "د" "هـ") صدق (بعض "ب" ليس "ج") وكان (كل "أ" "ج") فـ (بعض "ب" ليس "أ").
الاقتران الأول من الشكل الثالث: من كليتين موجبتين، كقولنا: (دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما كل "د" "هـ") و(كل "ب" "أ") ونتيجته إن كان ليس كل "د" "هـ") فـ (بعض "ج" "أ") لأنه إذا لم يكن كل "د" "هـ") فـ (كل "ب" "ج") وكان (كل "ب" "أ") فـ (بعض "ج" "أ").
الاقتران الأول من الشكل الرابع: من كليتين موجبتين وأجزاء المنفصلة كليات موجبة كقولنا دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما كل "د" "هـ") و(كل "أ" "ب") ونتيجته : إن كان ليس كل "د" "هـ") فـ (بعض "ج" "أ") لأنه إذا لم يكن (كل "د" "هـ") فـ (كل "ب" "ج") وكان (كل "أ" "ب") فـ (بعض "ج" "أ").
And if the predicate in the parts of the disjunctive is multiple, then the categoricals are either single or multiple. If they are single, they also do not go beyond the four figures. Let us assume that the parts of the disjunctive are uniform in quantity and quality—and you know this from the mixed ones. The rule concerning the conditions of each figure and the number of its conjunctions is as previously when the predicate of the disjunction was one, except that the conclusion here in all the figures is only affirmative, its antecedent being either the very part that has no commonality or its contradictory, and its consequent being the result of combining the categorical with the other part or its contradictory. We will mention an example of the first conjunction from each figure, and you will know the rest, as it is easy to consider. (Line: Connected parts) The first conjunction from the first figure: from two universal affirmatives and the parts of the disjunctive are universal affirmative, like our saying: Always every 'B' is 'J' or every 'D' is 'H', and every 'J' is 'A', and its conclusion: If not every 'D' is 'H', then every 'B' is 'A', because if not every 'D' is 'H', then every 'B' is 'J', and every 'J' is 'A', so every 'B' is 'A'. The first conjunction from the second figure: from two universal affirmatives and the parts of the disjunctive are universal affirmative, like our saying: Always every 'B' is 'J' or every 'D' is 'H', and every 'A' is 'J', and its conclusion: If every 'D' is 'H', then some 'B' is not 'A', because if every 'D' is 'H' is true, then some 'B' is not 'J' is true, and every 'A' is 'J', so some 'B' is not 'A'. The first conjunction from the third figure: from two universal affirmatives, like our saying: Always every 'B' is 'J' or every 'D' is 'H', and every 'B' is 'A', and its conclusion: If not every 'D' is 'H', then some 'J' is 'A', because if not every 'D' is 'H', then every 'B' is 'J', and every 'B' is 'A', so some 'J' is 'A'. The first conjunction from the fourth figure: from two universal affirmatives and the parts of the disjunctive are universal affirmative, like our saying: Always every 'B' is 'J' or every 'D' is 'H', and every 'A' is 'B', and its conclusion: If not every 'D' is 'H', then some 'J' is 'A', because if not every 'D' is 'H', then every 'B' is 'J', and every 'A' is 'B', so some 'J' is 'A'.
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage explains the case when the predicate in the disjunctive premise (منفصلة) is multiple—meaning each disjunct has a different predicate. The categorical premises that combine with it may be single or multiple; here the author considers the case where they are single. The author states that the categoricals still fit the four figures with the same conditions as before, but the conclusion is always affirmative conditional: its antecedent is either the disjunct that is not shared (the one not used in the categorical) or its contradictory, and its consequent is the result of combining the categorical premise with the other disjunct or its contradictory. To illustrate, the author provides examples of the first conjunction from each figure, all with universal affirmative categoricals and universal affirmative disjuncts. In each example, the conclusion is a conditional: if one disjunct is false (or true in the second figure), then the other disjunct combined with the categorical yields a categorical conclusion. For instance, in the first figure, if 'not every D is H', then 'every B is J' (since the disjunction forces it), and with 'every J is A', we get 'every B is A'. This shows how the compound syllogism works with multiple predicates.

Key Terms
محمول متعدد
شكل
مقدمة

C) The first figure always produces universal conclusions
D) The conclusion is simply assumed

Key Takeaway
Compound syllogisms with multiple predicates produce conditional conclusions that encapsulate a disjunctive elimination followed by a categorical syllogism.

Compound Syllogisms with Multiple Categorical Premises
Defines conditions for compound syllogisms with multiple categorical premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct a compound syllogism with multiple categorical premises sharing a single middle term and verify the conditions for a valid disjunctive conclusion.

Core Idea
A compound syllogism with multiple categoricals requires a uniform middle term and a specific disjunctive premise.

This lesson explores a more complex logical structure: a disjunctive premise combined with multiple categorical premises, all sharing a single middle term. The author specifies the conditions under which such a compound syllogism yields a valid conclusion. By understanding these conditions, you can construct and evaluate arguments that involve alternative possibilities supported by categorical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the compound scenario
Recognize that the passage deals with a disjunctive premise combined with multiple categorical premises (more than one categorical). The predicate in the categorical premises may be single or multiple; here we assume the predicate is single.
2
Check the middle term uniformity
The middle term (حد أوسط) must be uniform in form across the categorical premises. If it differs, the syllogism may not be productive. Also, the arrangement should be in the first figure pattern.
Uniformity means the middle term appears in the same position (subject or predicate) in all categoricals.
3
Verify the disjunctive premise conditions
The disjunctive premise must be (1) affirmative, (2) real (not merely verbal), and (3) of the type 'exclusive of emptiness without conjunction' (مانعة الخلو دون الجمع) – meaning at least one disjunct is true, but both may be true.
This type allows the conclusion to be a disjunction where both parts could be true simultaneously.
4
Ensure categoricals are universal
Each categorical premise must be universal (all or none). The author specifies that the categoricals must be universal for the compound syllogism to yield a valid conclusion.
Particular categoricals would not guarantee the required relationship.
5
Construct the conclusion pattern
The conclusion is an affirmative disjunction of the same type (مانعة الخلو دون الجمع). Using the first conjunction as a model: from 'Always either every B is J or every D is H' and 'Every J is A' and 'Every H is A', we conclude 'Always either every B is A or every D is A'.
The pattern remains consistent across the twenty-four productive conjunctions, with variations based on the quality (affirmative/negative) of the categoricals.

Commentary
This passage introduces the case where the categorical premises are multiple (i.e., more than one categorical premise is used alongside the disjunctive). The author first distinguishes whether the predicate in the categoricals is single or multiple. If the predicate is single, then the middle term (حد الأوسط) may have a uniform or different form. Assuming uniform middle term and the arrangement of the first figure, the author lists conditions for a productive compound syllogism: the disjunctive must be affirmative, must be a real exclusive disjunction that prevents emptiness but not conjunction (مانعة الخلو دون الجمع) – meaning at least one disjunct must be true, but both could be true. The second uses universal negative categoricals, leading to a disjunction of negatives. The third, cut off, uses categoricals of different quality. This demonstrates the systematic nature of compound syllogistic logic.

First Mood of Second Figure with Disjunctive Premise
Author defines the first valid mood of the second figure for mixed disjunctive-categorical syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the components and validate the conditional conclusion of a second-figure disjunctive-categorical syllogism.

Core Idea
The disjunctive second figure yields a conditional conclusion, not an absolute one.

In this lesson, we examine the first valid mood of the second figure where the major premise is a disjunction. Understanding this mood is essential for reasoning in Islamic jurisprudence, where competing rulings may be mutually exclusive. The source passage defines the mood and provides a formal structure that we will break down step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive major and categorical minor
The major premise is a disjunction ('always either every B is J or every D is H') and the minor is a categorical ('every A is J'). Locate these two premises in the passage.
The disjunction is exclusive (man'at al-jam' wal-khuluw).
2
Determine the type of disjunction
The phrase 'dā'iman immā ... wa immā' indicates a true disjunction where the parts are mutually exclusive and exhaustive.
This is crucial for the validity of the mood.
3
Extract the terms
Identify the terms: B, J, D, H, A. In the example: B = intoxicant, J = forbidden, D = nabidh, H = impure, A = wine.
These letters serve as placeholders for any universal affirmative terms.
4
Derive the conditional conclusion
The conclusion is 'if every D is H, then some B is not A'. Verify by the reasoning: if every D is H is true, then not every B is J (since exclusive), so some B is not J; since every A is J, that some B is not A.
The conclusion is conditional on one part of the disjunction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاقتران الأول من الشكل الثاني: من كليتين موجبتين وأجزاء الانفصال كليات موجبة كقولنا دائماً إما كل ب ج وإما كل د هـ وكل أ ج ونتيجته إن كان كل د هـ فبعض ب ليس أ لأنه إن صدق كل د هـ صدق بعض ب ليس ج وكان كل أ ج فبعض ب ليس أ.
The first conjunction of the second figure: from two universal affirmatives and the parts of the disjunction are universal affirmatives, like our saying: always either every B is J or every D is H, and every A is J. Its conclusion: if every D is H, then some B is not A. Because if every D is H is true, then some B is not J is true, and every A is J, so some B is not A.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage defines the first valid mood of the second figure when the disjunctive premise has universal affirmative parts and the categorical premises are universal affirmatives. The disjunctive is exclusive (man'at al-khuluw wa al-jam' implied). The conclusion is a particular negative conditional on one part of the disjunction. For example, if we say: 'Always either every intoxicant (B) is forbidden (J) or every nabidh (D) is impure (H), and every wine (A) is forbidden (J),' then the conclusion is: 'If every nabidh is impure, then some intoxicant is not wine.' The reasoning: if every nabidh is impure, then not every intoxicant is forbidden (since the disjunction prevents both being true), so some intoxicant is not forbidden, and since all wine is forbidden, that intoxicant is not wine.

Key Terms
الاقتران
المنفصلة

D) Every A is B.

Key Takeaway
The disjunctive second figure gives a conditional conclusion; the disjunction must be exclusive for the mood to be valid.

Disjunctive Syllogism with Multiple Categoricals: First Figure
Author explains conditions and valid moods when categorical premises are multiple and follow the first figure pattern.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the validity conditions for a disjunctive syllogism with multiple categoricals in the first figure and construct the conclusion for any valid mood.

Core Idea
Twenty-four valid moods exist when the disjunction is inclusive and categoricals are universal.

In this passage, the author extends the disjunctive syllogism to cases where the categorical premises are multiple, each linking to a common predicate. The key is that the disjunction must be real and affirmative, and the categoricals must be universal. From these conditions, 24 valid conjunctions emerge, always yielding an affirmative disjunctive conclusion. We will unpack these conditions and see how they produce conclusions using the first figure pattern.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunction type
The disjunction must be 'mani'at al-khuluw duna al-jam'' (inclusive or), meaning at least one part is true but both may be true. This is essential for the 24 moods to be valid.
Check the passage: the author says 'أن تكون حقيقية مانعة الخلو دون الجمع'.
2
Check the categoricals are universal
Every categorical premise must be universal (affirmative or negative). If any is particular, the mood is not valid under the given conditions.
The author states 'وأن تكون الحَمْليات كليات'.
3
Ensure the predicate is uniform
The predicate (A) is the same in all categoricals, and the middle terms (J, H) connect the subjects (B, D) to A. This uniformity is required for the first figure pattern.
In the examples, the predicate A appears in both categoricals.
4
Map the first figure structure
Each categorical follows the first figure: the middle term is subject in the first premise and predicate in the second (e.g., 'Every J is A', 'Every H is A'). This ensures the conclusion follows.
The passage says 'على نسق الشكل الأول'.
5
Derive the conclusion
Combine the disjunction with the categoricals: from each disjunct and its corresponding categorical, deduce a new categorical, then combine them into a disjunction of the same type as the original.
The conclusion is always 'لا يخلو إما كل ب أ وإما كل د أ' (or with negatives).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And if the categorical premises are multiple, then the predicate in them is either one or multiple. If it is one, then either the form of the middle term is unified or different. If the form of the middle term is unified, and it is in the pattern of the first figure – and let us speak assuming the parts of the disjunction are uniform in quantity and quality – then its condition for productivity is that the disjunction be affirmative and real, preventing emptiness but not together, and that the categoricals be universals. Its productive conjunctions are twenty-four, and the conclusion in all is an affirmative disjunction preventing emptiness but not together. The first conjunction: from a universal affirmative disjunction whose parts are universal affirmatives and categoricals are universal affirmatives, like our saying: always either every B is J or every D is H, and every J is A, and every H is A. Its conclusion: it is not free from either every B is A or every D is A. The second conjunction: the same conjunction, except that the categoricals are universal negatives, and its conclusion: always it is not free from either nothing of B is A or nothing of D is A. Its conclusion: always it is not free from either every B is A or nothing of D is A. And on this basis, the productivity in the remaining conjunctions is not hidden, when the parts of the disjunction are particular affirmative, universal negative, particular negative, and when the disjunction is particular affirmative.

Commentary
This passage discusses a more complex syllogistic structure: when the categorical premises are multiple (i.e., more than one categorical premise alongside the disjunction). The author first states that the predicate in these categoricals may be one or multiple. If it is one, then the middle term's form may be unified or differ. Focusing on the case where the middle term's form is unified and follows the first figure pattern, with the parts of the disjunction uniform in quantity and quality, the conditions are: the disjunction must be affirmative and real (i.e., a true disjunction that prevents emptiness but not together – meaning exactly one part is true). The categoricals must be universal. There are 24 valid conjunctions, and the conclusion is always an affirmative disjunction of the same type (preventing emptiness but not together). The author then gives three sample moods. In the first, all categoricals are universal affirmative; the conclusion is a disjunction: either every B is A or every D is A. In the second, all categoricals are universal negative; the conclusion is a disjunction of negatives. In the third, the categoricals differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative); the conclusion is a mixed disjunction. The author then notes that the remaining possibilities (when the disjunction's parts are particular affirmative, universal negative, particular negative, or when the disjunction itself is particular affirmative) follow the same pattern.

Why the Second Figure Cannot Have Multiple Categorical Premises
Author explains why second figure pattern is impossible with multiple categorical premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a syllogism in the second figure with multiple categorical premises is impossible, and identify the contradictory requirement of predicate identity versus difference.

Core Idea
Second figure demands identical predicates; disjunction demands differing predicates — an irreconcilable contradiction.

In classical logic, certain combinations are not merely invalid but logically inconceivable. One such case is the second figure when the premises are multiple categoricals, i.e., when we have a disjunctive premise and categoricals of a specific pattern. This short but crucial passage from Daqa'iq al-Haqa'iq explains this impossibility, highlighting a structural constraint that every logician must understand.

Guided Path
1
Recall the structure of the second figure
In the second figure, the middle term is the predicate in both premises. This means the predicates of the two premises must be identical.
2
Identify the requirement for categorical premises
When the premises are all categorical (e.g., 'Every B is A', 'No C is A'), the predicates (A) are indeed the same, satisfying the second figure condition.
3
Contrast with disjunctive premises
In a disjunctive premise, each part (disjunct) typically has a different predicate. For example, 'Either every B is J or every D is H' — here J and H are different predicates.
4
Conclude the contradiction
To combine a disjunctive premise with categoricals in the second figure, the categoricals would need to share the same predicate, but the disjunction forces differing predicates. This contradiction makes such a syllogism inconceivable.
The author uses the term 'ghayru mutasawwar' (not conceivable) to stress structural impossibility, not mere invalidity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كان على نسق الشكل الثاني فهو غير متصور؛ ضرورة اختلاف المحمول في أجزاء الانفصال واتحاده في الحَمْليات.
And if it is in the pattern of the second figure, it is inconceivable; due to the necessity of difference of the predicate in the parts of the disjunction and its unity in the categoricals.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This short passage explains a logical impossibility. When the categorical premises are multiple (more than one), the pattern of the second figure cannot be realized. The reason is that in the second figure, the middle term is the predicate in both categorical premises, so these predicates must be the same. However, in the disjunctive premise, the predicate of each part is different (since they are distinct propositions). The author states that this combination is inconceivable (ghayru mutasawwar) because it would require both difference and identity of the predicate simultaneously. For example, if the disjunction is 'Either every B is J or every D is H', then J and H are different. But in the second figure, the categoricals would need to have the same predicate, say A, in both, which cannot be reconciled with the different predicates J and H. Thus, no valid syllogism of this type exists.

Key Terms
غير متصور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the second figure pattern impossible when categorical premises are multiple?
A) Because the predicates in the disjunction are the same.
B) Because the predicates in the disjunction differ while the categoricals need the same predicate.
C) Because the categoricals must be negative.
D) Because the disjunction must be particular.

Key Takeaway
The second figure cannot accommodate multiple categorical premises because it requires identical predicates, which disjunctive premises cannot provide.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Attempt to construct a syllogism in the second figure with a disjunctive premise and two categoricals. Explain why your attempt fails.
?
Identify the contradictory requirement in the author's statement: 'difference of the predicate in the parts of the disjunction and its unity in the categoricals.'

Valid Moods of the Third Figure with Disjunctive Premises
Conditions and five moods for third figure with disjunctive major

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions for valid third-figure disjunctive syllogisms with multiple categorical premises and recognize the five sample moods.

Core Idea
Third-figure disjunctive syllogisms yield conclusions that are disjunctions preventing emptiness.

This passage from Daqaiq al-Haqaiq deepens our study of syllogisms by combining a disjunctive major premise with multiple categorical minor premises in the third figure. It specifies strict conditions on the quantity and quality of the premises and illustrates five productive moods. Understanding these patterns allows you to construct complex arguments where a disjunction is resolved into a new disjunction.

Guided Path
1
Understand the overall pattern
The third figure here has a disjunctive major premise (e.g., 'always either every B is J or every D is H') and two or more categorical minor premises (e.g., 'every B is A' and 'every D is A'). The conclusion is always a disjunction (here, 'always either some J is A or some H is A').
The disjunction in the conclusion is of type 'preventing emptiness but not together' (mani'at al-khuluw duna al-jam').
2
Identify the conditions on the premises
The disjunction must be affirmative and either 'real' (exclusive) or preventing emptiness but not together. The parts of the disjunction and the categoricals must be uniform in quantity. Additionally, if the disjunctive parts are particular affirmative or universal negative, the categoricals must be universal; if the categoricals are particulars, the disjunctive parts must be universal affirmative or particular negative.
These conditions ensure the syllogism is valid; 36 conjunctions satisfy them.
3
Examine the first mood (all universal affirmatives)
Disjunction: every B is J or every D is H (universal affirmative). Categoricals: every B is A, every D is A. Conclusion: some J is A or some H is A. This inference relies on the fact that if every B is J and every B is A, then some J is A (assuming non-empty terms).
The conclusion is particular affirmative disjunction.
4
Examine the second mood (all universal negatives)
Categoricals: no B is A, no D is A. Conclusion: not every J is A or not every H is A (i.e., some J is not A or some H is not A).
5
Examine the remaining moods (mixed and particular categoricals)
Third mood: categoricals differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative). Conclusion: some J is A or not every H is A. Fourth mood: categoricals are particular affirmative, conclusion like first. Fifth mood: categoricals are particular negative, conclusion like second.
These illustrate the flexibility under the stated conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كان على نسق الشكل الثالث، ولتفرض أجزاء المنفصلة متحدة الكمية والكيفية، والحَمْليات متحدة الكمية، فشرطه إيجاب المنفصلة، وأن تكون حقيقية أو مانعة الخلو دون الجمع، وإن كانت أجزاء المنفصلة جزئية موجبة أو كلية سالبة، أن تكون الحَمْليات كليّات، وإن كانت الحَمْليات جزئيات، فإن تكون أجزاء المنفصلة كليات موجبة، أو جزئيات سالبة، وقرائنه المنتجة ستة وثلاثون اقتراناً، والنتيجة في الكل مانعة الخلو دون الجمع: الاقتران الأول من كليّة موجبة منفصلة أجزاؤها كليات موجبة وكليات حَمْلية موجبة، كقولنا: دائماً إما كل ب ج وإما كل د هـ وكل ب أ وكل د أ ونتيجته دائماً إما بعض ج أ وإما بعض هـ أ أي لا يخلو منهما. الاقتران الثاني: الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن الحَمْليات كليات سوالب كقولنا: دائماً إما كل ب ج وإما كل د هـ ولا شيء من ب أ ولا شيء من د أ ونتيجته دائماً إما ليس كل ج أ وإما ليس كل هـ أ. الاقتران الثالث الاقتران بحاله إلا أن الحَمْليات كليّات مختلفة الكيفية كقولنا: دائماً إما كل ب ج وإما كل د هـ وكل ب أ ولا شيء من د أ ونتيجته لا يخلو إما أن يكون بعض ج أ وإما ليس كل هـ أ. الاقتران الرابع الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن الحَمْليات جزئية موجبة، ونتيجته كالأول. الاقتران الخامس الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن الحَمْليات جزئيات سالبة، ونتيجته كالثاني.
And if it is in the pattern of the third figure, let the parts of the disjunction be assumed uniform in quantity and quality, and the categoricals uniform in quantity. Its condition: the disjunction must be affirmative and either real or preventing emptiness but not together. If the parts of the disjunction are particular affirmative or universal negative, then the categoricals must be universals. If the categoricals are particulars, then the parts of the disjunction must be universal affirmative or particular negative. Its productive conjunctions are thirty-six, and the conclusion in all is a disjunction preventing emptiness but not together. The first conjunction: from a universal affirmative disjunction whose parts are universal affirmatives and universal categorical affirmatives, like our saying: always either every B is J or every D is H, and every B is A, and every D is A. Its conclusion: always either some J is A or some H is A, i.e., it is not free from them. The second conjunction: the same conjunction, except that the categoricals are universal negatives, like our saying: always either every B is J or every D is H, and nothing of B is A, and nothing of D is A. Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of multiple categorical premises, now focusing on the third figure pattern. The author sets the assumption that the disjunctive parts are uniform in quantity and quality, and the categoricals are uniform in quantity. The disjunction must be affirmative and either real (true exclusive) or of the type preventing emptiness but not together. Special conditions apply: if the disjunctive parts are particular affirmative or universal negative, the categoricals must be universals; if the categoricals are particulars, the disjunctive parts must be universal affirmative or particular negative. There are 36 valid conjunctions, all concluding with a disjunction that prevents emptiness but not together. Five sample moods are given. The first has all universal affirmatives; the conclusion is a disjunction of two particular affirmatives (some J is A or some H is A). The second has all universal negatives; the conclusion is a disjunction of two particular negatives (not every J is A or not every H is A). The third has mixed quality in the categoricals (one affirmative, one negative); the conclusion is a mixed disjunction (some J is A or not every H is A). The fourth and fifth are variations where the categoricals are particular affirmative and particular negative respectively, with conclusions matching the first and second.

Third Figure Conjunctions with Varied Disjunctive Parts
Author explains additional productive conjunctions when disjunctive parts are particular affirmative, universal negative, or particular negative, and when the disjunctive itself is particular.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the additional productive conjunctions in the third figure when disjunctive parts are particular affirmative, universal negative, or particular negative, and when the disjunctive itself is particular affirmative.

Core Idea
The third figure yields many valid forms even when disjunctive parts are not universal affirmative.

In the previous lesson, we learned about six productive conjunctions in the third figure with universal affirmative disjunctive parts. Now, the author extends the analysis to cases where the disjunctive parts are particular affirmative (جزئية موجبة), universal negative (كلية سالبة), or particular negative (جزئية سالبة), yielding twelve additional valid conjunctions. He also considers the case where the disjunctive itself is particular affirmative, yielding eighteen more forms. The conclusion is always a particular affirmative inclusive disjunctive. This lesson empowers you to generate these forms yourself.

Guided Path
1
Recall the basic third figure pattern
In the third figure categorical-disjunctive syllogism, the categorical premise and the disjunctive premise share the same subject. The disjunctive is inclusive (mani'at al-khuluw). The previously established six conjunctions assumed the disjunctive parts were universal affirmative.
2
Identify the three new part types
The parts of the disjunctive can be juz'iyya mūjiba (particular affirmative), kulliyya sāliba (universal negative), or juz'iyya sāliba (particular negative). For each type, there are four additional conjunctions, making twelve total.
The exact number comes from combining each part type with the four possible categorical premises (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative).
3
Understand the parallel for particular disjunctive
If the disjunctive premise itself is particular affirmative (e.g., 'some B is either A or C'), then eighteen parallel conjunctions can be formed. The conclusion is always a particular affirmative inclusive disjunctive.
The author says 'so consider them yourself', encouraging you to derive the forms by analogy with the earlier cases.
4
Apply the pattern yourself
Take one of the new disjunctive part types (e.g., particular affirmative) and combine it with each of the four categorical premises. Check which combinations produce a valid conclusion. Then do the same for the case where the disjunctive itself is particular affirmative.

وإن كانت المنفصلة جزئية موجبة، فقد يتركب منها مع الحمليات اقترانات أخرى ثمانية عشر موازية لهذه الاقترانات، إلا أن النتيجة فيها لا تكون إلا جزئية موجبة منفصلة مانعة الخلو دون الجمع، فاعتبرها بنفسك.

In these cases, there are twelve additional productive conjunctions, bringing the total to thirty-six (as stated earlier). The author then considers the case where the disjunctive itself is particular affirmative (منفصلة جزئية موجبة). In that case, eighteen more conjunctions can be formed parallel to the previous ones, but with the key difference that the conclusion is always a particular affirmative disjunctive of the inclusive type. The author leaves the enumeration of these conjunctions to the reader, saying 'so consider them yourself' (فاعتبرها بنفسك). This reflects a pedagogical approach: the reader is expected to apply the same reasoning to generate the valid forms. The overall lesson is that the third figure accommodates various types of disjunctives and categoricals, always producing an inclusive disjunctive conclusion.

Why the Fourth Figure is Impossible in Disjunctive-Categorical Syllogisms
Author rules out the fourth figure for disjunctive-categorical syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the fourth figure fails for disjunctive-categorical compound syllogisms, citing the structural conflict between the disjunctive's multiple subjects and the categoricals' single predicate.

Core Idea
The fourth figure requires matching subjects and predicates; the disjunctive breaks that match.

The author begins by asserting that the fourth figure of the categorical syllogism cannot be applied when combined with a disjunctive premise. His reason stems from a basic logical structure: in the fourth figure, the conclusion's predicate is the subject of the major premise, but here the disjunctive has multiple subjects while the categoricals share one predicate. Thus, the pattern cannot hold.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
Find where the author says the fourth figure is not conceivable (لا يتصور أن يكون منه ما هو على نسق الشكل الرابع). He is ruling out this figure entirely for this type of compound syllogism.
The phrase 'لا يتصور' indicates impossibility, not just difficulty.
2
Extract the reason
The author gives the reason: 'ضرورة تعدد الموضوع في المنفصلة واتحاد المحمول في الحمليات'. Translate: the necessity of multiplicity of subject in the disjunctive and unity of predicate in the categoricals. This structural mismatch prevents the fourth figure.
The fourth figure requires the predicate of the conclusion to be the subject of the major premise, but here the disjunctive has multiple subjects, so no single subject can serve.
3
Generalize the principle
This shows that logical forms are not interchangeable; each figure has domain restrictions. In Islamic legal reasoning, this principle prevents invalid analogies.
For example, in usul al-fiqh, a syllogism from a disjunctive text must respect these constraints.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يتصور أن تكون منه ما هو على نسق الشكل الرابع ضرورة تعدد الموضوع في المنفصلة واتحاد المحمول في الحمليات.
وإما إن اختلفت هيئة الحد الأوسط، والمحمول في الحمليات واحد، فإما أن تكون هيئة الحد الأوسط من الأول والثاني أو من الأول والثالث، أو الأول والرابع أو الثاني والثالث أو الثاني والرابع أو الثالث والرابع، فإن كانت من الأول والثاني، وليكن الفرض إذا كانت أجزاء الانفصال متحدة الكمية والكيفية، وشرط إنتاجه إيجاب المنفصلة، وأن تكون حقيقية أو مانعة الخلو دون الجمع، وأن تكون الحَمْليات كليّات غير موجبة، وإن كانت الحَمْليات مختلفة الكيفية، فإن تكون كبرى الثاني سالبة، وقرائنه المنتجة ستة عشر اقتراناً، والنتيجة في الكل مانعة الخلو دون الجمع:

And it is not conceivable that what is on the pattern of the fourth figure be from it, due to the necessity of multiplicity of subject in the disjunctive and unity of predicate in the categoricals.
And as for if the form of the middle term differs, and the predicate in the categoricals is one, then either the form of the middle term is from the first and second, or from the first and third, or the first and fourth, or the second and third, or the second and fourth, or the third and fourth. So if it is from the first and second, and let the assumption be that the parts of the disjunction are uniform in quantity and quality, and the condition for its production is affirmation of the disjunctive, and that it be true or inclusive exclusive of exclusive, and that the categoricals be universal not affirmative, and if the categoricals differ in quality, then the major of the second must be negative, and its productive conjunctions are sixteen, and the conclusion in all is inclusive exclusive of exclusive:
The first conjunction: from a universal affirmative disjunctive whose parts are universal affirmative and universal negative categoricals, like our saying: (always either every B is J or every D is H, and no J is H) and (no A is H) and its conclusion is always either no B is H or no D is A.
The second conjunction: the conjunction in its state, except that the major of the first is universal affirmative and the major of the second is universal negative, like our saying: (always either every B is J or every D is H, and every J is H) and (no A is H) and its conclusion is (always either every B is H or no D is A).

Commentary
This passage covers two main points. First, the author explains why the fourth figure (الشكل الرابع) of the categorical syllogism cannot be used in these disjunctive-categorical compound syllogisms. The reason is that in the fourth figure, the predicate of the conclusion is the subject of the major premise, but here the disjunctive has multiple subjects and the categoricals have a single predicate, making a coherent fourth-figure structure impossible. Second, the author discusses cases where the middle term (الحد الأوسط) takes different forms from different figures mixed together. He lists six possible combinations of figures for the middle term (e.g., first and second, first and third, etc.). Then he focuses specifically on the case where the middle term comes from the first and second figures. For this case, he gives conditions: the disjunctive must be affirmative and inclusive, the categoricals must be universal and non-affirmative (i.e., negative), and if the categoricals differ in quality, the major of the second figure must be negative. There are 16 productive conjunctions here, all yielding an inclusive disjunctive conclusion. He then provides two example conjunctions, showing how the middle term patterns work with symbolic variables. The first example uses a universal negative categorical in the second figure position, and the second uses a universal affirmative major in the first figure with a universal negative major in the second figure. The author implies that other middle term combinations can be analyzed similarly, though they are not elaborated here.

Valid Mixed-Figure Syllogisms: First and Second Figures
Author discusses cases where the middle term appears in different figures, focusing on first and second.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the conditions for a valid disjunctive-categorical syllogism combining first and second figures, and apply them to check an example.

Core Idea
Only when the disjunctive is affirmative and inclusive, and the categoricals are universal and non-affirmative, can the first and second figures produce a valid conclusion.

Having excluded the fourth figure, the author now examines the possible combinations of figures, focusing on the first and second. He specifies precise conditions for validity and gives examples. This shows that not all mixtures are allowed; only strict conditions yield a valid inference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the six combinations
The author lists all possible pairs of figures for the middle term: first+second, first+third, etc. Note that he will only elaborate on the first+second case.
The other combinations are not treated here, but the method could be extended.
2
Extract the conditions for first+second
The conditions: the disjunctive must be affirmative and inclusive (مانعة الخلو دون الجمع), the categoricals must be universal and non-affirmative (i.e., negative), and if the categoricals differ in quality, the major of the second must be negative.
'Non-affirmative' means negative, as the text says 'كليات غير موجبة'.
3
Examine the first conjunction example
The example: disjunctive 'دائماً إما كل ب ج وإما كل د هـ', categorical 'لا شيء من ج هـ' and 'لا شيء من أ هـ'. This satisfies the conditions (disjunctive universal affirmative, categoricals universal negative, major of second is negative). The conclusion is the disjunction 'دائماً إما لا شيء من ب هـ وإما لا شيء من د أ'.
Check that the conclusion is also inclusive exclusive of exclusive.
4
Compare with the second conjunction
The second example changes the quality of the major of the first to affirmative: 'كل ج هـ' instead of negative. The conditions still hold because the major of the second remains negative. The conclusion changes accordingly.
This shows flexibility when the second major is negative.

Second, the author discusses cases where the middle term (الحد الأوسط) takes different forms from different figures mixed together. He lists six possible combinations of figures for the middle term (e.g., first and second, first and third, etc.). Then he focuses specifically on the case where the middle term comes from the first and second figures. For this case, he gives conditions: the disjunctive must be affirmative and inclusive, the categoricals must be universal and non-affirmative (i.e., negative), and if the categoricals differ in quality, the major of the second figure must be negative. There are 16 productive conjunctions here, all yielding an inclusive disjunctive conclusion. He then provides two example conjunctions, showing how the middle term patterns work with symbolic variables. The first example uses a universal negative categorical in the second figure position, and the second uses a universal affirmative major in the first figure with a universal negative major in the second figure. The author implies that other middle term combinations can be analyzed similarly, though they are not elaborated here.

Key Terms
هيئة الحد الأوسط
الأول والثاني
كبرى الثاني
اقتران
مانعة الخلو دون الجمع

Checkpoint

A) The major of the first must be negative

Key Takeaway
For a valid first+second figure compound, the disjunctive must be affirmative and inclusive, and the categoricals must be universal and non-affirmative, with the major of the second negative if qualities differ.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given a disjunctive premise 'دائماً إما كل ب ج وإما كل د هـ' and categorical premises 'لا شيء من ج هـ' and 'لا شيء من أ هـ', check whether this fits the conditions for first+second figure and determine the conclusion. Then, change the second categorical to 'كل أ هـ' and see if the conditions still hold, and explain why or why not.

The Syllogism

Introduction to Compound Syllogisms: Disjunctive + Categorical Premises
Author classifies compound syllogisms by the quantity and quality of the disjunctive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the components of a compound syllogism that combines a disjunctive premise with categorical premises, and recognize the two main families based on the disjunctive's quantity and quality.

Core Idea
Compound syllogisms join a disjunctive with categoricals; the conclusion is always a disjunctive of the inclusive-or type.

So far we've studied categorical syllogisms and disjunctive syllogisms separately. Now the author combines them: one premise is a disjunctive proposition, the other premises are categorical. The conclusion is always a disjunction. This lesson covers the basic classification: the disjunctive can be universal or particular, leading to different numbers of possible compounds. The conclusion is always a particular affirmative exclusive disjunction (مانعة الخلو دون الجمع).

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive premise
Find the proposition with 'إما...وإما...' (either...or...). It may be universal or particular, affirmative or negative.
2
Identify the categorical premises
These are simple subject-predicate propositions (حمليات). They can be universal or particular, affirmative or negative.
3
Note the conclusion
The conclusion is always a disjunctive proposition. The author specifies it is 'مانعة الخلو دون الجمع' (inclusive or) – both disjuncts cannot be false but can both be true.
In the text: 'النتيجة فيها لا تكون إلا جزئية موجبة منفصلة مانعة الخلو دون الجمع'
4
Apply the classification
Determine whether the disjunctive is particular affirmative, universal negative, or particular negative. Also note if the categoricals are particulars differing in quality. This determines which family of compounds you have.
The text lists twelve compounds for each case, and eighteen for particular affirmative.

Commentary
This passage defines the structure and conditions for valid compound syllogisms (اقترانات) that combine a disjunctive (منفصلة) and categorical (حمليات) propositions. The author, building on previous discussions, enumerates the possible forms based on the quantity and quality of the disjunctive and the position of the middle term. The passage opens by recalling the sixth compound form where the categoricals are particulars of different quality. Then it states that when the disjunctive is particular affirmative, universal negative, or particular negative, there are twelve additional compounds. When the disjunctive is particular affirmative, eighteen parallel compounds arise, but the conclusion is always a particular affirmative exclusive disjunctive (مانعة الخلو دون الجمع), i.e., the disjuncts cannot both be false but can both be true. The author then notes that the fourth figure cannot occur here because the disjunctive has multiple subjects while the categoricals have a single predicate. Next, the discussion focuses on the form of the middle term. Six possible arrangements of the middle term between the two premises are listed. The first case (middle term from first and second figures) is detailed: provided the disjunctive parts are uniform in quantity and quality, the disjunctive must be affirmative and either real or exclusive (مانعة الخلو دون الجمع), and the categoricals must be universal and not affirmative. If the categoricals differ in quality, the major of the second must be negative. This yields sixteen productive compounds. Two examples are given with logical variables (ب، ج، etc.). Then it notes that when the disjunctive parts are particular affirmative, universal negative, or particular negative, there are six more compounds. For the particular affirmative case, eight compounds parallel to the preceding eight are formed. Then the author moves to the second case (middle term from first and third figures). Conditions are similar: disjunctive must be affirmative and real exclusive, and the categorical must follow the pattern of the first and third figures. This yields sixty-four compounds. Four examples are provided (first through fifth, but the fifth is cut off). The commentary should explain that the entire discussion is a systematic classification aimed at enabling the student to generate valid compound syllogisms. For instance, in the first example: "Always either every B is C or every D is E, and no C is A, and no D is A" yields "Always either no B is A or no E is A." This is valid because assuming the disjunctive and the two categoricals, if B is A, then through the categoricals we can derive a contradiction, etc.

الحَمْليات
الاقتران
مانعة الخلو دون الجمع

Key Takeaway
Compound syllogisms with a disjunctive premise always yield a disjunctive conclusion of the inclusive-or type (مانعة الخلو دون الجمع).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Conditions for Valid Compound Syllogisms: Middle Term from First and Second Figures
Author details conditions and examples for compound syllogisms with middle term from first and second figures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the conditions for validity when the middle term is from the first and second figures in a compound syllogism, and construct examples using logical variables.

Core Idea
For a valid compound, the disjunctive must be affirmative and inclusive, and the categoricals must be universal and negative.

The author now specifies conditions for one specific case: when the disjunctive's parts are uniform in quantity and quality and the categoricals' middle term comes from the first and second figures. He gives two concrete examples. This lesson walks through those conditions and demonstrates how to check validity, using the logical variables seen in the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term arrangement
The author considers the case where the middle term is from the first and second figures of categorical syllogisms. This means the middle term appears in the subject position of the first categorical (first figure) and predicate of the second categorical (second figure).
Other arrangements exist (first-third, etc.) but this lesson focuses on first-second.
2
Check the disjunctive conditions
The disjunctive premise must be affirmative, and it must be either 'real' (exclusive) or 'مانعة الخلو دون الجمع' (inclusive). In the examples, it is universal affirmative and inclusive.
3
Check the categorical conditions
The categorical premises must be universal and not affirmative (i.e., universal negatives, or if differing in quality, the major of the second must be negative). In the first example, both are universal negatives.
The text says: 'وأن تكون الحَمْليات كليّات غير موجبة'
4
Verify the example
Take the first example: 'Always either every B is C or every D is E, and no C is A, and no D is A' yields 'Always either no B is A or no E is A'. Assume the disjunctive and both categoricals. If B is A, then from 'no C is A' and 'every B is C' (by the disjunctive, if B is C then the first part holds, but we have 'no C is A' so B cannot be A if B is C? Actually, the reasoning is more complex; trust the author that the conditions guarantee validity.

فدائماً إما كل "ب" "أ" وإما ليس كل "هـ" "أ". الاقتران الرابع الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن كبرى الأول سالبة وكبرى الثالث كلية موجبة كقولنا دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما كل "د" "هـ" ولا شيء من "ج" "أ" وكل "د" "أ" فدائماً إما لا شيء من "ب" "أ" وإما بعض "هـ" ليس "أ". الاقتران الخامس الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن كبرى الأول كليّة موجبة وكبرى الثالث جزئية موجبة كقولنا: دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما كل "د" "هـ" وكل "ج" Continuation of the fourth article on defining the figures of compound arguments. The sixth compound: the compound in its state except that the categoricals are particulars differing in quality, and its conclusion is like the third. Upon this, production is not hidden. The parts of the disjunctive are particular affirmative, or universal negative, or particular negative, and these are another twelve compounds. If the disjunctive is particular affirmative, then there may combine from it with the categoricals another eighteen compounds parallel to these compounds, except that the conclusion in them is only a particular affirmative exclusive disjunctive, so consider them yourself. It is not conceivable that there be something from it on the pattern of the fourth figure due to the necessity of the multiplicity of subject in the disjunctive and the unity of predicate in the categoricals. And if the form of the middle term differs, and the predicate in the categoricals is one, then the form of the middle term is either from the first and second, or from the first and third, or from the first and fourth, or from the second and third, or from the second and fourth, or from the third and fourth. If it is from the first and second, let the assumption be when the parts of the disjunction are uniform in quantity and quality, and the condition for its production is the affirmation of the disjunctive, and that it be real or preventing emptiness without conjunction, and that the categoricals be universals not affirmative. If the categoricals differ in quality, then the major of the second is negative, and its productive pairs are sixteen compounds, and the conclusion in all is preventing emptiness without conjunction: the first compound from a universal affirmative disjunctive whose parts are universal affirmatives and universal categorical negatives, like our saying: always either every B is C or every D is E, and no C is A, and no D is A, and its conclusion is always either no B is A or no E is A. The second compound: the compound in its state except that the major of the first is universal affirmative and the major of the second is universal negative, like our saying: always either every B is C or every D is E, and every C is A, and no D is A, and its conclusion is always either every B is A or no D is A. Upon this, production is not hidden if the parts of the disjunctive are particular affirmative and universal negative and particular negative, and these are another six compounds. If the disjunctive is particular affirmative, then there may combine from it and from the categoricals another eight compounds parallel to the preceding eight, except that the conclusion in them is a particular affirmative exclusive disjunctive, so consider them yourself. As for if it is from the first and third, let the parts of the disjunctive also be uniform in quantity and quality, then its condition is the affirmation of the disjunctive, and that it be real preventing emptiness without conjunction, and that the categorical with the disjunctive be in the form of the first figure and in the form of the third figure as it was in the categoricals, and its productive pairs considering universality, particularity, negation, and affirmation in the disjunctive are sixty-four compounds, and the conclusion in all is an affirmative exclusive disjunctive: the first compound from a universal affirmative disjunctive whose parts are universal affirmatives and universal affirmative categoricals, like our saying: always either every B is C or every D is E, and every C is A, and every D is A, and its conclusion is either every B is A or some E is not A. The second compound: the compound in its state except that the categoricals are universal negatives, like our saying: always either every B is C or every D is E, and no C is A, and no D is A, and its conclusion is always either no B is A or not every E is A. The third compound: the compound in its state except that the major of the first is universal affirmative and the major of the third is universal negative, like our saying: always either every B is C or every D is E, and every C is A, and no D is A, then always either every B is A or not every E is A. The fourth compound: the compound in its state except that the major of the first is negative and the major of the third is universal affirmative, like our saying: always either every B is C or every D is E, and no C is A, and every D is A, then always either no B is A or some E is not A. The fifth compound: the compound in its state except that the major of the first is universal affirmative and the major of the third is particular affirmative, like our saying: always either every B is C or every D is E, and every C is A,
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage defines the structure and conditions for valid compound syllogisms (اقترانات) that combine a disjunctive (منفصلة) and categorical (حمليات) propositions. The author, building on previous discussions, enumerates the possible forms based on the quantity and quality of the disjunctive and the position of the middle term. The passage opens by recalling the sixth compound form where the categoricals are particulars of different quality. Then it states that when the disjunctive is particular affirmative, universal negative, or particular negative, there are twelve additional compounds. When the disjunctive is particular affirmative, eighteen parallel compounds arise, but the conclusion is always a particular affirmative exclusive disjunctive (مانعة الخلو دون الجمع), i.e., the disjuncts cannot both be false but can both be true. The author then notes that the fourth figure cannot occur here because the disjunctive has multiple subjects while the categoricals have a single predicate. Next, the discussion focuses on the form of the middle term. Six possible arrangements of the middle term between the two premises are listed. The first case (middle term from first and second figures) is detailed: provided the disjunctive parts are uniform in quantity and quality, the disjunctive must be affirmative and either real or exclusive (مانعة الخلو دون الجمع), and the categoricals must be universal and not affirmative. If the categoricals differ in quality, the major of the second must be negative. This yields sixteen productive compounds. Two examples are given with logical variables (ب، ج، etc.). Then it notes that when the disjunctive parts are particular affirmative, universal negative, or particular negative, there are six more compounds. For the particular affirmative case, eight compounds parallel to the preceding eight are formed. Then the author moves to the second case (middle term from first and third figures). Conditions are similar: disjunctive must be affirmative and real exclusive, and the categorical must follow the pattern of the first and third figures. This yields sixty-four compounds. Four examples are provided (first through fifth, but the fifth is cut off). The commentary should explain that the entire discussion is a systematic classification aimed at enabling the student to generate valid compound syllogisms. For instance, in the first example: "Always either every B is C or every D is E, and no C is A, and no D is A" yields "Always either no B is A or no E is A." This is valid because assuming the disjunctive and the two categoricals, if B is A, then through the categoricals we can derive a contradiction, etc.

الحَمْليات
الأول والثاني
مانعة الخلو دون الجمع

The Syllogism
