Eight Valid Conjunctions with Disjunctive Premises
Author enumerates eight valid conjunctions from first and third figures with disjunctive premises

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the eight valid conjunctions of first and third figures with an affirmative exclusive disjunctive premise and verify their validity.

Core Idea
Compound syllogisms with disjunctives always produce a disjunctive conclusion of the same exclusive type.

This passage systematically enumerates valid compound syllogisms formed by combining a disjunctive premise with categorical premises in the first and third figures. Understanding these conjunctions is crucial for complex reasoning in Islamic jurisprudence and theology where multiple premises are combined. By mastering these eight patterns, you can construct and evaluate compound arguments used in proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive condition
The disjunctive must be affirmative and exclusive (māni‘at al-khulūw dūn al-jam‘), meaning at least one disjunct must be true, possibly both. The parts of the disjunctive must be uniform in quantity and quality.
This condition ensures the disjunctive itself is valid and productive.
2
Recognize the categorical arrangement
The categorical premises must be arranged in the first figure (middle term subject of one premise and predicate of the other) and the third figure (middle term subject of both premises). Here, there are two categoricals: one in first figure (major) and one in third figure (major).
The overall syllogism combines two syllogistic figures.
3
Trace the eight variations of the categorical majors
Each conjunction varies the quality and quantity of the major premises of the first and third figures. List the eight combinations: (1) both universal affirmative, (2) both universal negative, (3) first affirmative, third negative, (4) first negative, third affirmative, (5) first affirmative, third particular affirmative, (6) first affirmative, third particular negative, (7) first negative, third particular affirmative, (8) first negative, third particular negative.
All other premises remain constant: the disjunctive and the minor categoricals (every C is A, every D is A, etc.) stay the same.
4
Observe the conclusion pattern
The conclusion is always an affirmative exclusive disjunction (māni‘at al-khulūw dūn al-jam‘) with two disjuncts: one about B (subject of first categorical) and one about H (subject of third categorical). The quality of each disjunct depends on the categorical majors: if the major is affirmative, the disjunct is positive; if negative, the disjunct is negative. For particular negatives, the disjunct becomes 'not every' or 'some not'.
The conclusion mirrors the categorical arrangements without introducing new terms.
5
Apply to a concrete example
Take the first conjunction: 'Always either every B is C or every D is H, and every C is A, and every D is A.' The conclusion is 'Always either every B is A or some H is A.' Trace how each categorical contributes to the disjuncts.
This step internalizes the pattern through a worked example.

And as for [the case where it is] from the first and the third [figures], and let the parts of the disjunctive also be uniform in quantity and quality; its condition is that the disjunctive be affirmative, and that it be a true one that prevents vacancy without collection, and that the categorical [premises] with the disjunctive be in the form of the first figure and in the form of the third figure as was the case in categoricals. The eighth conjunction: the conjunction unchanged except that the major of the third is particular negative, so (Always either no "B" is "A" or not every "H" is "A"). And on this, the productivity is not hidden if the parts of the disjunctive are particular affirmative, or universal negative, or particular negative, and likewise also if the disjunctive is particular affirmative.

Commentary
This passage enumerates valid compound syllogisms formed by combining a disjunctive premise with categorical premises arranged in the first and third figures. The author, following the method of Ibn Sina in al-Isharat, provides detailed conjunctions (iqtiranāt) for these compound syllogisms. The conditions are: the disjunctive must be affirmative and a true exclusive disjunction (māni‘at al-khulūw dūn al-jam‘), and the categoricals must conform to the first and third figures. The author lists eight specific conjunctions out of a possible sixty-four, each varying the quality and quantity of the categorical majors. The examples use variable letters (B, J, A, D, H) as placeholders. The conclusion in every case is an affirmative exclusive disjunction. The first conjunction uses universal affirmative categoricals; the second uses universal negatives; the third mixes affirmative first major and negative third major; the fourth mixes negative first and affirmative third; the fifth uses a particular affirmative third major; the sixth uses a particular negative third major; the seventh uses a universal negative first and particular affirmative third; the eighth uses a particular negative third major. The author then generalizes that the pattern holds for other quantities and qualities of the disjunctive parts.

The Syllogism

Understanding Conjunctive Syllogisms with Disjunctives
Author enumerates productive conjunctive syllogisms with disjunctive and categorical premises from first and fourth figures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the components and conditions of a productive conjunctive syllogism (iqtirān) combining a disjunctive premise with categorical premises in the first and fourth figures.

Core Idea
A productive iqtirān requires an exclusive disjunction and categoricals in first and fourth figures.

In this passage, the author defines conjunctive syllogisms that combine a disjunctive premise with categorical premises, arranged according to the first and fourth figures of the categorical syllogism. The conditions for productivity are: the disjunctive must be affirmative and exclusive (māni‘at al-khuluw dūn al-jam‘), and the categoricals must follow the form of the first and fourth figures as in pure categorical syllogisms. Understanding these conditions is crucial for constructing valid compound arguments in the Islamic logical tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive premise
Locate the disjunctive proposition (munfaṣila) in the argument. It will have parts joined by 'either...or' (إما...وإما).
In this passage, the disjunctive is 'real' (ḥaqīqiyya) or exclusive of emptiness without conjunction.
2
Check the disjunctive type
The disjunctive must be affirmative and exclusive (exactly one part true). This is called māni‘at al-khuluw dūn al-jam‘.
If it were inclusive, the conditions change.
3
Identify the categorical premises
The argument includes categorical propositions (ḥamliyya) that are arranged in the first and fourth figures. For example, a universal affirmative categorical in the first figure position.
The categoricals may be universal or particular, affirmative or negative.
4
Verify the arrangement
The categoricals must be in the form of the first and fourth figures. This means the middle term positions match those of standard categorical syllogisms.
The author says 'as was in the categoricals' referring to the earlier discussion of figures.
5
State the conclusion
The conclusion is always an affirmative disjunctive proposition that is exclusive (māni‘at al-khuluw dūn al-jam‘).
The conclusion follows necessarily if the conditions are met.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كان من الأول والرابع، ولنفرض أجزاء المنفصلة متحدة الكمية والكيفية، وشرطه في الإنتاج إيجاب المنفصلة، وأن تكون حقيقية أو مانعة الخلو دون الجمع، وأن تكون الحَمْلية مع المنفصلة على هيئة الأول والرابع، كما كان في الحَمْليات وقرائنه المنتجة اثنان وثلاثون اقتراناً والنتيجة في الكل موجبة منفصلة مانعة الخلو دون الجمع:
*
الاقتران الأول من كليّة موجبة منفصلة أجزاؤها كليّة موجبة وكليات حَمْلية موجبة، كقولنا: (دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما كل "د" "هـ" وكل "ج" "د") و(كل "أ" "د" ونتيجته (دائماً إما كل "ب" "د" وإما بعض "هـ" "أ").
الاقتران الثاني: الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن الحَمْليات كليّات سالبة، ونتيجته دائماً د" إما لا شيء من وإما ليس كل "هـ" "هـ" "أ".
الاقتران الثالث الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن كبرى الأول كليّة موجبة وكبرى الرابع كلية سالبة، ونتيجته (دائماً إما كل "ب" "د" وإما ليس كل "هـ" "أ").
الاقتران الرابع الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن كبرى الأول سالبة كلية وكبرى الرابع كلية موجبة، ونتيجته (دائماً إما لا شيء من "ب" "د" وإما بعض "هـ" "أ").
الاقتران الخامس الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن كبرى الأول كلية موجبة وكبرى الرابع جزئية موجبة، ونتيجته (دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما بعض "هـ" "أ").
الاقتران السادس الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن كبرى الأول كلية سالبة وكبرى الرابع جزئية موجبة، ونتيجته (دائماً إما لا شيء من "ب" "د" وإما بعض "هـ" "أ").
وعلى هذا فلا يخفى الإنتاج إذا كانت أجزاء المنفصلة جزئية موجبة، أو كلية سالبة، أو جزئية سالبة وهي عشر اقترانات أخرى.
وإن كانت المنفصلة جزئية موجبة فقد تركب منها مع الحَمْليات ستة عشر اقتراناً أخرى، إلا أن النتيجة فيها جزئية موجبة منفصلة، فاعتبرها بنفسك.
And as for if it is from the first and fourth, and let us suppose the parts of the disjunctive are uniform in quantity and quality, and its condition for production is the affirmativity of the disjunctive, and that it be real or exclusive of emptiness without conjunction, and that the categoricals with the disjunctive be in the form of the first and fourth, as was in the categoricals, and its productive combinations are thirty-two conjunctive syllogisms, and the conclusion in all is an affirmative disjunctive exclusive of emptiness without conjunction: * The first conjunctive syllogism: from a universal affirmative disjunctive whose parts are universal affirmative and universal categorical affirmatives, like our saying: 'Always either every B is J and every D is H, and every J is D' and 'Every A is D', and its conclusion is 'Always either every B is D or some H is A'. The second conjunctive syllogism: the conjunctive syllogism in its same state, except that the categoricals are universal negatives, and its conclusion is 'Always either no D is something and not every H is H A' [garbled]. The third conjunctive syllogism: the conjunctive syllogism in its same state, except that the major of the first is universal affirmative and the major of the fourth is universal negative, and its conclusion is 'Always either every B is D or not every H is A'. The fourth conjunctive syllogism: the conjunctive syllogism in its same state, except that the major of the first is universal negative and the major of the fourth is universal affirmative, and its conclusion is 'Always either no B is D or some H is A'. The fifth conjunctive syllogism: the conjunctive syllogism in its same state, except that the major of the first is universal affirmative and the major of the fourth is particular affirmative, and its conclusion is 'Always either every B is J or some H is A'. The sixth conjunctive syllogism: the conjunctive syllogism in its same state, except that the major of the first is universal negative and the major of the fourth is particular affirmative, and its conclusion is 'Always either no B is D or some H is A'. And on this basis, production is not hidden when the parts of the disjunctive are particular affirmative, or universal negative, or particular negative, and they are ten other conjunctive syllogisms. And if the disjunctive is particular affirmative, then it may be composed with the categoricals into sixteen other conjunctive syllogisms, except that the conclusion in them is a particular affirmative disjunctive, so consider them yourself.
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of productive conjunctive syllogisms (iqtirānāt) that combine a disjunctive premise (munfaṣila) with categorical premises (ḥamliyya), specifically when the disjunctive and categoricals are arranged according to the first and fourth figures of the categorical syllogism. The author states the general condition: the disjunctive must be affirmative, and it must be real (ḥaqīqiyya) or exclusive of emptiness without conjunction (māni‘at al-khuluw dūn al-jam‘), which means an exclusive disjunction where exactly one part is true. The categoricals must be in the first and fourth figures as in pure categorical syllogisms. There are 32 productive combinations. The author then lists the first six iqtirans in detail, each with a specific combination of quantities and qualities of the major premises, and gives the resulting disjunctive conclusion. For example, the first iqtiran uses a universal affirmative disjunctive with universal affirmative categoricals, and the conclusion is a universal affirmative disjunctive. The second iqtiran changes the categoricals to universal negatives. After the sixth, the author notes that similar reasoning applies when the disjunctive parts are particular affirmative, universal negative, or particular negative, yielding ten more iqtirans. Finally, if the disjunctive itself is particular affirmative, there are sixteen more iqtirans, but the conclusion becomes particular affirmative. The author leaves the detailed enumeration to the reader.

Mapping the First Six Conjunctive Syllogisms
Author enumerates productive conjunctive syllogisms with disjunctive and categorical premises from first and fourth figures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to match the premises of the first six enumerated conjunctive syllogisms to their given conclusions.

Core Idea
The first six iqtirans systematically vary the quantity and quality of the major premises, producing distinct disjunctive conclusions.

After stating the general conditions, the author lists six specific conjunctive syllogisms (iqtirānāt) as examples. Each one combines a universal affirmative disjunctive with two categoricals in the first and fourth figures, but the categoricals differ in quantity and quality. The resulting conclusion is always an exclusive disjunction. In this lesson, we will map each of these six iqtirans to understand how the variation in premises affects the conclusion.

Guided Path
1
List the six iqtirans
Read the passage and identify the six iqtirans. They are numbered from the first to the sixth. Each begins with 'الاقتران الأول' etc.
The first iqtiran is described in detail; the subsequent ones are described relative to it ('الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن...').
2
Note the describing phrase
For each iqtiran after the first, note what changes: 'except that the major of the first is ... and the major of the fourth is ...'. Record the quantity and quality of these major premises.
The first iqtiran uses universal affirmative for both majors. The second uses universal negative for both. The third: first universal affirmative, fourth universal negative. The fourth: first universal negative, fourth universal affirmative. The fifth: first universal affirmative, fourth particular affirmative. The sixth: first universal negative, fourth particular affirmative.
3
Write the conclusion for each
The text gives the conclusion for each iqtiran. Write them down. For example, first: 'دائماً إما كل ب د وإما بعض هـ أ'. Second: garbled but likely 'دائماً إما لا شيء من ب د وإما ليس كل هـ أ'. etc.
The conclusions are all affirmative exclusive disjunctions with the first disjunct relating to B and D, the second to H and A.
4
Identify the pattern
Observe that the first disjunct changes according to the major of the first figure categorical, and the second disjunct changes according to the major of the fourth figure categorical. If the major is affirmative, the disjunct is affirmative; if negative, the disjunct is negative. The quantity of the second disjunct matches the quantity of the fourth major (universal -> 'not every'? Actually it's tricky).
Check: For first (both affirmative universal), conclusion first disjunct: 'كل ب د' (universal affirmative), second: 'بعض هـ أ' (particular affirmative). For fourth (first universal negative, fourth universal affirmative), first disjunct: 'لا شيء من ب د' (universal negative), second: 'بعض هـ أ'. So the pattern holds.
5
Summarize the mapping
Create a table mapping the premises to conclusions for the six iqtirans. This will help you see the system.
The table can be used to generate conclusions for other combinations as well.

Extending the Pattern to All 32 Conjunctive Syllogisms
Author enumerates productive conjunctive syllogisms with disjunctive and categorical premises from first and fourth figures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the total number of productive conjunctive syllogisms and infer conclusions for variations not explicitly listed.

Core Idea
The 32 iqtirans arise from systematic variations of the disjunctive and categorical premises; the pattern scales predictably.

After detailing the first six iqtirans, the author states that there are 32 productive combinations in total. He then mentions that if the parts of the disjunctive are particular affirmative, universal negative, or particular negative, there are ten more iqtirans. Finally, if the disjunctive itself is particular affirmative, there are sixteen more. In this lesson, we will see how these numbers arise from combinatorial variations and how to extend the pattern.

Guided Path
1
Review the base pattern
The first six iqtirans assumed a universal affirmative disjunctive with universal affirmative parts. We saw variations in the categorical major premises (2 quantities × 2 qualities = 4 types for each major, but only some combinations are given? Actually the first six cover: both universal affirmative, both universal negative, first affirmative second negative, first negative second affirmative, first affirmative second particular affirmative, first negative second particular affirmative. That's 6 out of 16 possible combinations of the two majors (4×4).
The author later adds variations in the disjunctive parts themselves.
2
Count the variations in disjunctive parts
When the disjunctive is universal, its parts can be: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative. That's 4 possibilities. The author mentions three more besides the universal affirmative: particular affirmative, universal negative, particular negative (note: universal affirmative already used). So that gives 3 more sets of 6? Wait, the text says: 'وعلى هذا فلا يخفى الإنتاج إذا كانت أجزاء المنفصلة جزئية موجبة، أو كلية سالبة، أو جزئية سالبة وهي عشر اقترانات أخرى.' That is: if the parts of the disjunctive are particular affirmative, or universal negative, or particular negative, there are ten other iqtirans. So for each of those three types of disjunctive parts, how many iqtirans? Possibly the same pattern of 6 each? But 3×6=18, not 10. So the number is different. The text says 'عشر اقترانات أخرى' ten others. Perhaps the enumeration changes because the disjunctive parts themselves impose restrictions. We need to be careful.
The author does not elaborate further; he says 'it is not hidden' meaning the reader should infer.
3
Understand the sixteen additional iqtirans
If the disjunctive itself is particular affirmative (instead of universal), there are sixteen more iqtirans. The conclusion then becomes particular affirmative disjunctive. So total: 6 (for universal affirmative disjunctive parts) + 10 (for three other types of parts) + 16 (for particular disjunctive) = 32.
This matches the initial claim of 32 productive iqtirans.
4
Practice inferring a conclusion
Take one of the unseen variations, e.g., disjunctive parts universal negative, with categorical majors both universal affirmative. Use the pattern from the first iqtiran but change the quality of the disjuncts accordingly. The conclusion should be an exclusive disjunction with appropriate quality and quantity.
The pattern: first disjunct conclusion follows the first figure categorical, second disjunct follows the fourth figure categorical, both in quantity/quality of the corresponding disjunct parts.

Conditions for Compound Syllogisms in Second and Third Figures
Author lists conditions and examples for productive conjunctive syllogisms with disjunctive and categorical premises from second and third figures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a compound syllogism from a disjunctive premise and categorical premises in the second and third figures is productive based on the given conditions.

Core Idea
Productive compound syllogisms in second/third figures require a real disjunctive, negative categoricals, and uniformity of quantity and quality.

This passage presents the rules for constructing valid compound syllogisms when the categorical premises belong to the second or third figures. The author lists specific conditions for the disjunctive and categorical premises, then enumerates the productive forms. Understanding these conditions allows you to quickly identify valid combinations without memorizing all 24 cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive premise's type
Check if the disjunctive is real (haqiqiyya) or exclusive-of-emptiness (māni‘at al-khuluw), and whether it is affirmative. Only affirmative disjunctives are productive.
The text says: 'شرطه إيجاب المنفصلة، وأن يكون حقيقية أو مانعة الخلو دون الجمع' (its condition is the affirmativity of the disjunctive, and that it be real or exclusive of emptiness without conjunction).
2
Check the categoricals' quantity and quality
The categorical premises must be negative (saliba). Additionally, the major premise of the second figure must be universal (kulliyya).
Text: 'وأن لا تكون الحَمْليات موجبات، وأن تكون كبرى الثاني كلية' (and that the categoricals be negatives, and that the major of the second be universal).
3
Check uniformity of the disjunctive parts
If the parts of the disjunctive are particular affirmatives or universal negatives, then for the syllogism to be productive, the disjunctive must be universal affirmative or particular negative respectively.
Text: 'وإذا كانت أجزاء المنفصلة جزئية موجبة، أو كلية سالبة أن تكون أجزاء المنفصلة كلية موجبة، أو جزئية سالبة' (and if the parts of the disjunctive are particular affirmative or universal negative, then let the parts of the disjunctive be universal affirmative or particular negative).
4
Locate the conclusion form
The conclusion is always an affirmative disjunctive exclusive-of-emptiness-without-conjunction (māni‘at al-khuluw dūn al-jam‘). The exact content depends on the premises.
Text: 'والنتيجة في الكل موجبة منفصلة مانعة الخلو دون الجمع' (and the conclusion in all is an affirmative disjunctive exclusive of emptiness without conjunction).
5
Apply the conditions to example iqtirans
Verify that the given examples (first and second iqtirans) satisfy all conditions. For instance, the first iqtiran uses a universal affirmative disjunctive with universal affirmative parts, and universal negative categoricals.
The first iqtiran: 'دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما كل "د" "ج" ...' and the categoricals 'لا شيء من "أ" "ج"' and 'لا شيء من "د" "ج"'.

Continuation of the first art: On defining the forms of conjunctive syllogisms. 107. The sixth conjunctive syllogism: the conjunctive syllogism in its same state, except that the major of the first is universal negative and the major of the fourth is particular affirmative, and its conclusion is 'Always either no B is D or some H is A'. And on this basis, production is not hidden when the parts of the disjunctive are particular affirmative, or universal negative, or particular affirmative, and they are ten other conjunctive syllogisms. And if the disjunctive is particular affirmative, then it may be composed with the categoricals into sixteen other conjunctive syllogisms, except that the conclusion in them is a particular affirmative disjunctive, so consider them yourself. And as for if it is from the second and third, and let us speak and also in them, if the parts of the disjunctive are uniform in quantity and quality, and its condition for production is the affirmativity of the disjunctive, and that it be real or exclusive of emptiness without conjunction, and that the categoricals be negatives, and that the major of the second be universal, and if the parts of the disjunctive are particular affirmatives, or universal negatives, then let the parts of the disjunctive be universal affirmative, or particular negative, and its productive combinations are twenty-four conjunctive syllogisms, and the conclusion in all is an affirmative disjunctive exclusive of emptiness without conjunction: The first conjunctive syllogism: from a universal affirmative disjunctive whose parts are universal affirmatives, and universal categorical negatives, like our saying: 'Always either every B is J or every D is J or every D is H and nothing of H and nothing of A is J' and 'Nothing of D is J', so 'Always either no B is A or not every H is J'. The second conjunctive syllogism: the conjunctive syllogism in its same state, except that the major of the second is universal negative and the major of the third is universal affirmative, so 'Always either no B is A or some H is J'. The third conjunctive syllogism: the conjunctive syllogism in its same state, except that the major of the second is universal negative and the major of the third is particular affirmative, and its conclusion is like the previous one. The fourth conjunctive syllogism: the conjunctive syllogism in its same state, except that the major of the second is universal negative and the major of the third is particular negative, and its conclusion is like the first conjunctive syllogism. And on this basis, production is not hidden when the parts of the disjunctive are particular affirmatives, or universal negatives, or particular negatives, and they are eight conjunctive syllogisms.

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of compound syllogisms but now focuses on combinations from the second and third figures. The author repeats a summary of the previous case (from first and fourth figures) likely due to a page break, then introduces the new case. The conditions for production here are: the disjunctive must be affirmative and real or exclusive (māni‘at al-khuluw dūn al-jam‘ ), the categoricals must be negative, and the major premise of the second figure must be universal. Additionally, if the disjunctive parts are particular affirmative or universal negative, then the disjunctive must be universal affirmative or particular negative. There are 24 productive iqtirans. The author then lists the first four as examples. The first iqtiran uses a universal affirmative disjunctive with three universal affirmative parts? Actually the example seems to have two parts: 'every B is J' and 'every D is J or every D is H' – this is a bit garbled. The categoricals are universal negatives. The conclusion is a universal negative disjunctive: 'either no B is A or not every H is J'. The second iqtiran changes the major of the third premise to universal affirmative, yielding a particular affirmative conclusion. The third makes the third premise particular affirmative with same conclusion. The fourth makes it particular negative, returning to the first conclusion. The author then states that for other variations (particular affirmative, etc.), there are eight more iqtirans, leaving the details to the reader.

Key Takeaway
For compound syllogisms from the second and third figures, productivity hinges on an affirmative disjunctive, negative categoricals, and a universal major in the second figure, with additional uniformity conditions for the disjunctive parts.

The Syllogism
advanced
12 min
Compound Syllogisms from Figures II+III: Conditions and Examples
Author states conditions and gives example conjunctions for II+III.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions for a productive compound syllogism from figures II+III and reconstruct the conclusion pattern of the first four example conjunctions.

Core Idea
Compound syllogisms from II+III require an affirmative disjunction and negative categoricals, yielding a disjunctive conclusion.

The author now turns to compound syllogisms where the disjunctive major is from figures II+III. He specifies that the disjunction must be affirmative and either real or exclusive of emptiness but not conjunction, the categoricals must be negative, and the major of the second must be universal. He then gives four example conjunctions with their conclusions, showing the pattern.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditions
The disjunction must be affirmative (either real or māni‘at al-khulū dūn al-jam‘). The categorical minors must not be affirmative (i.e., they must be negative). The major of the second figure must be universal. Also, the parts of the disjunction must be uniform in quantity and quality.
If these conditions hold, the conjunction is productive.
2
Examine the first example
The first conjunction: disjunctive major with universal affirmative parts and universal negative categoricals (e.g., 'no D is J') yields a conclusion: 'always either no B is A or not every H is J'. Notice the conclusion mirrors the premises.
The variables: B, A, D, J, H. The disjunction originally included three parts; the categorical eliminates one.
3
Vary the categorical majors
The second and third examples change the categorical majors while keeping the same disjunction. The conclusion becomes 'either no B is A or some H is J' when a major is universal affirmative or particular affirmative.
The fourth example returns to the pattern of the first.
4
Extrapolate to other variations
After these four, the author notes that when the parts of the disjunctive are particular affirmative, universal negative, or particular negative, eight more conjunctions follow, all with similar structure.
If the disjunctive itself is particular affirmative, twelve more conjunctions arise with particular affirmative conclusion.

And if the parts of the disjunctive are particular affirmatives, or universal negatives, the parts of the disjunctive must be universal affirmative, or particular negative. The productive pairs are twenty-four conjunctions, and the conclusion in all is an affirmative disjunctive exclusive of emptiness but not of conjunction. The first conjunction: from a universal affirmative disjunctive whose parts are universal affirmatives and universal categorical negatives, like our saying: (Always either every B is J or every D is J or every D is H and no H is... and no A is J) and (no D is J), so (Always either no B is A or not every H is J). The second conjunction: the conjunction is as it is, except that the major of the second is universal negative and the major of the third is universal affirmative, so (Always either no B is A or some H is J). The third conjunction: the conjunction is as it is, except that the major of the second is universal negative and the major of the third is particular affirmative, and its conclusion is like the previous one. The fourth conjunction: the conjunction is as it is, except that the major of the second is universal negative and the major of the third is particular negative, and its conclusion is like the first conjunction. Thus production is not hidden when the parts of the disjunctive are particular affirmatives, or universal negatives, or particular negatives—these are eight conjunctions.

The second conjunction: the conjunction is as it is, except that the major of the second is universal negative and the major of the fourth is universal affirmative, like our saying: (Always either every B is J or every J is H and no A is J) and (every D is J), so (Always either no B is A or some and some H is D). The third conjunction: the conjunction is as it is, except that the major of the fourth is particular affirmative, and its conclusion is like the previous one. Thus production is not hidden when the parts of the disjunctive are particular affirmative, or universal negatives, or particular negatives—these are seven other conjunctions. And if the disjunctive is particular affirmative, ten other conjunctions parallel to the previous ten are composed from it, except that the conclusion in them is a particular affirmative disjunctive, so consider them yourself. As for when it is from the third and fourth, let us suppose in it if the parts of the disjunctive are uniform...
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of a systematic enumeration of productive conjunctions (اقترانات منتجة) formed by combining a disjunctive premise (منفصلة) with categorical premises (حَمْليات) in specific figure combinations. The author has previously discussed conjunctions involving figures I and IV, and now moves to combinations from the second and third figures (II+III) and then from the second and fourth figures (II+IV). The discussion is technical, aimed at logicians who already understand the basic figures and moods.

The passage first states the conditions for productive conjunctions when the disjunctive is from figures II+III: the parts of the disjunctive must be uniform in quantity and quality, the disjunctive must be affirmative and either real (حقيقية) or exclusive of emptiness but not of conjunction (مانعة الخلو دون الجمع), the categoricals must not be affirmatives, and the major premise of the second figure must be universal. The author then lists four example conjunctions (اقترانات) with their conclusions, using placeholder letters (ب, ج, أ, د, هـ). For instance, the first conjunction: a universal affirmative disjunctive with universal affirmative parts and universal negative categoricals yields a conclusion that is either a universal negative or a particular negative disjunctive. The second and third conjunctions vary the categorical majors, and the fourth returns to the pattern of the first. After these examples, the author notes that the pattern can be extended when the parts of the disjunctive are particular affirmative, universal negative, or particular negative, yielding eight more conjunctions. If the disjunctive itself is particular affirmative, sixteen more conjunctions arise, all with particular affirmative disjunctive conclusions.

Next, the author treats the combination from figures II+IV. Here the conditions are more specific: the disjunctive must be affirmative real or exclusive of emptiness but not of conjunction, the predicate of one part of the disjunction must be the subject of the other (to ensure uniform predicate in the categoricals), the major of the second must be universal, and further conditions on the quality of the majors based on the parts of the disjunctive. The author gives three example conjunctions. The first: a universal affirmative disjunctive with universal affirmative parts and universal negative categoricals yields a conclusion like 'either no B is A or not every H is D'. The second: varying the majors produces a conclusion with a particular affirmative component. The third: if the fourth major is particular affirmative, the conclusion remains similar. The pattern continues for other quantity/quality combinations, leading to seven more conjunctions when the disjunctive parts are particular affirmative, universal negative, or particular negative. If the disjunctive itself is particular affirmative, ten parallel conjunctions arise. The passage then begins the discussion for figures III+IV but breaks off.

Throughout, the author emphasizes that the conclusions in all these conjunctions are affirmative disjunctives that are exclusive of emptiness but not of conjunction (مانعة الخلو دون الجمع), meaning the two parts cannot both be false but can both be true. This systematic enumeration illustrates the depth of Aristotelian/Islamic logical analysis of compound syllogisms.

Key Terms
المنفصلة
الحمليات
اقتران
مانعة الخلو دون الجمع

In the compound syllogisms from II+III, what must be true of the categorical minor premises?
A) They must both be affirmative.
B) They must not be affirmative (i.e., at least one must be negative).
C) They must be universal affirmative.
D) They must be particular negative.

Key Takeaway
Productive compound syllogisms from II+III require an affirmative disjunction, negative categoricals, and universal major of the second figure, yielding a disjunctive conclusion that is exclusive of emptiness but not of conjunction.

Construct a compound syllogism of the II+III type using a fiqh example: Let the disjunction be 'Every intoxicant is forbidden or every nabidh is forbidden' (as two parts), and add a categorical 'No nabidh is intoxicant'. Following the pattern of the first conjunction, derive the conclusion. Does your conclusion match the expected form?

The Syllogism
advanced

Conditions for Mixed Syllogisms: Second and Fourth Figures with Disjunctive Major
Author enumerates conditions and productive conjunctions for second and fourth figures with disjunctive major.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions for a valid mixed syllogism with a disjunctive major in the second and fourth figures, and verify that a given example satisfies them.

Core Idea
A disjunctive major requires its parts to be connected with categorical minors through shared terms.

This passage systematically enumerates valid mixed syllogisms combining a disjunctive major with two categorical premises in the second and fourth figures. The author lays out strict conditions for production and provides illustrative examples. By mastering these conditions, you will be able to construct and validate such mixed syllogisms, a tool used in Islamic jurisprudence and theology to reason from hypothetical disjunctions to categorical conclusions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure and major premise
Determine if the syllogism is second or fourth figure. The disjunctive proposition is the major premise. For the second figure, the major categorical must be universal; for the fourth, it may be particular.
The major categorical is the one that contains the middle term as predicate in the second figure.
2
Check the type of disjunction
The disjunctive must be an affirmative real disjunction or of the type 'mani'at al-khuluw duna al-jam'' (inclusive OR). This ensures that both disjuncts cannot be false together.
An exclusive disjunction ('mani'at al-jam' wal-khuluw') would not produce the required conclusion form.
3
Verify term interconnection
The predicate of one disjunct must be the subject of the other disjunct. This ensures that the categorical minors can share terms with the disjuncts.
For instance, if the disjuncts are 'Every B is C' and 'Every C is H', then C is the predicate in the first and subject in the second.
4
Apply quantity/quality conditions
Check the conditions on the categorical majors based on the parts of the disjunction. For the second figure, if the disjuncts are universal affirmatives or particular negatives, the major must be negative. For the fourth figure, if the major is particular affirmative, the disjuncts must be universal affirmatives or particular negatives.
The text lists these conditions explicitly for the twenty productive conjunctions.
5
Test an example
Take the first example: 'Always either every B is C or every C is H, and no A is C, and no D is C' gives 'Always either no B is A or not every H is D.' Trace how the terms connect and verify the conditions.
The conclusion is a disjunction of the same type as the major.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كانت المنفصلة جزئية موجبة فقد تركب منها مع الحَمْليات اقترانات أخرى اثنا عشر، إلا أن النتيجة فيها جزئية موجبة منفصلة مانعة الخلو دون الجمع. وأما إن كان من الثاني والرابع، وليكن الفرض أجزاء المنفصلة متحدة الكمية والكيفية وشرطه في الإنتاج أن تكون المنفصلة موجبة حقيقية أو مانعة الخلو دون الجمع، وأن يكون المحمول في أحد جزأي الانفصال موضوعاً في الآخر؛ ضرورة اتحاد المحمول في الحمليات، وأن تكون كبرى الثاني كليّة. وإن كانت موجبة أن تكون أجزاء الانفصال كليات سالبة، أو جزئيات موجبة. وإن كانت أجزاء المنفصلة كلية موجبة أو جزئيات سالبة أن تكون كبرى الثاني سالبة. وإن كانت كبرى الرابع جزئية موجبة أن تكون أجزاء المنفصلة كليّة موجبة، أو جزئية سالبة، وقرائنه المنتجة عشرون اقتراناً، ونتيجته في الكل موجبة منفصلة مانعة الخلو دون الجمع: الاقتران الأول من كليّة موجبة منفصلة أجزاؤها كليات موجبة وكليات حَمْلية سالبة، كقولنا: (دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما كل "ج" "هـ" ولا شيء من "أ" "ج") و(لا شيء من "د" "ج") فـ (دائماً إما لا شيء من "ب" "أ" وإما ليس كل "هـ" "د"). الاقتران الثاني: الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن كبرى الثاني كلية سالبة، وكبرى الرابع كلية موجبة، كقولنا دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما كل "ج" "هـ" ولا شيء من "أ" "ج") و(كل "د" "ج") فـ (دائماً إما لا شيء من "ب" "أ" وإما بعض "هـ" "د"). الاقتران الثالث: الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن كبرى الرابع جزئية موجبة، ونتيجته كالتي قبله. وعلى هذا فلا يخفى الإنتاج وأجزاء المنفصلة جزئية موجبة، أو كليات سالبة، أو جزئيات سالبة، وهي سبع اقترانات أخرى. وإن كانت المنفصلة جزئية موجبة، فقد يتركب منها مع الحَمْليات عشرة اقترانات أخرى موازية للعشرة السابقة، إلا أن النتيجة فيها جزئية موجبة منفصلة، فاعتبرها بنفسك.
And if the disjunctive is particular affirmative, twelve other conjunctions are composed from it with categoricals, except that the conclusion in them is a particular affirmative disjunctive, preventing emptiness without preventing collection. As for if it is from the second and fourth (figures), let the assumption be that the parts of the disjunctive are uniform in quantity and quality, and its condition for production is that the disjunctive be an affirmative real (disjunctive) or preventing emptiness without preventing collection, and that the predicate in one of the two parts of the disjunction be the subject in the other, due to the necessity of the predicate being uniform in the categoricals, and that the major of the second (figure) be universal. And if it is affirmative, then the parts of the disjunction must be universal negatives, or particular affirmatives. And if the parts of the disjunctive are universal affirmative or particular negative, then the major of the second must be negative. And if the major of the fourth is particular affirmative, then the parts of the disjunctive must be universal affirmative, or particular negative. Its productive conjunctions are twenty conjunctions, and the conclusion in all is an affirmative disjunctive preventing emptiness without preventing collection: The first conjunction from a universal affirmative disjunctive whose parts are universal affirmative and categorical universal negatives, as our saying: (Always either every B is C or every C is H, and no A is C) and (no D is C) gives (Always either no B is A or not every H is D). The second conjunction: the conjunction remains the same, except that the major of the second is universal negative, and the major of the fourth is universal affirmative, as our saying: Always either every B is C or every C is H, and no A is C) and (every D is C) gives (Always either no B is A or some H is D). The third conjunction: the conjunction remains the same, except that the major of the fourth is particular affirmative, and its conclusion is like the one before it. And upon this, production is not hidden, and (when) the parts of the disjunctive are particular affirmative, or universal negatives, or particular negatives, they are seven other conjunctions. And if the disjunctive is particular affirmative, then ten other conjunctions parallel to the previous ten are composed from it with categoricals, except that the conclusion in them is a particular affirmative disjunctive, so consider it yourself.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage is a technical enumeration of valid mixed syllogisms where one premise is a disjunctive proposition and the others are categoricals, specifically focusing on the second and fourth figures of categorical syllogisms. The author begins by noting that if the disjunctive major is particular affirmative, twelve compound syllogisms result with a particular affirmative disjunctive conclusion. Then the main discussion is for when the disjunctive is universal affirmative. The key conditions for a productive syllogism are: the disjunctive must be genuinely affirmative and of the 'exclusive' type that allows both disjuncts to be true (i.e., inclusive or). The predicate of one disjunct must be the subject of the other, ensuring the categorical major premise has a uniform predicate across the two categorical minor premises. For the second figure, the major premise must be universal. Depending on the quantity and quality of the disjunct parts, the major of the second figure must be negative or the major of the fourth must be affirmative with specific constraints. Twenty productive conjunctions are given. Three explicit examples are provided using variables (B, C, H, A, D). For instance, from 'Always either every B is C or every C is H, and no A is C, and no D is C' we conclude 'Always either no B is A or not every H is D.' This pattern illustrates how the positions of terms in the disjunctive and categoricals combine to yield a disjunctive conclusion. The author then notes that when the disjunct parts have other forms (particular affirmative, universal negative, particular negative), seven more conjunctions exist. Finally, for a particular affirmative disjunctive major, ten more conjunctions parallel the previous ones, again yielding a particular affirmative disjunctive conclusion. The reader is left to derive them, indicating an exhaustive enumeration.

Key Terms
منفصلة (munfasila): A disjunctive proposition, here specifically a compound proposition of the form 'either P or Q' where P and Q are categoricals, and the disjunction is 'مانعة الخلو دون الجمع' (inclusive or). [Note: In earlier logic, 'munfasila' strictly meant exclusive disjunction; here it is inclusive, as specified by the qualifier.]
حملية (hamliyya): A categorical proposition of the form 'Subject is Predicate' (e.g., 'every B is C'). [Note: Stable term across Islamic logic.]
اقتران (iqtiran): A conjunction or combination of premises forming a syllogism; here specifically the pairing of a disjunctive proposition with two categoricals. [Note: In some contexts, 'iqtiran' refers specifically to categorical syllogisms, but here it is used for mixed syllogisms.]
كبرى (kubra): The major premise of a syllogism; in the context of second and fourth figures, it refers to the categorical premises that occupy the positions of major premises in those figures. [Note: Stable term.]
مانعة الخلو دون الجمع (mani'at al-khuluw duna al-jam'): A disjunction that prevents the case where both disjuncts are false (emptiness) but allows both to be true (collection); equivalent to inclusive logical OR. [Note: The terminology for disjunction types is consistent across the tradition.]

C) Its parts must be particular.
D) It must be an exclusive disjunction.

Key Takeaway
Valid mixed syllogisms with a disjunctive major require interconnected terms, a non-exclusive disjunction, and specific quantity/quality conditions on the categorical minors based on the figure.

Conditions and Enumeration of Valid Disjunctive Syllogisms
Author specifies conditions and enumerates 32 valid conjunctions for syllogisms with a universal affirmative disjunctive major and categorical minors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a valid conjunctive syllogism with a universal affirmative disjunctive major and identify which of the 32 conjunctions applies in a given example.

Core Idea
A universal affirmative disjunctive major with uniform categorical minors yields 32 productive conjunctions, each giving an exclusive disjunctive conclusion.

In this lesson, we examine a key passage from the Daqa'iq al-Haqa'iq that systematically lays out the conditions and enumerates the valid forms of conjunctive syllogisms where the major premise is a universal affirmative disjunction and the minors are categoricals. This foundational exercise in parsing logical combinatorics is essential for constructing exhaustive arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overall structure of the passage
Notice that the passage first states conditions on the categoricals and the disjunctive, then announces the total number of productive conjunctions, and then lists examples. This structure helps you separate general rules from specific instantiations.
2
Extract the conditions for the categoricals and the disjunctive
Look for phrases like 'الحَمْليات متحدة الكمية' and 'شرطه إيجاب المنفصلة'. The categoricals must be uniform in quantity (all universal or all particular). The disjunctive must be affirmative and either haqiqiyya or mani'at al-khuluww duna al-jam'.
3
Determine the number of productive conjunctions and the nature of the conclusion
The passage explicitly states that there are 32 productive conjunctions (قرائنه المنتجة اثنان وثلاثون اقترانا) and that the conclusion is always an affirmative exclusive disjunction (موجبة منفصلة مانعة الخلو دون الجمع).
4
Analyze the first few examples to see how patterns vary
Study the first six conjunctions. Notice how changing the quantity and quality of the categoricals and the parts of the disjunctive affects the conclusion. For instance, when categoricals are universal positives, the conclusion has 'بعض' (some); when they are universal negatives, the conclusion uses 'ليس كل' (not every).
The pattern shows a systematic variation.
5
Understand how the count reduces for certain types of parts
The final sentence notes that when the parts of the disjunctive are particulars affirmative, universals negative, or particulars negative, the conjunctions reduce to ten. This is because some combinations become identical or invalid under those conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تتمة الفن الأول: في تعريف صور القياسات الاقترانية الكمية والكيفية، والحَمْليات متحدة الكمية - وأنت تعرف ما وراء ذلك - وشرطه إيجاب المنفصلة، وأن تكون حقيقية أو مانعة الخلو دون الجمع. وإن كانت كبرى الثالث جزئية أن تكون أجزاء المنفصلة كليات موجبة أو جزئيات سوالب. وإن كانت كبرى الرابع سالبة أن تكون كلية، وإن كانت جزئية موجبة أن تكون أجزاء المنفصلة كليات موجبة أو جزئيات سوالب. وإن كانت أجزاء المنفصلة جزئيات موجبة، أو كليات سالبة، أن تكون كبرى الرابع كلية سالبة، وقرائنه المنتجة اثنان وثلاثون اقترانا والنتيجة في الكل موجبة منفصلة مانعة الخلو دون الجمع. الاقتران الأول من كليّة موجبة منفصلة أجزاؤها كليّة موجبة وكليات حَمْلية موجبة، كقولنا: (دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما كل "د" "هـ" وكل "ب" "د") و(كل "أ" "د") ونتيجته (دائماً إما بعض "ج" "د" وإما بعض "هـ" "أ"). الاقتران الثاني الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن الحمليات كليّات سالبة، ونتيجته دائماً إما ليس كل "ج" "د" وإما ليس كل "هـ" "أ"). الاقتران الثالث الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن كبرى الثالث كلية موجبة وكبرى الرابع كلية سالبة، ونتيجته (دائماً إما بعض "ج" "د" وإما ليس كل "هـ" "أ"). الاقتران الرابع الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن كبرى الثالث كلية سالبة وكبرى الرابع كلية موجبة، ونتيجته (دائماً إما ليس كل "ج" "د" وإما بعض "هـ" "أ"). الاقتران الخامس الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن الحَمْليات جزئيات موجبة، ونتيجته (دائماً إما بعض "ج" "د" وإما بعض "هـ" "أ"). الاقتران السادس الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن كبرى الثالث جزئية سالبة وكبرى الرابع جزئية موجبة، ونتيجته (دائماً إما ليس كل "ج" "د" وإما بعض "هـ" "أ"). وعلى هذا فلا يخفى الإنتاج إذا كانت أجزاء المنفصلة جزئيات موجبة، أو كليات سوالب أو جزئيات سوالب هي عشر اقترانات.
This is the continuation of the first art: on defining the forms of conjunctive syllogisms. [Concerning] quantity and quality, and the categoricals are uniform in quantity — and you know what lies beyond that. Its condition is the affirmation of the disjunctive, and that it be real (haqiqiyya) or exclusive disjunction (mani'at al-khuluww duna al-jam'). And if the minor of the third [figure] is particular, [then] the parts of the disjunctive must be universals affirmative or particulars negative. And if the major of the fourth [figure] is negative, it must be universal; and if it is particular affirmative, [then] the parts of the disjunctive must be universals affirmative or particulars negative. And if the parts of the disjunctive are particulars affirmative, or universals negative, [then] the major of the fourth must be universal negative. Its productive conjunctions are thirty-two conjunctions, and the result in all is an affirmative disjunctive exclusive (mani'at al-khuluww duna al-jam'). The first conjunction: from a universal affirmative disjunctive whose parts are universal affirmative and universal categoricals affirmative, like our saying: 'Always, either every B is C or every D is H, and every B is D' and 'Every A is D', and its result: 'Always, either some C is D or some H is A.' The second conjunction: the conjunction remains the same except that the categoricals are universal negatives, and its result: 'Always, either not every C is D or not every H is A.' The third conjunction: the conjunction remains the same except that the minor of the third is universal affirmative and the major of the fourth is universal negative, and its result: 'Always, either some C is D or not every H is A.' The fourth conjunction: the conjunction remains the same except that the minor of the third is universal negative and the major of the fourth is universal affirmative, and its result: 'Always, either not every C is D or some H is A.' The fifth conjunction: the conjunction remains the same except that the categoricals are particulars affirmative, and its result: 'Always, either some C is D or some H is A.' The sixth conjunction: the conjunction remains the same except that the minor of the third is particular negative and the major of the fourth is particular affirmative, and its result: 'Always, either not every C is D or some H is A.' And accordingly, the production is not hidden when the parts of the disjunctive are particulars affirmative, or universals negative, or particulars negative: they are ten conjunctions.
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This passage defines the conditions for valid conjunctive syllogisms (qiyas iqtirani) where the major premise is a disjunctive proposition (munfasila) and the minor premises are categoricals (hamliyya). The author begins by stating that the categoricals must be uniform in quantity (i.e., all universal or all particular). The disjunctive must be affirmative and either haqiqiyya (real) or mani'at al-khuluww duna al-jam' (exclusive disjunction that forbids both false but not both true). Then he details conditions for specific figures: if the minor of the third figure is particular, the parts of the disjunctive must be universals affirmative or particulars negative. Similarly for the fourth figure... The total number of productive conjunctions is 32, and the conclusion is always an affirmative exclusive disjunction. He then illustrates the first six conjunctions with examples using variables (ب, ج, د, هـ, أ). The patterns show how varying the quantity and quality of the categoricals and the parts of the disjunctive yields different results. Finally, he notes that when the parts of the disjunctive are particulars affirmative, or universals negative, or particulars negative, the conjunctions reduce to ten. This is a technical enumeration essential for understanding the combinatorics of syllogistic forms.

Particular Affirmative Disjunctive Syllogisms
Rules for particular affirmative disjunctive syllogisms and uniform application when predicates differ.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the number and type of valid conjunctive syllogisms when the disjunctive major is particular affirmative, and apply the same rules when the categorical minors have different predicates.

Core Idea
A particular affirmative disjunctive major halves the valid conjunctions to 16, but the same rules for production apply.

In the previous lesson, we saw 32 valid conjunctions when the disjunctive major is universal. The author now turns to the particular affirmative case. He also makes a general remark that if the predicates in the categorical minors differ, the same conditions apply as when they are identical. This passage is a parsing exercise that requires careful attention to the relationship between universal and particular disjunctives.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of disjunctive major
Read the Arabic: 'وإن كانت المنفصلة جزئية موجبة' — the disjunctive is particular affirmative. This means it applies to some instances only, not all.
2
Determine the number of valid conjunctions
The text says: 'فقد يأتلف منها مع الحمْليات اقترانات أخرى ستة عشر موازية لهذه الاقترانات' — there are sixteen conjunctions parallel to the previous ones (the 32 of the universal case). Note that it is exactly half.
The parallelism means each conjunction in the universal case has a counterpart in the particular case, but with a weaker conclusion.
3
Identify the conclusion type
The conclusion is 'جزئية موجبة منفصلة مانعة الخلو دون الجمع' — a particular affirmative exclusive disjunction (man'at al-khuluw without man'at al-jam'). This means at least one disjunct is true, but both cannot be true.
4
Consider the case of different predicates
The author adds: 'وإن كان المحمول في الحَمْليات مختلفاً، فالحكم في شروط الإنتاج، وعدد الاقترانات في كل شكل على ما عرف إذا كان المحمول واحداً' — if the predicates in the categorical minors are different, the judgment regarding conditions of production and number of conjunctions remains as known when the predicate is one. So no new rules.
This is a general statement that the previous rules apply irrespective of predicate identity.
5
Apply the principle by analogy
The student is told: 'فعليك بالاعتبار' — it is upon you to consider. You should be able to list the 16 valid conjunctions by taking the 32 from the universal case and reducing them based on the particular affirmative nature of the major.
For example, if a universal major allowed both premises to be universal, the particular major may require at least one particular premise, etc.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كانت المنفصلة جزئية موجبة فقد يأتلف منها مع الحمْليات اقترانات أخرى ستة عشر موازية لهذه الاقترانات إلا أن النتيجة فيها جزئية موجبة منفصلة مانعة الخلو دون الجمع. وأما إن كان المحمول في الحَمْليات مختلفاً، فالحكم في شروط الإنتاج، وعدد الاقترانات في كل شكل على ما عرف إذا كان المحمول واحداً، فعليك بالاعتبار.
And if the disjunctive is particular affirmative, then from it with the categoricals there arise other sixteen conjunctions parallel to these conjunctions, except that the result in them is a particular affirmative exclusive disjunction. And as for if the predicate in the categoricals is different, then the judgement regarding the conditions of production and the number of conjunctions in each figure is as known when the predicate is one; so it is upon you to consider.
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This short passage continues the discussion by considering the case where the disjunctive major is particular affirmative. In this case, there are sixteen conjunctions parallel to the previous ones (the 32 of the universal case), but the conclusion is a particular affirmative exclusive disjunction. The author then makes a general remark: if the predicates in the categorical minors are different from each other, the same rules apply as when they were identical. The student is left to apply the principles.

Key Takeaway
When the disjunctive major is particular affirmative, the number of valid conjunctions drops to 16, the conclusion is a particular affirmative exclusive disjunction, and the rules apply uniformly even if the predicates of the categorical minors differ.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a valid conjunctive syllogism with a particular affirmative disjunctive major and two categorical minors that have different predicates (e.g., one minor about 'created' and another about 'eternal'). State the conclusion as per the rule.
?
Given a universal affirmative disjunctive major (e.g., 'Every body is either composed or simple'), explain how many valid conjunctions exist in the universal case. Then adjust for a particular affirmative major (e.g., 'Some bodies are composed or simple') and specify how the number of conjunctions changes.

The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
Structure of Mixed Syllogisms (Conditionals and Disjunctives)
Author classifies mixed syllogisms and explains conditions for the first case.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify mixed syllogisms according to the position of the disjunctive and the common element.

Core Idea
Mixed syllogisms chain a conditional and a disjunctive; the common term determines the figure.

In this passage, al-Daqqāq begins by mapping out the possible structures when a conditional and a disjunctive are combined. He shows that the disjunctive can be the minor or major premise, and the shared term can be the disjunctive itself, the consequent, or the antecedent of the conditional. Understanding this classification is the first step before applying validity conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
Locate which premise is the conditional (muttaṣila) and which is the disjunctive (munfaṣila). In the text, the author says 'the disjunctive and the conditional are either in the position of the minor or the major'.
2
Determine which is minor
The minor premise is the one that contains the subject of the conclusion. For now, note that the author first treats the case where the disjunctive is minor.
3
Find the common element (al-ishtirāk)
The common element is the term that appears in both premises. It can be the entire disjunctive, the consequent (tālī), or the antecedent (muqaddam) of the conditional.
4
Trace the subcase
If the common element is the consequent, then it can be either the entire consequent or part of it. The author focuses on the case where the consequent is made a part of the disjunction.
5
Recognize the classification scheme
Use the author's breakdown to classify any given mixed syllogism into one of these categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Chapter Five: On the Syllogisms Composed of Conditionals and Disjunctives. The disjunctive and the conditional are either in the position of the minor or the major. If it is in the position of the minor, then the common element is either the disjunctive, or the consequent of the conditional, or its antecedent. If the common element is with the consequent of the conditional, then the commonality between them is either by the consequent or by a part of it. If the commonality is by the consequent, then the commonality is either by making it a part of the parts of the disjunction or by making it its subject. If it is the first, then its condition for production is that the disjunctive be a real affirmative, exclusive of conjunction but not of emptiness, and that one of the two propositions be universal. The condition of affirming the disjunctive is because it is possible that the consequent is more general than two things, one of which is separate from the antecedent, as if you say: 'Everything that is such-and-such, if it is a human, it is an animal; and not at all is it either an animal or a horse.' And the second is concomitant to it, as if you replaced 'horse' with 'rational'. Thus, with negation, neither opposition nor implication follows, contrary to affirmation. And the condition of preventing conjunction is because if the disjunctive prevents conjunction but not emptiness, then the prevention of all does not follow, as if we say: 'If such-and-such does not blur vision, it is not white; and either not white or not black.' Then the prevention of conjunction between 'not black' and 'does not blur vision' does not follow, and the prevention of emptiness does not follow, as if we say: 'Everything that is such-and-such, if it is not black, it is not white' and 'Always either not white or not yellow.' Then emptiness is not impossible for not black and yellow. And the condition of universality of one of them is because two particulars may differ in the time of judgment, so no opposition or implication. And its productive combinations are six, and the conclusion in all is a universal affirmative conditional: The first combination is from two universal affirmatives, as our saying: (Every B is C) and (Always either E is R or D). So (Every B is C implies that it is not D of A) because (E is R) is concomitant with (B is C) and it is opposed to (D is A). The second combination is from a universal affirmative conditional and a particular affirmative disjunctive, as our saying: (Every
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This passage defines the conditions for productive mixed syllogisms combining conditional (muttaṣil) and disjunctive (munfaṣil) premises. The author first classifies possible positions: the disjunctive may be minor or major. When disjunctive is minor, the common term (middle) can be the disjunctive itself, the consequent (tālī) of the conditional, or its antecedent (muqaddam). The focus is on the case where the common term is the consequent, and the disjunctive is included as a part of that consequent. The condition for productivity is that the disjunctive be a real affirmative exclusive disjunction (māni‘at al-jam‘ dūn al-khuluw, i.e., allowing both false but not both true) and that one premise be universal. The author explains why the disjunctive must be affirmative: if negative, the fallacy of undistributed middle can occur. He illustrates with an Arabic example using letters: 'Every B is C' (conditional) and 'Always either E is R or D' (disjunctive). The conclusion is a conditional: 'Every B is C implies that it is not D of A'. The second combination uses a particular instead of universal disjunctive. The author also notes that there are six productive combinations in this scenario, all yielding a universal affirmative conditional conclusion. The commentary follows the author's enumeration: first combination (two universal affirmative premises) and second (universal affirmative conditional with particular affirmative disjunctive). These examples model how to apply the conditions in syllogistic reasoning, e.g., in kalām arguments about divine attributes where conditionals and disjunctives are common.

Key Takeaway
Mixed syllogisms are classified by the position of the disjunctive and the nature of the common term; this determines which validity rules apply.

The Syllogism

5 min
Three Essential Conditions for Validity in Mixed Syllogisms (First Case)
Author classifies mixed syllogisms and explains conditions for the first case.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to check whether a given mixed syllogism of the first case satisfies the three conditions for a valid conclusion.

Core Idea
The disjunctive must be affirmative, exclusive, and one premise must be universal – otherwise the syllogism fails.

After classifying the first case, the author lays down three conditions for it to produce a valid conclusion: (1) the disjunctive must be affirmative (real affirmative), (2) it must be exclusive (māni‘at al-jam‘ dūn al-khuluw), and (3) one of the two premises must be universal. He explains each condition with examples showing what goes wrong if it is not met. You will apply these conditions to test validity.

Guided Path
1
Check the disjunctive's quality
It must be affirmative (mūjaba). The author warns that if it is negative, no definite relationship (opposition or implication) follows. Look for negation in the disjunctive.
2
Check if the disjunctive is exclusive (māni‘at al-jam‘ dūn al-khuluw)
That is, it prevents both parts from being true together but allows both to be false. The author gives an example: if it were māni‘at al-khuluw (prevents both false), the conclusion would not follow.
3
Check universality of at least one premise
Both premises cannot be particular, because the time of judgment might differ. One must be universal (either the conditional or the disjunctive).
4
Apply to an example
Take a valid example from the text (e.g., the first combination) and verify each condition. Then try an invalid one (e.g., a negative disjunctive) and see how it fails.
5
State the reason for each condition
Understand why each is necessary, as the author explains.

Source Text

The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
The First Two Productive Combinations (First Case)
Author classifies mixed syllogisms and explains conditions for the first case.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the conclusion for the first two combinations of mixed syllogisms where the disjunctive is minor and the common term is the consequent.

Core Idea
The first combination uses two universal affirmatives; the second uses a universal affirmative conditional and a particular affirmative disjunctive.

The author enumerates six productive combinations for the first case, giving the first two in detail. The first is from two universal affirmatives (a universal conditional and a universal disjunctive). The second substitutes a particular disjunctive for the universal one. In both, the conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional: 'Every such-and-such implies that something is not the case.' We will work through these two combinations and see how to derive the conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first combination
Two universal affirmatives: the conditional is universal (e.g., 'Every B is C') and the disjunctive is universal (e.g., 'Always either E is R or D').
2
State the condition of exclusivity
The disjunctive must be exclusive (māni‘at al-jam‘ dūn al-khuluw).
3
Derive the conclusion
Since the conditional says B implies C, and the disjunctive says always either E is R or D, and E is R is concomitant with B is C (as per the text), then B being C implies that D is not A (because E is R opposes D is A). So conclusion: 'Every B is C implies that it is not D of A' – a conditional statement.
4
Second combination
Keep the conditional universal, but the disjunctive is particular (e.g., 'Sometimes either E is R or D'). The conclusion remains a universal affirmative conditional.
5
Apply to an Islamic example
For the first combination, use: 'If an act is commanded by Allah, then it is good' (universal conditional) and 'Either the act is good or it is evil' (universal exclusive disjunctive). Conclusion: 'If an act is commanded by Allah, then it is not evil.'

Commentary
This passage defines the conditions for productive mixed syllogisms combining conditional (muttaṣil) and disjunctive (munfaṣil) premises. The author first classifies possible positions: the disjunctive may be minor or major. When disjunctive is minor, the common term (middle) can be the disjunctive itself, the consequent (tālī) of the conditional, or its antecedent (muqaddam). The focus is on the case where the common term is the consequent, and the disjunctive is included as a part of that consequent. The condition for productivity is that the disjunctive be a real affirmative exclusive disjunction (māni‘at al-jam‘ dūn al-khuluw, i.e., allowing both false but not both true) and that one premise be universal. The author explains why the disjunctive must be affirmative: if negative, the fallacy of undistributed middle can occur. He illustrates with an Arabic example using letters: 'Every B is C' (conditional) and 'Always either E is R or D' (disjunctive). The conclusion is a conditional: 'Every B is C implies that it is not D of A'. The second combination uses a particular instead of universal disjunctive. The author also notes that there are six productive combinations in this scenario, all yielding a universal affirmative conditional conclusion. The commentary follows the author's enumeration: first combination (two universal affirmative premises) and second (universal affirmative conditional with particular affirmative disjunctive). These examples model how to apply the conditions in syllogistic reasoning, e.g., in kalām arguments about divine attributes where conditionals and disjunctives are common.

اقتران ثان
كليتان موجبتان
كلية موجبة متصلة وجزئية موجبة منفصلة

C) One universal affirmative, one particular affirmative
D) One universal, one particular both negatives

Key Takeaway
The first two productive combinations both yield a universal affirmative conditional conclusion, even if the disjunctive is particular.

The Syllogism
intermediate
7 min
Conditions for Mixed Syllogisms: Consequent as Part of Disjunction
Conditions for mixed syllogisms when common term is the consequent part of the disjunction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three conditions for a valid mixed syllogism where the common term is the consequent part of the disjunction, and evaluate whether a given mixed syllogism meets them.

Core Idea
The disjunctive must be affirmative, exclusive of combination, and one premise must be universal.

Mixed syllogisms combine conditional and disjunctive premises. The author focuses on the case where the consequent of the conditional is a part of the disjunctive premise. This lesson breaks down the three necessary conditions for such a syllogism to be productive, with rationales and counterexamples. Mastering these conditions prevents logical errors in complex arguments used in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the position of the premises
In the mixed syllogism, the disjunctive and conditional can be minor or major. Here, the focus is when the disjunctive is the minor premise and the common term is the consequent of the conditional.
2
Specify the type of commonality
When the common term is the consequent, it can be either a part of the disjunction or the subject of the disjunction. This lesson considers the first case: the consequent is a part of the disjunction.
3
Extract the three conditions
The author states: (1) the disjunctive must be affirmative and true (موجبة حقيقية), (2) it must be exclusive of combination (مانعة الجمع) but not of emptiness, (3) at least one premise must be universal (كلّية).
4
Understand the reason for each condition
The author provides counterexamples. For condition (1): if the disjunctive is negative, the conclusion may not follow. For condition (2): if the disjunctive is exclusive of emptiness only, then exclusion of combination does not follow. For condition (3): two particular premises may have different time references, so no fixed relation.

الفصل الخامس في الاقترانات الكائنة من المتصلات والمنفصلات. المنفصلة والمتصلة إما مكان الصغرى أو الكبرى، فإن كانت مكان الصغرى فالشركة إما المنفصلة أو التالي من المتصلة أو المقدم منها، فإن كان الاشتراك والتالي من المتصلة فإما أن يكون الاشتراك بينهما بالتالي أو بجزء منه، فإن كان الاشتراك بالتالي فإما أن يكون الاشتراك بجعله جزءاً من أجزاء الانفصال أو بجعله موضوعاً لها، فإن كان الأول فشرطه في الإنتاج أن تكون المنفصلة موجبة حقيقية مانعة الجمع دون الخلو وأن تكون إحدى القضيتين كلية. وإما بشرط إيجاب المنفصلة؛ فلأنه من الجائز أن يكون التالي أعم من شيئين وأحدهما مباين للمقدم، كما لو قلت كل ما كان كذا إنساناً فهو حيوان وليس البتة إما أن يكون حيواناً وإما فرساً، والثاني ملازم له كما لو بدلت الفرس بالناطق، فلا يلزم مع السلب عناد ولا لزوم، بخلاف الإيجاب. وإما شرط المنع من الجمع؛ لأن المنفصلة إذا كانت مانعة الخلو دون الجمع لم يلزم المنع من الجميع، كما لو قلنا إن كان كذا غير مفرق للبصر فليس بأبيض وإما لا أبيض وإما لا أسود فإنه لا يلزم المنع من الجمع بين لا أسود ولا مفرق للبصر، ولا يلزم المنع من الخلو كما لو قلنا كل ما كان كذا لا أسود فليس بأبيض ودائماً إما لا أبيض وإما لا أصفر فإنه لا يمتنع الخلو لا أسود. وإما اشتراط كليّة أحدهما؛ لأن الجزئيتين قد يختلف وقت الحكم فيهما فلا عناد ولا لزوم. وقرائنه المنتجة ستة، والنتيجة في الكل متصلة موجبة. الاقتران الأول من كليتين موجبتين كقولنا: كل ما كان "ب" "ج" ودائماً إما "هـ" "ر" وإما "د" "أ" فـ كل ما كان "ب" "ج" فلا يكون "د" "أ" لأن "هـ" "ر" ملازم لـ "ب" "ج" وهو معاند لـ "د" "أ". الاقتران الثاني من كليّة موجبة متصلة وجزئية موجبة منفصلة، كقولنا: كل ما كان "ب" "ج" فـ "هـ" "ر" وقد يكون إما "هـ" "ر" وإما "د" "أ" ونتيجته جزئية موجبة متصلة، وهي أنه قد يكون إذا كان "ب" "ج" فلا يكون "د" "أ". الاقتران الثالث من جزئية موجبة متصلة وكليّة منفصلة، كقولنا: قد يكون إذا كان "ب" "ج" و "هـ" "ر" ودائماً إما "هـ" "ر" وإما "د" "أ" ونتيجته كالتي قبله. الاقتران الرابع من كلية سالبة متصلة وكلية موجبة منفصلة، كقولنا: ليس البتة إذا كان "ب" "ج" فـ "هـ" "ر" ودائماً إما "هـ" "ر" وإما "د" "أ" فـ كل ما كان "ب" "ج" فـ "د" "أ" لأنه يلزم من "ب" "ج" أن لا يكون "هـ" "ر"
