وكلية موجبة منفصلة، ونتيجته كالتي قبله. وقس على هذا ما إذا كانت أجزاء الانفصال سالبة أو مختلفة الكيفية، وكون التالي سالباً أو موجباً.
Chapter Five on the composite syllogisms composed of conditionals and disjunctives. The disjunctive and the conditional can be either as minor or major premise. If it is as minor, then the common element is either the disjunctive or the consequent of the conditional or its antecedent. If the common element and the consequent of the conditional, then the commonality is either with the consequent or with a part of it. If the commonality is with the consequent, then it is either by making it a part of the parts of the disjunction or by making it a subject for them. If the first, then its condition for production is that the disjunctive be a true affirmative exclusive of combination but not of emptiness, and that one of the two propositions be universal. As for the condition of affirming the disjunctive: because it is possible that the consequent is more general than two things and one of them is contrary to the antecedent, as if you say: Everything that is such and such a human is an animal, and absolutely not either it is an animal or a horse, and the second is concomitant to it as if you replaced the horse with the rational; so with negation there is neither contrariety nor concomitance, unlike affirmation. As for the condition of excluding combination: because if the disjunctive is exclusive of emptiness but not of combination, then exclusion of combination does not follow, as if we said: If such is not sight-dimming then it is not white, and either it is not white or it is not black, then exclusion of combination between not-black and not-sight-dimming does not follow; and exclusion of emptiness does not follow as if we said: Everything that is such and such not-black is not white, and always either not-white or not-yellow, then emptiness of not-black is not impossible. As for the condition of universality of one of them: because two particular propositions may differ in time of judgment, so there is neither contrariety nor concomitance. Its productive combinations are six, and the result in all is a conditional affirmative. The first combination from two affirmatives universal, as we say: Everything that is B is J, and always either H is R or D is A, so everything that is B is J is not D is A, because H is R is concomitant to B is J and it is contrariant to D is A. The second combination from a universal affirmative conditional and a particular affirmative disjunctive, as we say: Everything that is B is J is H is R, and sometimes either H is R or D is A, and its result is a conditional particular affirmative, namely that sometimes if B is J then it is not D is A. The third combination from a particular affirmative conditional and a universal disjunctive, as we say: Sometimes if B is J then H is R, and always either H is R or D is A, and its result is like the previous. The fourth combination from a universal negative conditional and a universal affirmative disjunctive, as we say: Absolutely not if B is J then H is R, and always either H is R or D is A, so everything that is B is J is D is A, because from B is J it follows that H is R is not, and if H is R is not, then D is A, due to the necessity of non-emptiness from the two. The fifth combination is the same, except that the disjunctive is particular affirmative, and its result is: if B is J then D is A. The sixth combination from a particular negative conditional and a universal affirmative disjunctive, and its result is like the previous. And analogize to this when the parts of the disjunction are negative or of different quality, and when the consequent is negative or affirmative.
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage defines and enumerates the six productive forms of mixed syllogisms composed of a conditional and a disjunctive premise, where the common element (the term shared between them) is the consequent of the conditional and also a part of the disjunction. The author begins by categorizing the possible positions of the disjunctive and conditional (as minor or major), then focuses on the specific case where the common element is the consequent. Two subcases arise: either the consequent is a part of the disjunction's parts, or it is the subject of the disjunction. The passage covers the first subcase. The conditions for validity are: the disjunctive must be a true affirmative (to avoid cases where the consequent may be more general and only one part contrary), it must be exclusive of combination (مانعة الجمع) but not of emptiness (مانعة الخلو), and one of the premises must be universal (to ensure uniformity of time of judgment). The author provides examples to illustrate why these conditions are necessary. For instance, if the disjunctive were not exclusive of combination, the conclusion would not necessarily exclude both parts. Then the six productive combinations are enumerated, each with a specific pattern of quantifiers and qualities (universal/particular, affirmative/negative). The conclusion in all cases is a conditional affirmative proposition. The first combination uses two universals affirmative; the second uses a universal conditional and particular disjunctive; the third uses a particular conditional and universal disjunctive; the fourth uses a universal negative conditional and universal affirmative disjunctive; the fifth is like the fourth but with a particular disjunctive; the sixth uses a particular negative conditional and universal affirmative disjunctive. The author ends by noting that the same scheme applies when the parts of the disjunction are negative or of mixed quality, and when the consequent is negative or affirmative. This is a typical expansion (sharh) of the compressed rules found in logical texts like al-Sullam or Isaghuji.

المتصلة
التالي
مانعة الجمع
مانعة الخلو

D) A hypothetical proposition

Key Takeaway
For a productive mixed syllogism, the disjunctive must be affirmative, exclusive of combination, and at least one premise universal.

The Six Productive Forms of Mixed Syllogisms
Enumeration of six productive mixed syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the six productive combinations of mixed syllogisms (when the common term is the consequent part of the disjunction) and identify which combination a given argument belongs to.

Core Idea
Six patterns of quantifier and quality pairs yield a conditional affirmative conclusion.

This lesson builds on the conditions by presenting the six specific forms of mixed syllogisms that yield a valid conclusion. Each form is defined by the quantifiers and qualities of the conditional and disjunctive premises. Understanding these patterns allows you to quickly assess the validity of such arguments in Islamic logical discourse.

Guided Path
1
Recall the three conditions
Briefly remember that the disjunctive must be affirmative, exclusive of combination, and at least one premise universal. These conditions apply to all six forms.
2
Identify the six combinations from the text
The author lists six pairs: (1) universal affirmative conditional + universal affirmative disjunctive, (2) universal affirmative conditional + particular affirmative disjunctive, (3) particular affirmative conditional + universal affirmative disjunctive, (4) universal negative conditional + universal affirmative disjunctive, (5) universal negative conditional + particular affirmative disjunctive, (6) particular negative conditional + universal affirmative disjunctive.
3
Observe the conclusion pattern
The conclusion in all six forms is a conditional affirmative. Its quantifier matches the least universal premise: if both are universal, the conclusion is universal; if one is particular, the conclusion is particular.
4
Apply the pattern to new examples
Choose a fiqh or kalam topic and construct a valid mixed syllogism following one of the six forms. For example, use 'Every created thing is temporally originated' and 'Always either it is temporally originated or it is eternal' to form a first-form argument.

Identify which of the six productive forms the following argument belongs to: 'Sometimes if the world is created then it is temporally originated, and always either it is temporally originated or it is eternal.' Then determine the conclusion.

Mixed Syllogisms: Consequent as Subject of Disjunction
Author gives conditions and first two productive mixed syllogisms when the common term is the consequent serving as subject of the disjunction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three conditions for a valid mixed syllogism (consequent as subject of disjunction) and construct the first two productive combinations.

Core Idea
When the consequent becomes the subject of the disjunction, three conditions guarantee a valid inference.

In the previous lesson, we saw mixed syllogisms where a part of the disjunction is the common term. Here, the common term is the entire consequent of the conditional, serving as the subject of the disjunctive premise. The author outlines strict conditions to ensure the middle term unifies the premises, and then gives the first two of six productive combinations. Understanding these conditions prevents invalid inferences when combining conditional and disjunctive propositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of mixed syllogism
Recognize that the common term between the conditional and disjunctive premises is the entire consequent (tālī) of the conditional, not just a part of the disjunction. The disjunction predicates of this consequent.
In the source, this is described as 'the consequent being a subject for the parts of the disjunction'.
2
Check the three conditions for validity
The author gives three conditions: (1) The conditional must be affirmative; otherwise, the consequent is contrary to the antecedent and judgment on one contrary does not transfer. (2) The disjunctive must apply to the whole consequent universally (every consequent is R). (3) One of the two premises must be universal. Ensure all three are satisfied.
If any condition is violated, no conclusion follows.
3
Analyse the first productive combination
The first combination uses two universal affirmative premises. Example: 'Every B is J is H is R' and 'Every H is R is either D or A' yields 'Every B is J is either D or A'. Notice the conclusion is also universal affirmative.
This is analogous to a first-figure categorical syllogism but with compound propositions.
4
Analyse the second productive combination
The second combination uses two universal premises but the disjunctive is negative. Example: 'Every B is J is H is R' and 'Absolutely not every H is R is D or A' yields 'Absolutely not if B is J is D or A'. The conclusion is universal negative.
The negation in the disjunctive premise leads to a negative conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كان الاشتراك بكون التالي موضوعاً لأجزاء الانفصال فشرطه إيجاب المتصلة، وأن يكون الحكم في المنفصلة على كل التالي، وأن تكون إحدى المقدمتين كليّة. أما شرط إيجاب المتصلة؛ لأنها لو كانت سالبة فالتالي يكون مبايناً للمقدم والحكم على أحد المتباينين لا يكون حكماً على الآخر، لا بنفي ولا بإثبات. وأما شرط كلية الموضوع في المنفصلة؛ ليتحد الحد الأوسط. وأما اشتراط كليّة إحدى المقدمتين؛ فحتى لا يختلف وقت الحكم. وقرائنه المنتجة ستة: الاقتران الأول من كليتين موجبتين، كقولنا: كل ما كان "ب" "ج" فـ "هـ" "ر" ودائماً كل "هـ" "ر" إما "د" وإما "أ" فـ كل ما كان "ب" "ج" فإما "د" وإما "أ". الاقتران الثاني من كليتين والمنفصلة سالبة، كقولنا: كل ما كان "ب" "ج" فـ "هـ" "ر" وليس البتة إما أن تكون كل "هـ" "ر" إما "د" وإما "أ" فـ ليس البتة إذا كان "ب" "ج" إما "د" وإما "أ".
And if the commonality is by the consequent being a subject for the parts of the disjunction, then its condition for production is that the conditional be affirmative, and that the judgment in the disjunctive be upon the whole consequent, and that one of the two premises be universal. As for the condition of the conditional being affirmative: because if it were negative, the consequent would be contrary to the antecedent, and the judgment upon one of two contraries is not a judgment upon the other, neither by negation nor by affirmation. As for the condition of the universality of the subject in the disjunctive: to unify the middle term. As for the condition of universality of one of the two premises: so that the time of judgment does not differ. Its productive combinations are six: the first combination from two affirmatives universal, as we say: Everything that is B is J is H is R, and always every H is R is either D or A, so everything that is B is J is either D or A. The second combination from two universals and the disjunctive is negative, as we say: Everything that is B is J is H is R, and absolutely not either every H is R is D or A, so absolutely not if B is J is D or A.
Source pages p. 393

Commentary
This passage covers the second subcase of mixed syllogisms where the common element is not a part of the disjunction but rather the entire consequent of the conditional serves as the subject of the disjunctive premise. The author lays out three conditions: (1) the conditional must be affirmative; if it were negative, the consequent would be contrary to the antecedent, and a judgment on one contrary cannot be transferred to the other. (2) The disjunctive must apply to the whole consequent (i.e., the subject of the disjunction is the entire consequent), which ensures the middle term is unified. (3) One of the premises must be universal, again to avoid differing times of judgment. The author then begins enumerating the six productive combinations, but only the first two are given in this window. The first combination uses two universal affirmative premises, yielding a universal conclusion. The second combination uses two universal premises but the disjunctive is negative, yielding a universal negative conclusion. The examples use the same placeholder variables (ب, ج, هـ, ر, د, أ). The pattern is analogous to the first case but with the key difference in how the common term functions. This subcase is less common but still part of the complete theory of compound syllogisms.

Key Terms
الحد الأوسط
موضوعاً لأجزاء الانفصال
كليّة الموضوع

Checkpoint
In the second type of mixed syllogism (consequent as subject of disjunction), why must the conditional be affirmative?
A) Because a negative conditional has no consequent.
B) Because the consequent would be contrary to the antecedent, so a judgment on one does not transfer to the other.
C) Because an affirmative conditional is always stronger.
D) Because the disjunction must be affirmative.

Key Takeaway
In mixed syllogisms where the consequent is the subject of the disjunction, the conditional must be affirmative, the disjunction universal over the consequent, and at least one premise universal, to ensure a valid conclusion.

First Figure Conditions in Compound Syllogisms
Author describes the first figure of compound syllogisms when the middle term is the predicate of the consequent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the first figure of a compound syllogism when the shared part is the predicate of the consequent, and list its three necessary conditions.

Core Idea
Valid compound syllogisms require universality, full disjunctive coverage, and matched quality.

In this passage, the author details the precise conditions under which a compound syllogism belongs to the first figure. Understanding these conditions is crucial for constructing valid inferences in Islamic logical works, such as those found in Kalam and Usul al-Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Locate the shared part
Identify the element common to both premises in the consequent of the conjunctive. It must be a part of the disjunctive's subject or predicate.
In this figure the shared part is the predicate in the consequent.
2
Verify the figure designation
Check that the shared part is a subject for the disjunctive parts and is the predicate in the consequent. This arrangement defines the first figure.
3
Check condition 1: universality
One of the two premises must be universal so that the time of judgment does not differ, allowing a consistent ruling.
If both are particular, the conclusion may vary.
4
Check condition 2: full coverage of subject
The disjunctive parts (أجزاء الانفصال) must relate to all parts of the subject (أجزاء الموضوع) to realize genuine sharing in the middle term.
5
Check condition 3: quality matching
The consequent of the conjunctive must be affirmative if the conjunctive is affirmative, and negative if the conjunctive is negative.
This ensures consistency in the inference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كان الاشتراك في جزء التالي، فذلك الجزء إما أن يكون موضوعاً لأجزاء الانفصال أو محمولاً فيها، فإن كان موضوعاً لأجزاء الانفصال فإما أن يكون هو المحمول أو الموضوع في التالي فإن كان هو المحمول في التالي فهو على هيئة الشكل الأول، وله شروط وخواص، أما شروطه فإن تكون إحدى المقدمتين كلية؛ حتى لا يختلف وقت الحكم، وأن تكون نسبة أجزاء الانفصال في إلى كل أجزاء الموضوع لها؛ لتحقق الاشتراك في الحد الأوسط، وأن يكون التالي موجباً إن كانت المتصلة موجبة وسالباً إن كانت المتصلة سالبة، وقرائنه المنتجة أربعة وعشرون اقتراناً. وأما خواصه، فهو أن النتيجة فيه متصلة مقدمها مقدم المتصلة وتاليها نتيجة اقتران التالي بالمنفصلة لو انفردا. الاقتران الأول من كليتين موجبتين والتالي كلي موجب، كقولنا كل ما كان "ج" فكل "د" "ر") و(كل "ر" دائماً إما "هـ" وإما "أ") فـ (كل ما كان "ب" "ج" فكل "د" دائماً إما "هـ" وإما "أ"). الاقتران الثاني: الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن المتصلة كلية سالبة، كقولنا: (كل ما كان "ب" "ج" فكل "د" "ر") و(دائماً كل "ر" ليس إما "هـ" وإما "أ") فـ (كل ما كان "ب" "ج") فـ (كل "د" دائماً إما "هـ" وإما "أ"). الاقتران الثالث الاقتران بحاله إلا أن المنفصلة جزئية موجبة كقولنا كل ما كان "ب" "ج" فكل "د" "ر") و(قد يكون كل "ر" إما "هـ" وإما "أ") ونتيجته كل ما كان "ب" "ج" فقد يكون كل "د" إما "هـ" وإما "أ"). الاقتران الرابع الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن المنفصلة جزئية سالبة، ونتيجته كل ما كان "ب" "ج" فكل "د" ليس دائماً إما "هـ" وإما "أ"). المنفصلة وإن كان التالي من المنفصلة جزئياً موجباً فقد يأتلف منها مع المنفصلة اقترانات أخرى أربعة موازية لهذه الأربعة، إلا أن الموضوع في تالي المنتجة لا يكون إلا جزئياً، وعلى هذا فلا يخفى الإنتاج إذا كانت المتصلة جزئية موجبة، أو كلية سالبة، أو جزئية سالبة، وهي ستة عشر اقتراناً أخرى، فاعتبرها، وحكم النتيجة في الإطلاق والجهة إما بالنظر إلى لزوم تاليها لمقدمها فكحكم المتصلة، وإما بالنظر إلى لزوم محمول تاليها لموضوعه فكحكم المنفصلة.
And if the sharing is in the part of the consequent, that part is either a subject for the parts of the disjunctive or a predicate in them. If it is a subject for the parts of the disjunctive, then it is either the predicate or the subject in the consequent. If it is the predicate in the consequent, then it is in the form of the first figure, and it has conditions and properties. As for its conditions: that one of the two premises be universal so that the time of judgment does not differ; that the ratio of the parts of the disjunctive be to all the parts of the subject so that the share in the middle term is realized; and that the consequent be affirmative if the conjunctive is affirmative and negative if the conjunctive is negative. Its productive pairings are twenty-four pairings. As for its properties: that the conclusion in it is conjunctive, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conjunctive, and its consequent is the result of pairing the consequent with the disjunctive if they were alone. The first pairing: from two universal affirmatives and the consequent universal affirmative, as when we say: 'Every B is C so every D is R' and 'Every R is always either H or A' so 'Every B is C so every D is always either H or A'. The second pairing: the pairing in its state except that the conjunctive is universal negative, as when we say: 'Every B is C so every D is R' and 'Always every R is not either H or A' so 'Every B is C so every D is always either H or A'. The third pairing: the pairing in its state except that the disjunctive is particular affirmative, as when we say: 'Every B is C so every D is R' and 'Every R may be either H or A' and its conclusion: 'Every B is C so every D may be either H or A'. The fourth pairing: the pairing in its state except that the disjunctive is particular negative, and its conclusion: 'Every B is C so every D is not always either H or A'. The disjunctive: and if the consequent of the disjunctive is particular affirmative, then there may be composed from it with the disjunctive four other pairings parallel to these four, except that the subject in the consequent of the conclusion is only particular. And thus the production is not obscure if the conjunctive is particular affirmative, or universal negative, or particular negative, and they are sixteen other pairings. So consider them. And the ruling of the conclusion in absoluteness and modality is either with respect to the entailment of its consequent for its antecedent, so it is like the ruling of the conjunctive, or with respect to the entailment of the predicate of its consequent for its subject, so it is like the ruling of the disjunctive.
Source pages p. 394

Commentary
This passage is a systematic exposition of the first figure of compound syllogisms (قياسات اقترانية) where the shared part (الجزء المشترك) occurs in the consequent of the conjunctive premise and serves as the predicate. The author first clarifies the scenario: the shared part is a subject for the parts of the disjunctive, and it is the predicate in the consequent. This arrangement yields the first figure (الشكل الأول). The author then lists three conditions for valid inference: (1) one premise must be universal to ensure uniformity in time of judgment; (2) the disjunctive parts must relate to all parts of the subject to ensure a true middle term; (3) the consequent must match the conjunctive in quality (affirmative/negative). There are 24 productive pairings (اقتران) under these conditions. A key property is that the conclusion is a conditional (متصل) whose antecedent comes from the conjunctive's antecedent, and whose consequent is the result of pairing the original consequent with the disjunctive premise. Four sample pairings are given: (i) both universal affirmative with universal affirmative consequent; (ii) universal conjunctive negative; (iii) disjunctive particular affirmative; (iv) disjunctive particular negative. The author then notes an additional set of four pairings when the disjunctive's consequent is particular affirmative, and then enumerates a further sixteen pairings covering all combinations of conjunctive and disjunctive quantities and qualities. The ruling of the conclusion's modality depends on whether we consider the entailment of the consequent by the antecedent (like a conjunctive) or the predicate entailment within the consequent (like a disjunctive). This passage is highly technical and meant for memorization, as it provides the complete enumeration of valid forms.

Key Terms
الشكل الأول
الاقتران
المتصلة
المنفصلة
الحد الأوسط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the first figure of this compound syllogism (when the shared part is the predicate of the consequent), what is one of the necessary conditions for the premises?
A) Both premises must be universal.
B) One premise must be universal to ensure uniformity in time of judgment.
C) The disjunctive premise must be exclusive.
D) The conjunctive premise must be affirmative.

Key Takeaway
The first figure of compound syllogisms is defined by the shared part being the predicate in the consequent and requires one universal premise, full coverage of disjunctive parts, and matching quality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using the first pairing as a model, replace the abstract terms with a real Islamic example: let 'ب' = 'فعل' (act), 'ج' = 'حرام' (haram), 'د' = 'جزاء' (punishment), 'ر' = 'مقرر' (prescribed), 'هـ' = 'حد' (hadd), 'أ' = 'تعزير' (ta'zir). Write the compound syllogism in Arabic.

Enumeration of Valid Pairings in the First Figure
Author describes the first figure of compound syllogisms when the middle term is the predicate of the consequent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the 24 productive pairings of the first figure and identify the pattern of their construction.

Core Idea
The 24 pairings arise from variations in quantity and quality of the conjunctive and disjunctive premises.

After establishing the conditions, the author presents the actual productive pairings (اقترانات) that yield valid conclusions. This lesson guides you through replicating the enumeration yourself, using the patterns described.

Guided Path
1
Start with the baseline pairing
The first pairing is the simplest: both premises universal affirmative. Write it out using the abstract letters: 'كل ب ج فكل د ر' and 'كل ر دائماً إما هـ وإما أ'.
This gives the conclusion 'كل ب ج فكل د دائماً إما هـ وإما أ'.
2
Vary the disjunctive premise
The author gives three more pairings by changing the disjunctive: universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative. Write each variant and its conclusion.
These are the second, third, and fourth pairings.
3
Consider the disjunctive's consequent variation
If the disjunctive's consequent is particular affirmative, there are four parallel pairings, but the subject in the conclusion's consequent is particular.
This adds another four.
4
Vary the conjunctive premise
The author states that when the conjunctive is particular affirmative, universal negative, or particular negative, there are sixteen more pairings. Derive these systematically using the previous patterns.
Total: 4 (first set) + 4 (disjunctive consequent variant) + 16 = 24.
5
Check modality of the conclusion
The conclusion's modality has two aspects: the entailment of its consequent by its antecedent (like a conjunctive) and the entailment of the predicate within the consequent (like a disjunctive).
This does not affect enumeration but helps interpret the conclusion.

And if the sharing is in the part of the consequent, that part is either a subject for the parts of the disjunctive or a predicate in them. If it is a subject for the parts of the disjunctive, then it is either the predicate or the subject in the consequent. If it is the predicate in the consequent, then it is in the form of the first figure, and it has conditions and properties. How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the first pairing and replace the abstract terms with real terms from Islamic law: let 'ب' = 'فعل' (act), 'ج' = 'حرام' (haram), 'د' = 'جزاء' (punishment), 'ر' = 'مقرر' (prescribed), 'هـ' = 'حد' (hadd), 'أ' = 'تعزير' (ta'zir). Write the compound syllogism in Arabic.

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
Basic Conjunctive Mood: Universal Affirmative Conditional with Universal Affirmative Disjunctive
Author enumerates valid moods combining a conditional with a disjunctive proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the first four conjunctive moods from a universal affirmative conditional and a universal affirmative disjunctive.

Core Idea
Combining a conditional with a disjunctive requires verifying the quantity and quality of both premises.

This lesson focuses on the simplest combination: a universal affirmative conditional premise paired with a universal affirmative disjunctive premise. The author uses abstract variables to illustrate four distinct moods, which differ based on whether the parts of the disjunctive are universal or particular. Understanding these foundational moods prepares you for more complex combinations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional premise
Look for a statement of the form 'if B is J, then every D is R'. In the passage, this is represented as 'كل ما كان ب ج فكل د ر'. Note that the antecedent is 'B is J' and the consequent is 'every D is R'.
The conditional is universal affirmative.
2
Identify the disjunctive premise
Find the disjunctive statement, e.g., 'Always every D is either H or A'. The author gives variants: universal affirmative ('دائماً إما كل د هـ وإما أ') and universal negative ('دائماً كل د ليس إما هـ وإما أ'), with universal or particular parts.
The disjunctive can have universal or particular parts, and be affirmative or negative.
3
Recognize the four moods
The passage describes four moods: (1) disjunctive universal affirmative with universal parts, (2) same but with particular parts, (3) disjunctive universal negative with universal parts, (4) same with particular parts. Each yields a corresponding conclusion.
The conclusion is always a conditional, with the same antecedent and a consequent combining the original consequent with the disjunctive.
4
Form the conclusion
For the first mood, the conclusion is 'Every B that is J is such that always every D is either H or A'. In Arabic: 'كل ما كان ب ج فدائماً إما كل د هـ وإما أ'. The consequent is 'always every D is either H or A'.
The conclusion's quality and quantity mirror the premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كان "ب" "ج" فكل "د" "ر") و(قد يكون كل "ر" إما "هـ" وإما "أ") ونتيجته كل ما كان "ب" "ج" فقد يكون كل "د" إما فقد يكون كل "د" إما "هـ" وإما "أ"). الاقتران الرابع الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن المنفصلة جزئية سالبة، ونتيجته كل ما كان "ب" "ج" فكل "د" ليس دائماً إما "هـ" وإما "أ"). المنفصلة وإن كان التالي من المنفصلة جزئياً موجباً فقد يأتلف منها مع المنفصلة اقترانات أخرى أربعة موازية لهذه الأربعة، إلا أن الموضوع في تالي المنتجة لا يكون إلا جزئياً، وعلى هذا فلا يخفى الإنتاج إذا كانت المتصلة جزئية موجبة، أو كلية سالبة، أو جزئية سالبة، وهي ستة عشر اقتراناً أخرى، فاعتبرها، وحكم النتيجة في الإطلاق والجهة إما بالنظر إلى لزوم تاليها لمقدمها فكحكم المتصلة، وإما بالنظر إلى لزوم محمول تاليها لموضوعه فكحكم المنفصلة. وأما إن كان موضوع موضوعاً في تالي المتصلة فهو على هيئة الشكل الثالث، وليكن الفرض إذا كانت أجزاء المنفصلة متحدة الكمية، وشرطه في الإنتاج أن تكون إحداهما كلية حتى لا يختلف وقت الحكم، وإن كانت المتصلة موجبة أن يكون التالي موجباً، وإن كان جزئياً أن تكون أجزاء الانفصال كليّة، وإن كانت أجزاء الانفصال جزئية أن يكون التالي كلياً وإن كانت المتصلة سالبة أن يكون التالي سالباً، وإن كان التالي كلياً سالباً أن تكون أجزاء الانفصال كليّة، وإن كانت أجزاء الانفصال جزئية أن يكون التالي جزئياً سالباً، وقرائنه المنتجة ستة وثلاثون اقتراناً، والنتيجة في الكل متصلة مقدمها مقدم المتصلة وتاليها نتيجة اقتران التالي بالمنفصلة الاقتران الأول من كليتين موجبتين والتالي كلي موجب وأجزاء الانفصال كليّة، كقولنا : كل ما كان "ب" "ج" فكل "د" "ر") و(دائماً إما كل "د" "هـ" وإما "أ") فـ (كل ما كان "ب" "ج") فـ (دائماً بعض "ر" إما "هـ" وإما "أ"). الاقتران الثاني: الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن أجزاء المنفصلة جزئية، كقولنا: ودائماً إما بعض "د" "هـ" وإما "أ") ونتيجته كالتي قبله. الاقتران الثالث المتصلة بحالها، إلا أن المنفصلة كليّة سالبة أجزاؤها كلية كقولنا: ودائماً كل "د" ليس إما "هـ" وإما "أ") ونتيجته (كل ما "ب" "ج" فبعض "ر" دائماً ليس إما "هـ" وإما "أ"). الاقتران الرابع الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن أجزاء المنفصلة جزئية، كقولنا: ودائماً بعض "د" ليس إما "هـ" وإما "أ") ونتيجته كالتي قبله. وإن كانت المنفصلة جزئية موجبة أو جزئية سالبة والمتصلة بحالها، فقد يتألف منها مع المتصلة اقترانات أخرى أربعة موازية لهذه الأربعة، إلا أن النتيجة فيها تكون جزئية متصلة، وعلى هذا فلا يخفى الإنتاج إذا كانت المتصلة كلية سالبة وجزئية موجبة وجزئية سالبة، وهي ثمانية وعشرون اقتراناً أخرى، فاعتبرها، إذ هي سهلة الاعتبار.
[(If) 'B is J, then every D is R' and 'Every R is either H or A', the conclusion is 'Every B that is J is such that every D is either H or A'. The fourth conjunction is the same (except) that the disjunctive is particular negative, and its conclusion is 'Every B that is J is such that every D is not always either H or A'. The disjunctive: If the consequent of the disjunctive is particular affirmative, then with it and the disjunctive there may be composed other four conjunctions analogous to these four, except that the subject in the consequent of the productive (conclusion) is only particular. On this basis, production is not hidden if the conditional is particular affirmative, or universal negative, or particular negative, and they are sixteen other conjunctions—consider them. The ruling of the conclusion in absoluteness and modality is either with respect to the implication of its consequent for its antecedent, like the ruling of the conditional, or with respect to the implication of the predicate of its consequent for its subject, like the ruling of the disjunctive. And if the subject (of the disjunction) is subject in the consequent of the conditional, then it is in the form of the third figure. Let the assumption be that the parts of the disjunctive are uniform in quantity, and its condition for production is that one of them be universal so that the time of judgment does not differ. And if the conditional is affirmative, the consequent must be affirmative; if it is particular, then the parts of the disjunction must be universal; if the parts of the disjunction are particular, then the consequent must be universal; if the conditional is negative, then the consequent must be negative; if the consequent is universal negative, then the parts of the disjunction must be universal; if the parts of the disjunction are particular, then the consequent must be particular negative. And its productive conjunctions are thirty-six conjunctions. The conclusion in all is a conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the disjunctive. The first conjunction from two universal affirmatives with the consequent universal affirmative and the parts of the disjunction universal, like our saying: 'Every B that is J, every D is R' and 'Always every D is either H or A'; so (the conclusion) 'Every B that is J is such that always some R is either H or A'. The second conjunction: the same except that the parts of the disjunctive are particular, like our saying: 'Always some D is either H or A'; its conclusion is like the previous. The third conjunction: the conditional unchanged, but the disjunctive is universal negative and its parts universal, like our saying: 'Always every D is not either H or A'; its conclusion is 'Every B that is J is such that some R is always not either H or A'. The fourth conjunction: the same except that the parts of the disjunctive are particular, like our saying: 'Always some D is not either H or A'; its conclusion is like the previous. And if the disjunctive is particular affirmative or particular negative with the conditional unchanged, then from them with the conditional there may be composed other four conjunctions analogous to these four, except that the conclusion in them is a particular conditional. On this basis, production is not hidden if the conditional is universal negative and particular affirmative and particular negative, and they are twenty-eight other conjunctions—consider them, for they are easy to consider.
Source pages p. 395

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification and rule-set for compound syllogisms that combine a conditional proposition (متصلة) with a disjunctive proposition (منفصلة). The author, likely al-Qutb al-Tahtani in his commentary on Hidayat al-Hikmah, enumerates the valid moods (اقترانات) based on the quantity and quality of the premises. The discussion follows the standard Aristotelian-Avicennan logic but extends it to compound arguments. First, the author explains the basic combination: when the conditional is universal affirmative and the disjunctive is universal affirmative with various parts, yielding a universal affirmative conclusion. Then he treats the case where the disjunctive's consequent is particular affirmative, producing parallel moods but with a particular subject in the conclusion. The total number of moods for these first cases is sixteen. The author then discusses the ruling of the conclusion in terms of absoluteness and modality, distinguishing between the implication of the consequent for the antecedent (like a conditional) and the implication of the predicate of the consequent for its subject (like a disjunctive). Next, the author considers the scenario where the subject of the disjunctive is also the subject in the consequent of the conditional, which places the syllogism in the third figure. Conditions for production are given: one premise must be universal to avoid temporal variation; the consequent must match the conditional in quality; if the parts of the disjunction are particular, the consequent must be universal, etc. This yields thirty-six productive conjunctions, all concluding with a conditional whose antecedent is the original conditional's antecedent and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the disjunctive. Finally, four examples of moods are provided, using the arbitrary variables ب, ج, د, ر, هـ, أ to illustrate the patterns. The passage then notes that if the disjunctive is particular, there are four analogous moods yielding particular conditional conclusions, and if the conditional is universal negative or particular, there are twenty-eight more moods. The author encourages the reader to consider them as they are easy.

Key Terms
متصلة
منفصلة
اقتران
نتاج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the conclusion of the first conjunction (two universal affirmatives with universal parts of disjunction)?
A) Every B that is J is such that always some R is either H or A
B) Every B that is J is such that always every D is either H or A
C) Every B that is J is such that some R is always not either H or A
D) Every B that is J is such that every D is not always either H or A

Key Takeaway
The first four moods combine a universal affirmative conditional with a universal affirmative or negative disjunctive, yielding a conditional conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct the first mood using Islamic variables: conditional = 'If the food is halal, then it is permissible to eat'; disjunctive = 'Always the food is either halal or haram'. Write the conclusion.
?
Identify which of the four moods is used if the disjunctive is 'Always every food is not either halal or haram' (i.e., universal negative) with universal parts.

The Syllogism
advanced

Conditions for Valid Conditional-Disjunctive Syllogisms
Author enumerates conditions for productive conjunctions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the six conditions for a valid conditional-disjunctive syllogism as given in the passage.

Core Idea
Six conditions ensure the syllogism is productive; violating any yields an invalid mood.

Not every combination of a conditional and disjunctive yields a valid conclusion. The author lays down precise conditions on quantity, quality, and the relationship between the consequent and the parts of the disjunction. These rules prevent temporal ambiguity and ensure a necessary conclusion. This lesson extracts and explains each condition.

Guided Path
1
Condition 1: One premise must be universal
The author states 'شرطه في الإنتاج أن تكون إحداهما كلية حتى لا يختلف وقت الحكم' – one of the disjuncts must be universal to avoid differing times of judgment. This ensures the temporal scope is consistent.
Without a universal premise, the conclusion may be contingent.
2
Condition 2: If the conditional is affirmative, its consequent must be affirmative
The passage says: 'وإن كانت المتصلة موجبة أن يكون التالي موجباً'. This maintains logical consistency; an affirmative conditional cannot have a negative consequent.
3
Condition 3: If the consequent is particular, the disjuncts must be universal
If the consequent of the conditional is particular (i.e., 'some D is R'), then the parts of the disjunction must be universal. The text: 'وإن كان جزئياً أن تكون أجزاء الانفصال كليّة'.
4
Condition 4: If the disjuncts are particular, the consequent must be universal
Conversely, if the parts of the disjunction are particular (i.e., 'some D is H or A'), then the consequent of the conditional must be universal. Text: 'وإن كانت أجزاء الانفصال جزئية أن يكون التالي كلياً'.
This balances the scope.
5
Condition 5: If the conditional is negative, its consequent must be negative
Similar to condition 2, but for negative conditionals: 'وإن كانت المتصلة سالبة أن يكون التالي سالباً'.

Source Text

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Determine if the following combination satisfies the conditions: conditional = 'If the prayer is valid, then it is accepted' (affirmative, universal); disjunctive = 'Always the prayer is either valid or invalid' (affirmative, universal parts). Is it productive?
?

The 36 Productive Conjunctions: Patterns and Conclusion Forms
Author enumerates valid moods and the conclusion form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the pattern of the 36 productive conjunctions and describe the form of the conclusion.

Core Idea
The conclusion is always a conditional whose antecedent is the original antecedent and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the original consequent with the disjunctive.

The author systematically lists all productive moods arising from different combinations of the conditional and disjunctive premises. There are 36 such moods, each yielding a conditional conclusion. This lesson maps the overarching structure: the conclusion's antecedent is from the conditional premise, and its consequent is a combination of the original consequent and the disjunctive proposition.

Guided Path
1
Understand the general form of the conclusion
The passage says: 'والنتيجة في الكل متصلة مقدمها مقدم المتصلة وتاليها نتيجة اقتران التالي بالمنفصلة'. This means every conclusion is a conditional, with its antecedent identical to the antecedent of the original conditional premise, and its consequent derived from combining the consequent of the conditional with the disjunctive premise.
Example: If conditional is 'If B is J then every D is R', and disjunctive is 'Every R is either H or A', the conclusion is 'If B is J then (every D is R) combined with (either H or A)'.
2
Recognize how the 36 conjunctions arise
The 36 conjunctions come from varying the quantity (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative) of the conditional (3 possibilities: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative – actually 4, but the passage considers 3? It says 'if the conditional is universal negative and particular affirmative and particular negative' – more likely all four qualities and quantities). The disjunctive also varies in quantity and quality. The conditions narrow the valid combinations to 36.
The passage mentions 'سته وثلاثون اقتراناً' after listing conditions, implying all productive moods.
3
Examine the first four examples
The passage provides explicit examples for the first four moods, showing how the conclusion is constructed. For instance, the first conjunction yields 'كل ما كان ب ج فدائماً بعض ر إما هـ وإما أ'. Notice the antecedent is 'B is J' and the consequent is about 'some R' being either H or A, following the pattern.

سالبة، ونتيجته كل ما كان "ب" "ج" فكل "د" ليس دائماً إما "هـ" وإما "أ"). المنفصلة وإن كان التالي من المنفصلة جزئياً موجباً فقد يأتلف منها مع المنفصلة اقترانات أخرى أربعة موازية لهذه الأربعة، إلا أن أجزاء المنفصلة متحدة الكمية، وشرطه في الإنتاج أن تكون إحداهما كلية حتى لا يختلف وقت الحكم، وإن كانت المتصلة موجبة أن يكون التالي موجباً، وإن كان جزئياً أن تكون أجزاء الانفصال كليّة، وإن كانت أجزاء الانفصال جزئية أن يكون التالي كلياً وإن كانت المتصلة سالبة أن يكون التالي سالباً، وإن كان التالي كلياً سالباً أن تكون أجزاء الانفصال كليّة، وإن كانت أجزاء الانفصال جزئية أن يكون التالي جزئياً سالباً، وقرائنه المنتجة ستة وثلاثون اقتراناً، والنتيجة في الكل متصلة مقدمها مقدم المتصلة وتاليها نتيجة اقتران التالي بالمنفصلة الاقتران الأول من كليتين موجبتين والتالي كلي موجب وأجزاء الانفصال كليّة، كقولنا : كل ما كان "ب" "ج" فكل "د" "ر") و(دائماً إما كل "د" "هـ" وإما "أ") فـ (كل ما كان "ب" "ج") فـ (دائماً بعض "ر" إما "هـ" وإما "أ"). الاقتران الثاني: الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن أجزاء المنفصلة جزئية، كقولنا: ودائماً إما بعض "د" "هـ" وإما "أ") ونتيجته كالتي قبله. الاقتران الثالث المتصلة بحالها، إلا أن المنفصلة كليّة سالبة أجزاؤها كلية كقولنا: ودائماً كل "د" ليس إما "هـ" وإما "أ") ونتيجته (كل ما "ب" "ج" فبعض "ر" دائماً ليس إما "هـ" وإما "أ"). الاقتران الرابع الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن أجزاء المنفصلة جزئية، كقولنا: ودائماً بعض "د" ليس إما "هـ" وإما "أ") ونتيجته كالتي قبله. وإن كانت المنفصلة جزئية موجبة أو جزئية سالبة والمتصلة بحالها، فقد يتألف منها مع المتصلة اقترانات أخرى أربعة موازية لهذه الأربعة، إلا أن النتيجة فيها تكون جزئية متصلة، وعلى هذا فلا يخفى الإنتاج إذا كانت المتصلة كلية سالبة وجزئية موجبة وجزئية سالبة، وهي ثمانية وعشرون اقتراناً أخرى، فاعتبرها، إذ هي سهلة الاعتبار.
[(If) 'B is J, then every D is R' and 'Every R is either H or A', the conclusion is 'Every B that is J is such that every D is either H or A'. The fourth conjunction is the same (except) that the disjunctive is particular negative, and its conclusion is 'Every B that is J is such that every D is not always either H or A'. The disjunctive: If the consequent of the disjunctive is particular affirmative, then with it and the disjunctive there may be composed other four conjunctions analogous to these four, except that the subject in the consequent of the productive (conclusion) is only particular. On this basis, production is not hidden if the conditional is particular affirmative, or universal negative, or particular negative, and they are sixteen other conjunctions—consider them. The ruling of the conclusion in absoluteness and modality is either with respect to the implication of its consequent for its antecedent, like the ruling of the conditional, or with respect to the implication of the predicate of its consequent for its subject, like the ruling of the disjunctive. And if the subject (of the disjunction) is subject in the consequent of the conditional, then it is in the form of the third figure. Let the assumption be that the parts of the disjunctive are uniform in quantity, and its condition for production is that one of them be universal so that the time of judgment does not differ. And if the conditional is affirmative, the consequent must be affirmative; if it is particular, then the parts of the disjunction must be universal; if the parts of the disjunction are particular, then the consequent must be universal; if the conditional is negative, then the consequent must be negative; if the consequent is universal negative, then the parts of the disjunction must be universal; if the parts of the disjunction are particular, then the consequent must be particular negative. And its productive conjunctions are thirty-six conjunctions. The conclusion in all is a conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the disjunctive. The first conjunction from two universal affirmatives with the consequent universal affirmative and the parts of the disjunction universal, like our saying: 'Every B that is J, every D is R' and 'Always every D is either H or A'; so (the conclusion) 'Every B that is J is such that always some R is either H or A'. The second conjunction: the same except that the parts of the disjunctive are particular, like our saying: 'Always some D is either H or A'; its conclusion is like the previous. The third conjunction: the conditional unchanged, but the disjunctive is universal negative and its parts universal, like our saying: 'Always every D is not either H or A'; its conclusion is 'Every B that is J is such that some R is always not either H or A'. The fourth conjunction: the same except that the parts of the disjunctive are particular, like our saying: 'Always some D is not either H or A'; its conclusion is like the previous. And if the disjunctive is particular affirmative or particular negative with the conditional unchanged, then from them with the conditional there may be composed other four conjunctions analogous to these four, except that the conclusion in them is a particular conditional. On this basis, production is not hidden if the conditional is universal negative and particular affirmative and particular negative, and they are twenty-eight other conjunctions—consider them, for they are easy to consider.
Source pages p. 395

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification and rule-set for compound syllogisms that combine a conditional proposition (متصلة) with a disjunctive proposition (منفصلة). The author, likely al-Qutb al-Tahtani in his commentary on Hidayat al-Hikmah, enumerates the valid moods (اقترانات) based on the quantity and quality of the premises. The discussion follows the standard Aristotelian-Avicennan logic but extends it to compound arguments. First, the author explains the basic combination: when the conditional is universal affirmative and the disjunctive is universal affirmative with various parts, yielding a universal affirmative conclusion. Then he treats the case where the disjunctive's consequent is particular affirmative, producing parallel moods but with a particular subject in the conclusion. The total number of moods for these first cases is sixteen. The author then discusses the ruling of the conclusion in terms of absoluteness and modality, distinguishing between the implication of the consequent for the antecedent (like a conditional) and the implication of the predicate of the consequent for its subject (like a disjunctive). Next, the author considers the scenario where the subject of the disjunctive is also the subject in the consequent of the conditional, which places the syllogism in the third figure. Conditions for production are given: one premise must be universal to avoid temporal variation; the consequent must match the conditional in quality; if the parts of the disjunction are particular, the consequent must be universal, etc. This yields thirty-six productive conjunctions, all concluding with a conditional whose antecedent is the original conditional's antecedent and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the disjunctive. Finally, four examples of moods are provided, using the arbitrary variables ب, ج, د, ر, هـ, أ to illustrate the patterns. The passage then notes that if the disjunctive is particular, there are four analogous moods yielding particular conditional conclusions, and if the conditional is universal negative or particular, there are twenty-eight more moods. The author encourages the reader to consider them as they are easy.

Key Takeaway
All 36 productive conjunctions yield a conditional conclusion with a fixed antecedent from the conditional premise.

?

The Syllogism
advanced
8 min
Third Figure in Conditional-Disjunctive Syllogisms
Author introduces third figure scenario and conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a conditional-disjunctive syllogism falls into the third figure and apply the appropriate conditions.

Core Idea
When the subject of the disjunctive is also the subject in the consequent of the conditional, the syllogism takes the third figure, imposing additional conditions.

The passage notes a special case: if the subject of the disjunctive (e.g., 'D') appears as the subject in the consequent of the conditional ('every D is R'), then the syllogism follows the pattern of the third figure of categorical syllogisms. This lesson explores the implications and the specific conditions that apply to this figure.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the third figure scenario
The author says: 'وأما إن كان موضوع موضوعاً في تالي المتصلة فهو على هيئة الشكل الثالث' – if the subject (of the disjunction) is the subject in the consequent of the conditional, then it is in the form of the third figure. For example, the conditional 'If B is J, then every D is R' and the disjunctive 'Every D is either H or A' have 'D' as subject in both the consequent and the disjunct, so it's third figure.
2
Understand the condition of uniform quantity
The passage sets a special assumption: 'وليكن الفرض إذا كانت أجزاء المنفصلة متحدة الكمية' – let the assumption be that the parts of the disjunctive are uniform in quantity. This means the two disjuncts (H and A) have the same quantity (both universal or both particular).
This simplifies the analysis.
3
Apply the condition that one disjunct must be universal
The author repeats: 'شرطه في الإنتاج أن تكون إحداهما كلية حتى لا يختلف وقت الحكم' – one of the disjuncts must be universal to avoid temporal variation. This is a general condition but applies here too.
4
Apply the quality and quantity conditions specific to the third figure
The subsequent conditions (e.g., if conditional is affirmative, consequent affirmative; if consequent particular, disjuncts universal; etc.) are then applied in the context of the third figure. The text does not provide separate rules for the third figure but implies the same conditions hold.

Conditions for Compound Syllogisms with Predicate as Shared Term
Author sets conditions for production when the predicate is the common term in the disjunction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions for a valid compound syllogism when the predicate is the shared term in the disjunction, and apply them to a given example.

Core Idea
Second figure compound syllogism requires one universal premise, a maniat al-khuluw دون الجمع disjunction, and uniform quality.

This passage from Daqaiq al-Haqaiq details the conditions for production when the predicate of the conditional's consequent is the shared term in the disjunction. The author classifies this as a second-figure composite syllogism and imposes specific requirements on the disjunction's type and its parts' quality. Understanding these conditions is essential for constructing valid compound inferences in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the shared term's role
Determine whether the shared term is predicate or subject in the consequent of the conditional. The text specifies: 'إذا كان محمولاً في التالي فهو على هيئة الشكل الثاني' (if it is predicate in the consequent, it is in the form of the second figure).
This determines the figure of the combined syllogism.
2
Recognize the figure and its requirement
When the shared term is predicate in the consequent, the syllogism follows the second figure, which in categorical logic requires one universal premise. The text states: 'وشرطه في الإنتاج كليّة إحدى مقدمتيه' (its condition for production is the universality of one of its two premises).
This condition carries over to the compound form.
3
Specify the disjunction type
The disjunction must be 'حقيقية أو مانعة الخلو دون الجمع' (real or emptiness-excluding without collection-exclusion). This means at least one disjunct must be true, but both can be true together.
مانعة الخلو دون الجمع is required, not exclusive disjunction.
4
Ensure uniformity of quality
The text requires 'أن تكون أجزاؤها متحدة الكيفية' (that its parts be uniform in quality). All disjuncts must be either all affirmative or all negative.
Non-uniform quality would violate the condition.
5
Apply the quality conditions based on the conditional
The author gives four subcases: e.g., if the conditional is affirmative and its consequent affirmative, then the disjuncts must be universal negatives or particular affirmatives. Match the given premise qualities to determine the required disjunct types.
This ensures the conclusion follows validly.

And if (the term) is predicate in the parts of the disjunction, then either it is predicate in the consequent of the conditional, or subject in it. If it is predicate in the consequent, then it is in the form of the second figure, and its condition for production is the universality of one of its two premises, due to what preceded, and the affirmation of the disjunctive, and that it be real or exclusive of emptiness without exclusion, and that its parts be uniform in quality. And if the conditional is affirmative and its consequent affirmative, then the parts of the disjunction must be universal negatives or particular affirmatives. And if (the consequent) is negative, then the parts of the disjunction must be universal affirmatives or particular negatives. And if the conditional is negative and the consequent affirmative, then the parts of the disjunction must be universal affirmatives or particular negatives. And if (the consequent) is negative, then the parts of the disjunction must be universal negatives or particular affirmatives. And its productive conjunctions are forty-eight conjunctions. The conclusion in all is a conditional, its antecedent the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent the result of conjoining the consequent with the disjunctive. The first conjunction from two universal affirmatives with the consequent affirmative and the parts of the disjunction universal negatives, like our saying: 'Every B that is J is always D,' and 'Always either every R or every H,' and its conclusion: 'Every B that is J is such that always either nothing of D is A or nothing of D is H'—i.e., the situation is not empty of them. The second conjunction: the same except that the parts of the disjunction are particular affirmatives, like our saying:
Source pages p. 396

Commentary
This passage continues the classification of compound syllogisms, now focusing on the case where the shared term (the middle term) is a predicate in the parts of the disjunction. The author specifies that this term can be either a predicate or a subject in the consequent of the conditional. When it is a predicate in the consequent, the syllogism takes the form of the second figure (الشكل الثاني), which typically requires one universal premise and specific conditions: the disjunctive must be real or exclusive of emptiness (مانعة الخلو) but not exclusive of togetherness (دون الجمع), and its parts must be uniform in quality. The author then details four subcases based on the quality of the conditional and its consequent, each requiring specific types for the parts of the disjunction (universal/particular, affirmative/negative). In total, forty-eight productive conjunctions are counted. The conclusion in all cases is a conditional proposition whose antecedent is the antecedent of the original conditional and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the disjunctive. An example is given for the first mood: from 'Every B that is J is always D' and 'Always either every R or every H', we conclude 'Every B that is J is such that always either nothing of D is A or nothing of D is H'—meaning that D is never both R and H (or never empty of them). The second mood is introduced but cut off; it changes only in that the parts of the disjunction are particular affirmatives. The passage is incomplete but demonstrates the systematic enumeration typical of later Islamic logic texts.

Compound Syllogisms with Particular Disjunctive Premises
Fourth combination with particular disjunctive and enumeration of parallel forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the valid form of a compound syllogism when the disjunctive premise is particular and state that the conclusion must be a particular conditional.

Core Idea
The quantity of the disjunctive premise determines the quantity of the conclusion in compound syllogisms.

This passage continues the enumeration of valid conjunctions (اقترانات) combining a conditional and a disjunctive premise. The author now turns to cases where the disjunctive premise is particular rather than universal. Understanding this shift is crucial for correctly predicting the conclusion of such compound syllogisms, especially in systematic logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
Locate the conditional (متصل) and disjunctive (منفصل) premises in the juxtaposition. The conditional can be universal or particular, affirmative or negative.
2
Determine the quantity of the disjunctive
Check whether the disjunctive premise is universal (كلية) or particular (جزئية). In this passage, the disjunctive is particular.
3
Apply the quantity rule
When the disjunctive is universal, the conclusion is a universal conditional. When it is particular, the conclusion is a particular conditional. This is because the particularity restricts the inference.
4
Verify with the passage's example
Examine the author's example: 'ودائماً بعض "د" ليس إما "هـ" وإما "أ"' (always some D is not either H or A). The conclusion stated is a particular conditional.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاقتران الرابع الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن أجزاء المنفصلة جزئية، كقولنا: ودائماً بعض "د" ليس إما "هـ" وإما "أ") ونتيجته كالتي قبله. وإن كانت المنفصلة جزئية موجبة أو جزئية سالبة والمتصلة بحالها، فقد يتألف منها مع المتصلة اقترانات أخرى أربعة موازية لهذه الأربعة، إلا أن النتيجة فيها تكون جزئية متصلة، وعلى هذا فلا يخفى الإنتاج إذا كانت المتصلة كلية سالبة وجزئية موجبة وجزئية سالبة، وهي ثمانية وعشرون اقتراناً أخرى، فاعتبرها، إذ هي سهلة الاعتبار.
The fourth conjunction: the conjunction is the same, except that the parts of the disjunctive are particular, as we say: 'And always some D is not either H or A' and its conclusion is like the one before it. And if the disjunctive is particular affirmative or particular negative while the conditional is as it is, then four other conjunctions may be composed from it with the conditional, parallel to these four, except that the conclusion in them is a particular conditional. And on this, production is not hidden if the conditional is universal negative, particular affirmative, and particular negative. And these are another twenty-eight conjunctions, so consider them, for they are easy to consider.
Source pages p. 396

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the forms of conjunctive syllogisms (اقترانات) that combine a conditional (متصل) and a disjunctive (منفصل) premise. The author has been enumerating various valid combinations. Here, he addresses the fourth type of conjunction, where the disjunctive premise is particular (جزئية) rather than universal. He gives an example using the variables D, H, A: 'Always some D is not either H or A' (i.e., a particular negative disjunctive). The conclusion remains the same as in the previous universal case, as indicated by 'like the one before it.' Then he states that when the disjunctive is particular affirmative or particular negative, while the conditional remains universal or particular (as previously considered), four additional conjunctions arise that are parallel to the previous four. However, the key difference is that the conclusion becomes a particular conditional (جزئية متصلة) instead of a universal one. This is because the disjunctive is particular, so the inference yields a particular result. He further notes that production (الإنتاج) is clear when the conditional is universal negative, particular affirmative, or particular negative, leading to a total of twenty-eight additional conjunctions. The author encourages the reader to consider these forms, as they are easy to grasp. The passage demonstrates the systematic nature of logic in exhaustively enumerating all valid syllogistic forms.

The Third and Fourth Conjunctive Forms
Author lists the third and fourth conjunctive forms of compound syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and construct the third and fourth conjunctive forms of compound syllogisms from a conditional and a disjunctive premise.

Core Idea
Compound syllogisms combine conditional and disjunctive premises into new forms.

The author enumerates the third and fourth conjunctive forms (iqtirān) of compound syllogisms that mix a conditional premise (muttasila) with a disjunctive premise (munfaṣila). In the third form, the disjunctive is particular affirmative with universal negative parts; in the fourth, its parts are particular affirmatives. Understanding these forms helps you track how logicians systematically catalogue all valid combinations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional premise
Find the 'muttasila' (if… then…) premise. In the third and fourth forms, the conditional remains unchanged: it is a universal affirmative conditional. Note its parts: antecedent and consequent.
2
Examine the disjunctive premise
For the third form, the disjunctive is a particular affirmative proposition (juz'iyya mūjiba) with each disjunct being a universal negative (kulliyya sālaba). For the fourth form, the disjuncts are particular affirmative (juz'iyya mūjiba). Locate the quantifier and quality.
The text uses 'جزئية موجبة' for particular affirmative.
3
Construct the conclusion
The conclusion is a conditional that mirrors the structure of the premises. In the third form, the conclusion is: 'Everything that is B is C may be (nothing of D is A or nothing of D is H)'. In the fourth form, the conclusion is similar but with particular affirmatives.
The variables A, B, C, D, H, R are placeholders.
4
Check the logical connection
Understand why the conclusion follows: in the third form, because if the disjunctive is true and one disjunct is false (since parts are universal negatives), the other must hold. The text says 'it may be' indicating possibility.
The reasoning assumes a true disjunction (exclusive or inclusive?). The author does not specify type, so treat as exclusive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاقتران الثالث: المتصلة بحالها، إلا أن المنفصلة جزئية موجبة أجزاؤها كليات سالبة، كقولنا: وقد يكون إما لا شيء من "أ" "ر" وإما لا شيء من "هـ" "ر") ونتيجته كل ما كان "ب" "ج" فقد يكون إما لا شيء من "د" "أ" وإما لا شيء من "د" "هـ"). الاقتران الرابع: الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن أجزاء المنفصلة جزئيات موجبة، كقولنا: و(دائماً إما بعض "أ" "ر" وإما بعض "هـ" "ر") ونتيجته كالتي قبله؛ لأنه إن صدق (بعض "أ" "ب" فلا شيء من "هـ" "ر") وإن صدق (بعض "هـ" "ر" فلا شيء من "أ" "ر").

وإن كان تالي المتصلة الكليّة الموجبة جزئياً موجباً، أو كلياً سالباً، أو جزئياً سالباً، فقد يأتلف منها مع المنفصلة اقترانات أخرى اثنا عشر، موازية لهذه الأربعة. وعلى هذا فلا يخفى الإنتاج والمتصلة جزئية موجبة، أو كلية سالبة، أو جزئية سالبة، وهي اثنان وثلاثون اقتراناً أخرى، فيصير المجموع ثمانية وأربعون اقتراناً، فاعتبرها.
The third conjunctive form: the conditional remains as it is, except that the disjunctive is a particular affirmative whose parts are universals negative, as in our saying: 'And it may be that nothing of A is R or nothing of H is R' and its conclusion: 'Everything that is B is C may be nothing of D is A or nothing of D is H'. The fourth conjunctive form: the conjunctive form remains as it is, except that the parts of the disjunctive are particular affirmatives, as in our saying: 'And always either some A is R or some H is R' and its conclusion is like the previous one; because if 'some A is B' is true, then 'nothing of H is R' is true, and if 'some H is R' is true, then 'nothing of A is R' is true.

And if the consequent of the universal affirmative conditional is particular affirmative, or universal negative, or particular negative, then with the disjunctive there may be composed another twelve conjunctive forms parallel to these four. And based on this, the production is not hidden when the conditional is particular affirmative, or universal negative, or particular negative, and they are another thirty-two conjunctive forms, so the total becomes forty-eight conjunctive forms, so consider them.
Source pages p. 397

Commentary
This passage presents the third and fourth conjunctive forms (اقترانات) of compound syllogisms that combine a conditional (متصل) premise with a disjunctive (منفصل) premise. In the third form, the conditional remains unchanged, but the disjunctive is a particular affirmative whose parts are universal negatives—e.g., 'Either nothing of A is R or nothing of H is R'. The conclusion is a conditional stating that for every B that is C, either nothing of D is A or nothing of D is H. In the fourth form, the parts of the disjunctive are particular affirmatives—e.g., 'Always either some A is R or some H is R'—and the conclusion follows similarly. The author then generalizes: if the consequent of the conditional varies in quantity/quality (particular affirmative, universal negative, particular negative), twelve more forms emerge parallel to the basic four. Further, varying the conditional itself (particular affirmative, universal negative, particular negative) yields thirty-two additional forms, totaling forty-eight. This enumeration is typical of logical manuals that catalogue all possible moods of compound syllogisms. For an Islamic example: consider a conditional like 'If everything that is khamr is forbidden, then every nabidh shares the 'illa'—but the passage focuses on formal structure, not content.

Enumerating the 48 Compound Syllogistic Forms
Author enumerates the total 48 conjunctive forms through variations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the total number of compound conjunctive forms (48) by tracing the variations described by the author.

Core Idea
From four basic forms, variations in quantity and quality multiply to 48.

After presenting the third and fourth forms, the author expands the enumeration: if the consequent of the conditional varies (particular affirmative, universal negative, particular negative), twelve new forms emerge parallel to the four basic ones. Then varying the conditional itself (particular affirmative, universal negative, particular negative) adds thirty-two more. The final count is 48. This combinatorial approach is typical of logical encyclopedism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four basic forms
The author implies four basic forms (including the first two not detailed here). The third and fourth are given. Assume the first two are analogous with universal affirmative disjunctives etc.
The first two forms are not in this passage but are context.
2
Vary the consequent of the conditional
The conditional is universal affirmative. Change its consequent to three other types: particular affirmative, universal negative, particular negative. Each variation yields four new forms (parallel to the four basic ones), giving 3 × 4 = 12 forms.
3
Vary the conditional itself
The conditional can be particular affirmative, universal negative, or particular negative. The author says this yields 32 forms. So 4 + 12 + 32 = 48.
The exact combinatorial breakdown may be debated, but the total is clear.
4
Sum the totals
Add the basic four (including the first two) plus 12 from consequent variations plus 32 from conditional variations: 4 + 12 + 32 = 48.

The Fourth Figure Analogy in Compound Syllogisms
Author specifies conditions for productive compound syllogisms resembling the fourth figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a compound syllogism with the subject in the consequent of the conditional resembles the fourth figure and state the basic condition for its productivity.

Core Idea
Compound syllogisms with subject in consequent mirror the fourth figure; productivity requires universality and a proper disjunction.

The author extends categorical syllogistic figures to compound syllogisms involving conditionals and disjunctives. When the common term appears as the subject of the consequent of the conditional, the structure parallels the fourth figure. Here we learn the foundational condition that makes such syllogisms productive.

Guided Path
1
Identify the common term's position
In the compound syllogism, the common term (shared between the conditional and disjunctive) appears as the subject of the consequent of the conditional. This placement corresponds to the fourth figure's structure where the middle term is the predicate of the major and subject of the minor.
2
Recall the fourth figure of categorical syllogisms
In the fourth figure, the middle term serves as predicate in the major premise and subject in the minor. The author draws an analogy: here, the common term joins the conditional and disjunctive similarly.
Not all logicians accepted the fourth figure, but the author uses it as a pedagogical analogy.
3
Learn the productivity condition
The author states that for such a configuration to yield a valid conclusion, one premise must be universal (kulliyya) and the disjunctive must be a true disjunction (mūjiba ḥaqīqiyya) or an exclusive of emptiness (māni'at al-khulūw), but not excluding both combination and emptiness.
'Without the combination' means not māni'at al-jam' and not māni'at al-khulūw wal-jam'; so it is either exclusive (true disjunction) or inclusive (māni'at al-khulūw).
4
Apply to a simple example
Consider a conditional: 'If everything that is khamr is forbidden, then every nabidh is an intoxicant' (subject of consequent: nabidh). Combine with a disjunctive: 'Either every intoxicant is khamr or every intoxicant is nabidh.' Here the common term is 'intoxicant' appearing as subject in the conditional's consequent and predicate in the disjunctive. The condition is satisfied if one premise is universal and the disjunctive is true or inclusive.

And if it is a subject in the consequent of the conditional, then it is in the form of the fourth figure, and let the parts of the disjunctive be uniform in quantity and quality—and you know what is beyond that—so the condition for its productivity is that one of the two premises be universal for what preceded, and that the disjunctive be a true affirmative or māni'at al-khulūw without the combination.

And if the conditional is affirmative and its consequent is negative, then it must be universal and the parts of the disjunctive are universal affirmatives or particular negatives. And if its consequent is particular affirmative, then the parts of the disjunctive must be universal negatives or particular affirmatives. And if the conditional is negative, then its consequent must not be universal affirmative. And if its consequent is universal negative, then the parts of the disjunctive must be universal negatives or particular affirmatives. And if it is particular affirmative, then the parts of the disjunctive must be universal affirmatives or particular negatives, and its productive conjunctive forms are fifty-two, and its conclusion in all is a conditional whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the disjunctive.

The first conjunctive form: from two universal affirmatives with the consequent universal affirmative and the parts of the disjunction universal affirmatives, as in our saying: 'Everything that is B is C, then every D is R' and 'Always either every A is D or every H is D' and its conclusion: 'Everything that is B is C, then always either some R is A or some R is H.'

The second conjunctive form: the conjunctive form remains as it is, except that the parts of the disjunctive are universals negative, as in our saying: 'And always either nothing of A is D or nothing of H is D' and its conclusion: 'Everything that is B is C, then always either not every B is A or not every R is H.'

The third conjunctive form: the conjunctive form remains as it is, except that the parts of the disjunctive are particular affirmatives, as in our saying: 'And always either some A is D or some H is D' and its conclusion is like the first.

The fourth conjunctive form: the conjunctive form remains as it is, except that the parts of the disjunctive are particular negatives, as in our saying: 'And always either not every A is D or not every H is J' and its conclusion is also like the first; because if 'not every D is A' is true, then 'every H is D' is true, and it was 'every D is R'; and if 'not every H is D' is true, then 'every A is D' is true, and it was 'every D is R'.

And if the disjunctive is particular affirmative, then there may be composed from it with the conditional as it is another four conjunctive forms parallel to these conjunctive forms, except that the consequent in them is a particular disjunctive.

And based on this, the production is not hidden when the consequent of the disjunctive is particular affirmative, or universal negative, or the conditional is particular affirmative or universal negative, or particular negative, and they are another forty-six conjunctive forms, so you must consider them.

Commentary
This passage discusses compound syllogisms where the common term (the term shared between the conditional and disjunctive) is the subject of the consequent of the conditional. The author notes that this configuration resembles the fourth figure of categorical syllogisms. The condition for a productive syllogism is that one premise be universal and the disjunctive be a true disjunction or exclusive of emptiness (māni'at al-khulūw), not both combination and emptiness. The author then lists specific rules for various combinations of the conditional and disjunctive quantifiers. For example, if the conditional is affirmative and its consequent is negative, the consequent must be universal and the disjunctive parts must be universal affirmative or particular negative. After listing the conditions, the author gives examples of the first four conjunctive forms with different quantifier patterns, each with a sample conclusion. For instance, the first form uses two universal affirmatives where the conditional consequent is universal and the disjunctive parts are universal affirmatives; the conclusion is a conditional with a disjunctive consequent. The second form mirrors the first but with universal negative disjunctive parts, etc. The author also notes that if the disjunctive itself is particular affirmative, four additional forms arise. Finally, a total of forty-six more forms are mentioned when the consequent of the disjunctive varies and the conditional varies in quantity/quality. These enumerations are typical in advanced logical works like Daqā'iq al-Ḥaqā'iq, ensuring exhaustive coverage of all valid moods. An Islamic example might use terms like 'If everything that is khamr is forbidden, then every nabidh is an intoxicant' combined with a disjunctive such as 'Either every intoxicant is khamr or every intoxicant is nabidh'—but the author here uses placeholder letters for formal clarity.
