Quantifier Rules for Productive Compound Syllogisms
Author specifies conditions for productive compound syllogisms resembling the fourth figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the specific quantifier conditions for a productive compound syllogism when the subject is in the consequent, depending on the quality and quantity of the conditional and its consequent.

Core Idea
The productivity conditions change based on whether the conditional is affirmative/negative and whether its consequent is universal/particular.

Beyond the general condition, the author provides a detailed set of rules for different combinations of the conditional and its consequent. These rules determine which quantifier patterns in the disjunctive parts are valid. This lesson walks through those rules systematically.

Guided Path
1
Understand the structure
We have a conditional (muttaṣila) and a disjunctive (munfaṣila). The common term is the subject of the consequent of the conditional. The disjunctive has two parts. The author gives conditions based on the quality (affirmative/negative) of the conditional and its consequent.
2
Case 1: Conditional affirmative, consequent negative
If the conditional is affirmative and its consequent is negative, then the consequent must be universal (kulliyya) and the parts of the disjunctive are universal affirmatives or particular negatives.
For example, conditional: 'If every intoxicant is forbidden, then every nabidh is not permissible' (consequent universal negative). Then disjunctive parts must be either both universal affirmative or both particular negative.
3
Case 2: Conditional affirmative, consequent particular affirmative
If the conditional is affirmative and the consequent is particular affirmative, then the disjunctive parts must be universal negatives or particular affirmatives.
4
Case 3: Conditional negative
If the conditional is negative, then the consequent must not be universal affirmative. If the consequent is universal negative, disjunctive parts must be universal negatives or particular affirmatives. If the consequent is particular affirmative, disjunctive parts must be universal affirmatives or particular negatives.
These rules ensure that the quantifier combinations preserve validity.
5
Apply to an example
Take a fiqh example: Conditional: 'If every khamr is forbidden, then some drink is not khamr' (affirmative conditional with particular negative consequent? Actually the consequent here is 'some drink is not khamr' which is particular negative, but the rule for affirmative conditional with negative consequent requires the consequent to be universal. This example would not satisfy the condition. Instead, use a conditional like 'If every khamr is forbidden, then every drink is not khamr' (affirmative, consequent universal negative). Then the disjunctive could be 'Either every drink is nabidh or every drink is khamr' (universal affirmatives) or 'Either some drink is not nabidh or some drink is not khamr' (particular negatives). This would be productive.

Analyzing Productive Conjunctive Forms
Author specifies conditions for productive compound syllogisms resembling the fourth figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the conclusion of a compound syllogism in the fourth-figure style given its premises, using the author's first four conjunctive forms as models.

Core Idea
The conclusion's consequent is a disjunction formed from the parts of the original disjunctive and the predicates of the conditional.

After stating the conditions, the author provides concrete examples of productive conjunctive forms (iqtirānāt muntija). In these examples, the conclusion is a conditional whose antecedent matches the original conditional's antecedent and whose consequent is a disjunction relating the original disjunctive parts. We examine the first four forms to see the pattern.

Guided Path
1
Set up the first conjunctive form
Take the conditional: 'Everything that is B is C, then every D is R' (universal affirmatives). The disjunctive: 'Always either every A is D or every H is D' (universal affirmatives). The conclusion: 'Everything that is B is C, then always either some R is A or some R is H.' Notice how the predicates of the disjunctive (A and H) become the subjects of the conclusion's consequent, and the predicate R from the conditional's consequent becomes the predicate of each part.
2
Second form: universal negative disjunctive parts
The conditional remains the same. The disjunctive: 'Always either nothing of A is D or nothing of H is D' (universal negatives). The conclusion: 'Everything that is B is C, then always either not every B is A or not every R is H.' Here, the predicates change: 'not every B is A' and 'not every R is H'. The subject of each part in the conclusion is B (from the antecedent? Actually B appears from the conditional's antecedent) and R from the consequent.
The author uses 'B' and 'R' as variables. Do not confuse with actual words.
3
Third form: particular affirmative disjunctive parts
Disjunctive: 'Always either some A is D or some H is D.' The conclusion is like the first: '...then always either some R is A or some R is H.'
4
Fourth form: particular negative disjunctive parts
Disjunctive: 'Always either not every A is D or not every H is J' (note: J appears instead of D? The text says 'J' but earlier it was 'R'. Possibly a typo). The author gives a proof: if 'not every D is A' then 'every H is D' and from conditional 'every D is R', so conclusion holds. This shows the reasoning behind the validity.
The pattern is that the conclusion's consequent always has a disjunction of two statements relating the original predicate R (or the subject of the conditional's consequent?) and the disjunctive terms.
5
Generalize the pattern
In all productive forms, the conclusion is a conditional with the same antecedent as the original conditional. The consequent is a disjunction formed by taking each part of the original disjunctive and substituting the predicate of the conditional's consequent (R) as the subject and the disjunctive term (A or H) as the predicate, possibly with negation adjustments. The exact form depends on the quantifier of the disjunctive parts.

Source Text

وأما إن كان موضوعاً في تالي المتصلة فهو على هيئة الشكل الرابع، ولتكن أجزاء المنفصلة متحدة الكمية والكيفية - وأنت تعرف ما وراء ذلك - فشرط إنتاجه أن تكون إحدى المقدمتين كليّة لما سبق وأن تكون المنفصلة موجبة حقيقية أو مانعة الخلق دون الجمع.
وإن كانت المتصلة موجبة وتاليها سالباً أن يكون كلياً وأجزاء المنفصلة كليات موجبة، أو جزئيات سالبة. وإن كان تاليها جزئياً موجباً أن تكون أجزاء المنفصلة كليّة سالبة، أو جزئية موجبة. وإن كانت المتصلة سالبة أن لا يكون تاليها كلياً موجباً. وإن كان تاليها كلياً سالباً أن تكون أجزاء المنفصلة كليات سالبة، أو جزئيات موجبة. وإن كان جزئياً موجباً أن تكون أجزاء المنفصلة كليات موجبة، أو جزئيات سالبة، وقرائنه المنتجة اثنان وخمسون اقتراناً، ونتيجته في الكل متصلة مقدمها مقدم المتصلة وتاليها نتيجة اقتران التالي بالمنفصلة

الاقتران الأول من كليتين موجبتين والتالي كلي موجب وأجزاء الانفصال كليات موجبة كقولنا كل ما كان "ب" "ج" فكل "د" "ر") و(دائماً إما كل "أ" "د" وإما كل "هـ" "د") ونتيجته كل ما كان "ب" "ج" فدائماً إما بعض "ر" "أ" وإما بعض "ر" "هـ"

الاقتران الثاني: الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن أجزاء المنفصلة كليات سالبة، كقولنا: و (دائماً إما لا شيء من "أ" "د" وإما لا شيء من "هـ" "د") ونتيجته (كل ما كان "ب" "ج" فدائماً إما ليس كل "ب" "أ" وإما ليس كل "ر" "هـ").

الاقتران الثالث: الاقتران بحاله إلا أن أجزاء المنفصلة جزئيات موجبة كقولنا: و(دائماً إما بعض "أ" "د" وإما بعض "هـ" "د") ونتيجته كالأول.

الاقتران الرابع: الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن أجزاء المنفصلة جزئية سالبة، كقولنا: و(دائماً إما ليس كل "أ" "د" وإما ليس كل "هـ" "ج") ونتيجته كالأول أيضاً؛ لأنه إن صدق ليس كل "د" "أ" صدق كل "هـ" "د" وكان ( كل "د" "ر" وإن صدق (ليس كل "هـ" "د") صدق ( كل "أ" "د") وكان (كل "د" "ر").

وإن كانت المنفصلة جزئية موجبة فقد يأتلف منها مع المنفصلة بحالها، اقترانات أخرى أربعة موازية لهذه الاقترانات، إلا أن التالي فيها يكون جزئياً منفصلاً.

وعلى هذا فلا يخفى الإنتاج عندما إذا كان التالي من المنفصلة جزئياً موجباً، أو كلياً سالباً، أو كانت المتصلة جزئية موجبة أو كليّة سالبة، أو جزئية سالبة، وهي ستة وأربعون اقتراناً أخرى، فعليك باعتبارها.
And if it is a subject in the consequent of the conditional, then it is in the form of the fourth figure, and let the parts of the disjunctive be uniform in quantity and quality—and you know what is beyond that—so the condition for its productivity is that one of the two premises be universal for what preceded, and that the disjunctive be a true affirmative or māni'at al-khulūw without the combination.

And if the conditional is affirmative and its consequent is negative, then it must be universal and the parts of the disjunctive are universal affirmatives or particular negatives. And if its consequent is particular affirmative, then the parts of the disjunctive must be universal negatives or particular affirmatives. And if the conditional is negative, then its consequent must not be universal affirmative. And if its consequent is universal negative, then the parts of the disjunctive must be universal negatives or particular affirmatives. And if it is particular affirmative, then the parts of the disjunctive must be universal affirmatives or particular negatives, and its productive conjunctive forms are fifty-two, and its conclusion in all is a conditional whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the disjunctive.

The first conjunctive form: from two universal affirmatives with the consequent universal affirmative and the parts of the disjunction universal affirmatives, as in our saying: 'Everything that is B is C, then every D is R' and 'Always either every A is D or every H is D' and its conclusion: 'Everything that is B is C, then always either some R is A or some R is H.'

The second conjunctive form: the conjunctive form remains as it is, except that the parts of the disjunctive are universals negative, as in our saying: 'And always either nothing of A is D or nothing of H is D' and its conclusion: 'Everything that is B is C, then always either not every B is A or not every R is H.'

The third conjunctive form: the conjunctive form remains as it is, except that the parts of the disjunctive are particular affirmatives, as in our saying: 'And always either some A is D or some H is D' and its conclusion is like the first.

The fourth conjunctive form: the conjunctive form remains as it is, except that the parts of the disjunctive are particular negatives, as in our saying: 'And always either not every A is D or not every H is J' and its conclusion is also like the first; because if 'not every D is A' is true, then 'every H is D' is true, and it was 'every D is R'; and if 'not every H is D' is true, then 'every A is D' is true, and it was 'every D is R'.

And if the disjunctive is particular affirmative, then there may be composed from it with the conditional as it is another four conjunctive forms parallel to these conjunctive forms, except that the consequent in them is a particular disjunctive.

And based on this, the production is not hidden when the consequent of the disjunctive is particular affirmative, or universal negative, or the conditional is particular affirmative or universal negative, or particular negative, and they are another forty-six conjunctive forms, so you must consider them.
Source pages p. 397

Commentary
This passage discusses compound syllogisms where the common term (the term shared between the conditional and disjunctive) is the subject of the consequent of the conditional. The author notes that this configuration resembles the fourth figure of categorical syllogisms. The condition for a productive syllogism is that one premise be universal and the disjunctive be a true disjunction or exclusive of emptiness (māni'at al-khulūw), not both combination and emptiness. The author then lists specific rules for various combinations of the conditional and disjunctive quantifiers. For example, if the conditional is affirmative and its consequent is negative, the consequent must be universal and the disjunctive parts must be universal affirmative or particular negative. After listing the conditions, the author gives examples of the first four conjunctive forms with different quantifier patterns, each with a sample conclusion. For instance, the first form uses two universal affirmatives where the conditional consequent is universal and the disjunctive parts are universal affirmatives; the conclusion is a conditional with a disjunctive consequent. The second form mirrors the first but with universal negative disjunctive parts, etc. The author also notes that if the disjunctive itself is particular affirmative, four additional forms arise. Finally, a total of forty-six more forms are mentioned when the consequent of the disjunctive varies and the conditional varies in quantity/quality. These enumerations are typical in advanced logical works like Daqā'iq al-Ḥaqā'iq, ensuring exhaustive coverage of all valid moods. An Islamic example might use terms like 'If everything that is khamr is forbidden, then every nabidh is an intoxicant' combined with a disjunctive such as 'Either every intoxicant is khamr or every intoxicant is nabidh'—but the author here uses placeholder letters for formal clarity.

A) Always either every A is R or every H is R.
B) Always either some R is A or some R is H.
C) Always either not every R is A or not every R is H.
D) Always either some A is R or some H is R.

Key Takeaway
The conclusion of a productive compound syllogism in this style transforms the disjunctive parts by reordering terms according to the conditional's structure.

The Syllogism
advanced
15 min
The First Four Productive Conjunctions of Mixed Syllogisms
Enumeration of the first four conjunctions of mixed syllogisms with universal affirmative conditional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four productive conjunctions of a mixed syllogism when the conditional premise is universal affirmative and its consequent is universal affirmative, and to determine the conclusion for each disjunctive type.

Core Idea
Mixed syllogisms combine a conditional and a disjunctive; the conclusion's form depends on the disjuncts' quantity and quality.

In this lesson, we examine the first four productive conjunctions (iqtirānāt) of mixed syllogisms, where a universal affirmative conditional premise is combined with a disjunctive premise of various types. These forms are foundational for constructing valid compound arguments in Islamic logic, particularly in theological and jurisprudential reasoning. By parsing these conjunctions, you will learn how the conclusion is derived.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional premise
The passage assumes a conditional 'كل ما كان ب ج فكل د ر' (Everything that is B is J, so every D is R). Note that both antecedent and consequent are universal affirmatives. This remains constant across the four conjunctions.
The conditional antecedent is 'كل ما كان ب ج' and the consequent is 'كل د ر'.
2
Identify the disjunctive premise
The disjunctive premise varies in each conjunction. For the first, it is 'دائماً إما كل أ د وإما كل هـ د' (Always either every A is D or every H is D) — universal affirmative. For the second, it is universal negative: 'دائماً إما لا شيء من أ د وإما لا شيء من هـ د'. For the third, particular affirmative: 'دائماً إما بعض أ د وإما بعض هـ د'. For the fourth, particular negative: 'دائماً إما ليس كل أ د وإما ليس كل هـ ج' (with a typo: should be هـ د).
Pay attention to the signs: 'كل' (all), 'لا شيء' (nothing), 'بعض' (some), 'ليس كل' (not all).
3
Determine the conclusion pattern
For the first conjunction, the conclusion is 'كل ما كان ب ج فدائماً إما بعض ر أ وإما بعض ر هـ' (If everything that is B is J, then always either some R is A or some R is H). The second conjunction yields a different conclusion: 'كل ما كان ب ج فدائماً إما ليس كل ب أ وإما ليس كل ر هـ' (If everything that is B is J, then always either not every B is A or not every R is H). The third and fourth conjunctions produce the same conclusion as the first.
The second conjunction's consequent is a universal negative disjunction, while the others are particular affirmative.
4
Verify the reasoning for the fourth conjunction
The author provides a justification for the fourth: if 'ليس كل د أ' (not every D is A) is true, then 'كل هـ د' (every H is D) is true, and from the conditional 'كل د ر' (every D is R) follows. Similarly, if 'ليس كل هـ د' is true, then 'كل أ د' holds, and again 'كل د ر' follows. Thus the conclusion holds.
This step demonstrates the logical dependency between the disjuncts and the conditional consequent.

الاقتران الأول من كليتين موجبتين والتالي كلي موجب وأجزاء الانفصال كليات موجبة كقولنا كل ما كان "ب" "ج" فكل "د" "ر" و"دائماً إما كل "أ" "د" وإما كل "هـ" "د"" ونتيجته كل ما كان "ب" "ج" فدائماً إما بعض "ر" "أ" وإما بعض "ر" "هـ"

وإن كانت المنفصلة جزئية موجبة فقد يأتلف منها مع المنفصلة بحالها، اقترانات أخرى أربعة موازية لهذه الاقترانات، إلا أن التالي فيها يكون جزئياً منفصلاً.
وعلى هذا فلا يخفى الإنتاج عندما إذا كان التالي من المنفصلة جزئياً موجباً، أو كلياً سالباً، أو كانت المتصلة جزئية موجبة أو كلية سالبة، أو جزئية سالبة، وهي ستة وأربعون اقتراناً أخرى، فعليك باعتبارها.
Continuation of the first art: on defining the forms of conjunctive syllogisms. [From] negative, and its productive conjunctions are fifty-two conjunctions, and its conclusion in all is a conditional whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional and whose consequent is the result of conjointing the consequent with the disjunctive. The first conjunction: from two universal affirmatives, the consequent universal affirmative, and the parts of the disjunction universal affirmatives, as our saying: 'Everything that is B is J, so every D is R' and 'Always either every A is D or every H is D', and its conclusion: 'Everything that is B is J, so always either some R is A or some R is H'. The second conjunction: the conjunction in its state, except that the parts of the disjunction are universal negatives, as our saying: 'Always either nothing of A is D or nothing of H is D', and its conclusion: 'Everything that is B is J, so always either not every B is A or not every R is H'. The third conjunction: the conjunction in its state, except that the parts of the disjunction are particular affirmatives, as our saying: 'Always either some A is D or some H is D', and its conclusion is like the first. The fourth conjunction: the conjunction in its state, except that the parts of the disjunction are particular negatives, as our saying: 'Always either not every A is D or not every H is J', and its conclusion is also like the first; because if 'not every D is A' is true, then 'every H is D' is true, and 'every D is R' holds; and if 'not every H is D' is true, then 'every A is D' is true, and 'every D is R' holds. And if the disjunction is particular affirmative, then there can be composed from it with the disjunction in its state another four conjunctions parallel to these conjunctions, except that the consequent in them is a particular disjunction. And on this, production is not hidden when the consequent of the disjunction is particular affirmative, or universal negative, or the conditional is particular affirmative or universal negative, or particular negative, and these are another forty-six conjunctions, so you must consider them.
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage is a systematic enumeration of the productive forms (iqtirānāt) of mixed syllogisms that combine a conditional (muttaṣila) premise and a disjunctive (munfaṣila) premise. The author has already stated that there are fifty-two productive conjunctions overall. Here he begins with the case where the disjunctive premise serves as the antecedent of the conditional (shared term). He lists four basic conjunctions, each distinguished by the quantity and quality of the disjunctive parts. The first conjunction uses two universal affirmatives in both the conditional and disjunctive, yielding a conclusion that is a particular affirmative disjunction. The second uses universal negative disjuncts, giving a conclusion that negates parts universally. The third uses particular affirmative disjuncts, with the same conclusion as the first. The fourth uses particular negative disjuncts, and the author demonstrates why the conclusion remains the same through a logical justification: if one disjunct fails, the other must hold, and the conditional ensures the connection. After these four, he adds a second set of four when the disjunctive is particular affirmative, but the conclusion's consequent becomes a particular disjunction. Finally, he notes that many other combinations (forty-six more) follow the same pattern, urging the student to consider them. This is a parsing exercise: the student must map the logical forms to the evaluative rules of syllogistic.

Checkpoint
In the second conjunction of the mixed syllogism, if the disjunctive parts are universal negatives, what form does the conclusion take?
A) A conditional with a universal affirmative consequent
B) A conditional with a particular negative consequent
C) A conditional with a universal negative consequent (negating each part)
D) A conditional with a particular affirmative disjunction

Key Takeaway
The first four conjunctions show that when the conditional premise is universal affirmative, the conclusion is a conditional whose disjunctive consequent is particular affirmative for three of the four disjunctive types, and universal negative for the universal negative type.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the following argument: 'If every witness is reliable, then every testimony is accepted. Always either every eyewitness is a witness or every expert is a witness. Therefore, if every witness is reliable, then always either some accepted testimony is from an eyewitness or some accepted testimony is from an expert.' Which conjunction does this represent? Verify the validity by checking the quantifiers.
?
Construct a mixed syllogism in the first conjunction form using Islamic terms related to prayer (ṣalāh).

Additional Conjunctions and Generalization
Extension to particular affirmative disjunctions and generalization to forty-six other forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the additional four conjunctions when the disjunctive is particular affirmative and understand the general pattern for the remaining forty-six forms.

Core Idea
When the disjunctive is particular affirmative, four more valid conjunctions arise, and many other combinations follow the same logical pattern.

After examining the first four conjunctions, the author adds another set of four valid combinations when the disjunctive premise is a particular affirmative. This lesson covers these additional conjunctions and the general principle that allows extending the analysis to many more forms. This parsing exercise develops your ability to recognize valid structures in compound logical arguments.

Guided Path
1
Understand the new condition
The author introduces: 'If the disjunction is particular affirmative' — here the disjunctive premise itself is a particular proposition (e.g., 'some A is D or some H is D') rather than being composed of particular parts. This is a distinct case from the first four conjunctions.
In the first four, the disjunctive premise was universal; now the disjunctive itself is particular.
2
Identify the four parallel conjunctions
The four new conjunctions are parallel to the first four, but the consequent of the conditional conclusion becomes a particular disjunction. That is, the conclusion's consequent is 'some R is A or some R is H' rather than a universal or particular affirmative.
The antecedent remains the same universal conditional, but the conclusion's disjunction is particular.
3
Generalize to other combinations
The author then states that when the disjunctive consequent is particular affirmative, or universal negative, or if the conditional is particular affirmative, universal negative, or particular negative, there are forty-six other productive conjunctions. The pattern should be considered by the student.
This is an invitation to systematically derive all valid forms by varying the quantity and quality of each component.
4
Map the variables
Use the same variable mapping from the first lesson. The abstract form remains: (if P then Q) and (disjunction of two categoricals) yields (if P then a disjunction of two categoricals), but the quantifiers change systematically.
The key is to apply the rules of conversion and inference to ensure validity.

Continuation of the first art: on defining the forms of conjunctive syllogisms. [From] negative, and its productive conjunctions are fifty-two conjunctions, and its conclusion in all is a conditional whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional and whose consequent is the result of conjointing the consequent with the disjunctive. The first conjunction: from two universal affirmatives, the consequent universal affirmative, and the parts of the disjunction universal affirmatives, as our saying: 'Everything that is B is J, so every D is R' and 'Always either every A is D or every H is D', and its conclusion: 'Everything that is B is J, so always either some R is A or some R is H'. The second conjunction: the conjunction in its state, except that the parts of the disjunction are universal negatives, as our saying: 'Always either nothing of A is D or nothing of H is D', and its conclusion: 'Everything that is B is J, so always either not every B is A or not every R is H'. The third conjunction: the conjunction in its state, except that the parts of the disjunction are particular affirmatives, as our saying: 'Always either some A is D or some H is D', and its conclusion is like the first. The fourth conjunction: the conjunction in its state, except that the parts of the disjunction are particular negatives, as our saying: 'Always either not every A is D or not every H is J', and its conclusion is also like the first; because if 'not every D is A' is true, then 'every H is D' is true, and 'every D is R' holds; and if 'not every H is D' is true, then 'every A is D' is true, and 'every D is R' holds. And if the disjunction is particular affirmative, then there can be composed from it with the disjunction in its state another four conjunctions parallel to these conjunctions, except that the consequent in them is a particular disjunction. And on this, production is not hidden when the consequent of the disjunction is particular affirmative, or universal negative, or the conditional is particular affirmative or universal negative, or particular negative, and these are another forty-six conjunctions, so you must consider them.
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage is a systematic enumeration of the productive forms (iqtirānāt) of mixed syllogisms that combine a conditional (muttaṣila) premise and a disjunctive (munfaṣila) premise. The author has already stated that there are fifty-two productive conjunctions overall. Here he begins with the case where the disjunctive premise serves as the antecedent of the conditional (shared term). He lists four basic conjunctions, each distinguished by the quantity and quality of the disjunctive parts. The first conjunction uses two universal affirmatives in both the conditional and disjunctive, yielding a conclusion that is a particular affirmative disjunction. The second uses universal negative disjuncts, giving a conclusion that negates parts universally. The third uses particular affirmative disjuncts, with the same conclusion as the first. The fourth uses particular negative disjuncts, and the author demonstrates why the conclusion remains the same through a logical justification: if one disjunct fails, the other must hold, and the conditional ensures the connection. After these four, he adds a second set of four when the disjunctive is particular affirmative, but the conclusion's consequent becomes a particular disjunction. Finally, he notes that many other combinations (forty-six more) follow the same pattern, urging the student to consider them. This is a parsing exercise: the student must map the logical forms to the evaluative rules of syllogistic.

Key Takeaway
The mixed syllogism with a conditional and a disjunctive has many valid forms; the first eight are explicitly given, and the student is expected to derive the remaining forty-six by systematic variation.

Mixed Syllogism: Antecedent as Subject of the Disjunction
First subtype: antecedent as subject of disjunction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and validate the eight productive conjunctions when the antecedent is the subject of the disjunction in a mixed syllogism.

Core Idea
When the antecedent is the subject of the disjunction, the conclusion is a particular affirmative conditional with a disjunctive consequent.

This passage introduces the first type of mixed syllogism where the common element is the antecedent of the conditional, which also serves as the subject of a disjunctive proposition. You will learn the conditions for such conjunctions to be productive and how the eight valid forms emerge from different premise combinations. Understanding this helps in analyzing compound arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the common element
Locate the term that appears as the antecedent of the conditional and as the subject of the disjunctive. In the example, it is 'ب' (B).
2
State the conditions for productivity
The conditional must be affirmative, and the disjunctive must apply to all instances of the subject (universal). Also, the parts of the disjunction must be uniform in quality and quantity.
These conditions ensure the argument yields a valid conclusion.
3
List the eight productive conjunctions
There are four main conjunctions derived from varying the quality and quantity of the disjunctive (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative) with a universal affirmative conditional. Another four arise when the conditional is particular affirmative.
The conclusion is always a particular affirmative conditional with a disjunctive consequent.
4
Verify an example
Take the first conjunction: conditional 'كل ما كان ب د ر' and disjunctive 'دائماً كل ب ج إما هـ وإما أ'. The conclusion is 'قد يكون إذا كان د ر فإما هـ وإما أ'. Check that the conditions hold and the conclusion follows.
Validate by replacing variables with concrete Islamic terms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كان الاشتراك بين المنفصلة ومقدم المتصلة، فإما أن يكون الاشتراك بالمقدم أو بجزأيه، فإن كان بالمقدم فإما أن يكون موضوعاً لأجزاء الانفصال، أو هو أحد أجزاء الانفصال، فإن كان موضوعاً لأجزاء الانفصال، وليكن الفرض في ما إذا كانت أجزاء المنفصلة متحدة الكيفية والكمية، فشرط إنتاجه إيجاب المتصلة وأن يكون الحكم بأجزاء الانفصال على كل الموضوع، وقرائنه المنتجة ثمان اقترانات، والنتيجة في الكل جزئية موجبة متصلة، مقدمها تالي المتصلة وتاليها منفصل من أجزاء المنفصلة:
الاقتران الأول من كليتين موجبتين، كقولنا : كل ما كان "ب" د" "ر" و"دائماً كل "ب" "ج" إما "هـ" وإما "أ"" فـ "قد يكون إذا كان "د" "ر" فإما "هـ" وإما "أ".
الاقتران الثاني: المتصلة بحالها، إلا أن المنفصلة كلية سالبة، كقولنا: و"ليس البتة إما أن يكون كل "ب" "ج" "هـ" وإما "أ"" فقد يكون إذا كان "د" "ر" فـ "ليس البتة كل "ب" "ج" "أ" إما "هـ" وإما "أ".
الاقتران الثالث المتصلة بحالها، إلا أن المنفصلة جزئية موجبة كقولنا وقد يكون كل "ب" "ج" إما "هـ" وإما "أ" فـ "قد يكون إذا كان "د" "ر" فقد يكون كل "ب" "ج" إما "هـ" وإما "أ".
الاقتران الرابع المتصلة بحالها، إلا أن المنفصلة جزئية سالبة، كقولنا: و"ليس دائماً كل "ب" "ج" إما "هـ" وإما "أ".
وإن كانت المتصلة جزئية موجبة، فقد يأتلف منها مع المنفصلة اقترانات أخرى أربعة موازية لهذه الأربعة، فاعتبرها.
وأما إن كان المقدم هو أحد أجزاء المنفصلة، فشرط إنتاجه إيجاب المتصلة، وأن تكون المنفصلة كلية؛ حتى لا يختلف وقت الحكم. وإن كانت موجبة فإن تكون حقيقية مانعة الجمع دون الخلو، وقرائنه أربعة اقترانات، إلا أنه إذا كانت المنفصلة موجبة، فالنتيجة جزئية موجبة متصلة، مقدمها تالي المتصلة، وتاليها سلب الجزء الذي لا اشتراك فيه من أجزاء المنفصلة. وإن كانت المتصلة سالبة فالنتيجة جزئية سالبة منفصلة من تالي المتصلة والجزء الذي لا اشتراك فيه من أجزاء المنفصلة.
And as for if the commonality between the disjunctive and the antecedent of the conditional, then either the commonality is by the antecedent or by its two parts. If by the antecedent, then either it is the subject of the parts of the disjunction, or it is one of the parts of the disjunction. If it is the subject of the parts of the disjunction, and let the assumption be when the parts of the disjunction are uniform in quality and quantity, then the condition for its productivity is the affirmation of the conditional and that the judgment about the parts of the disjunction is upon all the subject, and its productive conjunctions are eight conjunctions, and the conclusion in all is a particular affirmative conditional, whose antecedent is the consequent of the conditional and whose consequent is a disjunction from the parts of the disjunction: The first conjunction from two universal affirmatives, as our saying: 'Everything that is B is D and R' and 'Always every B is J, either H or A' so 'It may be that when D is R, then either H or A'. The second conjunction: the conditional in its state, except that the disjunction is universal negative, as our saying: 'Not at all: either every B is J, H or A' so 'It may be that when D is R, then not at all every B is J, A or H or A'. The third conjunction: the conditional in its state, except that the disjunction is particular affirmative, as our saying: 'It may be that every B is J, either H or A' so 'It may be that when D is R, it may be that every B is J, either H or A'. The fourth conjunction: the conditional in its state, except that the disjunction is particular negative, as our saying: 'Not always every B is J, either H or A'. And if the conditional is particular affirmative, then there can be composed from it with the disjunction another four conjunctions parallel to these four, so consider them. And as for if the antecedent is one of the parts of the disjunction, then the condition for its productivity is the affirmation of the conditional, and that the disjunction be universal, so that the time of judgment does not differ. And if it is affirmative, then it must be a true exclusive disjunction (manī‘at al-jam‘ without al-khulū), and its productive conjunctions are four conjunctions, except that if the disjunction is affirmative, then the conclusion is a particular affirmative conditional, whose antecedent is the consequent of the conditional, and whose consequent is the negation of the part that has no share from the parts of the disjunction. And if the conditional is negative, then the conclusion is a particular negative disjunction of the consequent of the conditional and the part that has no share from the parts of the disjunction.
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage continues the analysis of mixed syllogisms, now considering two subtypes when the commonality is between the disjunctive and the antecedent of the conditional. First, the commonality can be by the antecedent itself (i.e., the antecedent is the subject of the disjunction). In that case, the author specifies conditions: the conditional must be affirmative, and the disjunction must apply to all instances of the subject. There are eight productive conjunctions (four main ones, plus four more when the conditional is particular affirmative). The conclusion is always a particular affirmative conditional with a disjunctive consequent. The author provides an example for the first conjunction where both premises are universal affirmative, showing how the conclusion follows. Second, the commonality can be that the antecedent itself is one of the disjuncts. Here the conditions differ: the conditional must be affirmative, and the disjunction must be universal and exclusive (manī‘at al-jam‘ without al-khulū). There are four productive conjunctions. If the disjunction is affirmative, the conclusion is a particular affirmative conditional that negates the non-shared part. If the conditional is negative, the conclusion becomes a particular negative disjunction. This passage requires careful parsing of the logical notation and understanding of the different types of disjunctions.

Mixed Syllogism: Antecedent as One of the Disjuncts
Second subtype: antecedent as one of the disjuncts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the validity of a mixed syllogism when the antecedent is one of the disjuncts and identify the conclusion's form.

Core Idea
When the antecedent is a disjunct, the disjunction must be exclusive and universal; the conclusion negates the non-shared part.

This lesson covers the second subtype where the antecedent of the conditional is itself one of the disjuncts in the disjunctive proposition. The author specifies stricter conditions: the conditional must be affirmative, the disjunction universal, and if affirmative, it must be an exclusive disjunction (māni‘at al-jam‘ dūn al-khulū). Understanding this allows you to evaluate arguments where a conditional premise shares its antecedent with a disjunctive as one of its alternatives.

Guided Path
1
Identify the common element
Here, the antecedent of the conditional appears as one of the disjuncts in the disjunctive. For example, if the antecedent is 'ب', then the disjunctive includes 'ب' as a disjunct.
2
State the conditions for productivity
The conditional must be affirmative. The disjunctive must be universal so that the judgment applies at all times. If the disjunctive is affirmative, it must be a 'pure exclusive' (māni‘at al-jam‘ without al-khulū) – meaning it excludes both disjuncts from being true together but allows both to be false.
There are four productive conjunctions in this subtype.
3
Analyze the conclusion for affirmative disjunctive
If the disjunctive is affirmative, the conclusion is a particular affirmative conditional. Its antecedent is the consequent of the original conditional, and its consequent is the negation of the disjunct that is not shared (the part not equal to the antecedent).
Example: If conditional 'If B then C' and disjunctive 'Either B or D', conclusion: 'It may be that if C then not D'.
4
Analyze the conclusion for negative conditional
If the conditional is negative, the conclusion changes to a particular negative disjunction of the consequent and the non-shared disjunct.
The structure reflects the interplay between the two premises.

Four Pairings of Uniform Disjunctive Syllogisms
Author lists four pairings of conditional and disjunctive premises with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the four productive pairings of a compound syllogism when the parts of the disjunctive premise are uniform in quality and quantity.

Core Idea
Uniform disjunctive premises yield eight productive pairings; the result is always a particular affirmative conditional.

In this passage, the author details the first four productive pairings (qara'in) of compound syllogisms where the disjunctive premise's parts are uniform. Understanding these patterns allows you to combine conditional and disjunctive premises to derive valid conclusions. We will walk through each pairing and see how the quality and quantity of the disjunctive affects the conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Understand the general condition for productiveness
The passage states two conditions: the conditional must be affirmative, and the judgment about the disjunctive parts must apply to every subject. These conditions ensure the syllogism yields a valid result.
2
Identify the four pairings based on the disjunctive premise
The four pairings differ only in the quality and quantity of the disjunctive premise: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative. The conditional remains affirmative in all.
The passage lists the pairings in order: (1) both universal affirmative, (2) universal negative, (3) particular affirmative, (4) particular negative.
3
Recognize the result structure
The conclusion is always a particular affirmative conditional whose antecedent is the consequent of the original conditional, and whose consequent is a disjunction formed from the parts of the disjunctive.
Thus the conclusion always begins with 'it may be that whenever... then...' (قد يكون إذا كان... فـ...).
4
Apply the patterns with real Islamic examples
Try to replace the term variables with real concepts from Fiqh or Kalam to see how this logical tool works in practice. For instance, use a conditional about salah and a disjunctive about sincerity.
This step builds familiarity with abstract terms by grounding them in known Islamic sciences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

الاقتران الثالث المتصلة بحالها، إلا أن المنفصلة جزئية موجبة كقولنا وقد يكون كل "ب" "ج" إما "هـ" وإما "أ") فـ (قد يكون إذا كان "د" "ر" فقد يكون كل "ب" "ج" إما "هـ" وإما "أ").
الاقتران الرابع المتصلة بحالها، إلا أن المنفصلة جزئية سالبة، كقولنا: و(ليس دائماً كل "ب" "ج" إما "هـ" وإما "أ").
If the parts of the disjunctive [premise] are uniform in quality and quantity, then the condition for its productiveness is the affirmation of the conditional [premise] and that the judgment about the parts of the disjunction applies to every subject. Its productive couplings are eight conjunctions, and the result in all is a particular affirmative conditional proposition, whose antecedent is the consequent of the conditional [premise] and whose consequent is a disjunction from the parts of the disjunctive [premise]: The first coupling from two universal affirmatives, like our saying: 'Everything that is B is D and R, and (always every B is either H or A)' so '(it may be that whenever D is R, then it is either H or A).' The second coupling: the conditional remains as it is, but the disjunctive is universal negative, like our saying: 'And (absolutely not: either every B is C is H or A)' so '(it may be that whenever D is R, then absolutely not every B is C A either H or A).' The third coupling: the conditional remains as it is, but the disjunctive is particular affirmative, like our saying: 'And (it may be that every B is C either H or A)' so '(it may be that whenever D is R, then it may be that every B is C either H or A).' The fourth coupling: the conditional remains as it is, but the disjunctive is particular negative, like our saying: 'And (not always every B is C either H or A).'
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage defines the productive pairings (qara'in) of compound syllogisms when the disjunctive premise has parts that are uniform in quality and quantity. The author states that for such a syllogism to be productive, the conditional premise must be affirmative, and the judgment about the parts of the disjunction must apply to the entire subject. There are eight such pairings in total; here the first four are detailed. The result is always a particular affirmative conditional proposition whose antecedent is the consequent of the original conditional, and whose consequent is a disjunction formed from the parts of the disjunctive premise. The four pairings differ according to the quality and quantity of the disjunctive premise: first, with two universal affirmatives; second, with a universal negative disjunctive; third, with a particular affirmative; fourth, with a particular negative. The author provides schematic examples using the letters B, J, D, R, H, A as term variables. For instance, in the first pairing, the conditional 'every D is R' and the disjunctive 'always every B is either H or A' yield the conclusion 'it may be that whenever D is R, then it is either H or A'.

Four Additional Pairings with a Particular Conditional
Author states there are four more parallel pairings if the conditional is particular affirmative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to generate the four additional pairings that arise when the conditional proposition is particular affirmative by analogy with the universal conditional pairings.

Core Idea
The logical structure of pairings remains the same; only the quantity of the conditional changes.

This passage extends the earlier discussion of four pairings between a conditional and a disjunctive proposition. When the conditional is universal affirmative, four specific pairings are productive. The author now points out that if the conditional is particular affirmative, there are four more parallel pairings. The reader is invited to derive them by analogy. This expansion completes the total count of productive composite syllogisms involving a conditional and a disjunctive.

Guided Path
1
Recall the four universal pairings
From the previous lesson, list the four pairings when the conditional is universal affirmative. For example: (1) universal conditional + universal disjunctive (man'at al-jam'), (2) universal conditional + universal exclusive disjunctive (man'at al-khuluw), etc. Each pairing yields a specific conclusion.
The pattern involves varying the quality and quantity of the disjunctive while keeping the conditional universal.
2
Identify the change in quantity
The conditional is now particular affirmative (e.g., 'Sometimes if A then B'). This means the antecedent-consequent relation holds only for some instances, not all. This change affects the strength of the conclusion but not the structural pairing with the disjunctive.
The disjunctive remains as before; only the conditional's quantity changes.
3
Generate the four new pairings by analogy
For each of the original four pairings, replace the universal conditional with a particular conditional. Keep the disjunctive's quality and quantity identical to the corresponding universal pairing. Write out the new composite forms explicitly.
The author says 'so consider them' (fā'labirhā), meaning you are to analogously construct them.
4
Verify the productivity
Check whether the conclusion still follows logically from the premises. Although the conditional is weaker, the same logical connections (e.g., via modus ponens or tollens within the conditional, combined with the disjunctive's exclusion) should still yield valid conclusions, albeit only in the scope where the conditional holds.
This step confirms that the parallel pairings are indeed productive and not merely formal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كانت المتصلة جزئية موجبة، فقد يأتلف منها مع المنفصلة اقترانات أخرى أربعة موازية لهذه الأربعة، فاعتبرها.
And if the conditional is particular affirmative, then from it and the disjunctive there may be composed another four pairings parallel to these four, so consider them.
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This brief passage adds that the productive pairings are not limited to the four when the conditional is universal affirmative. If the conditional is particular affirmative, there are an additional four pairings that parallel the previous four with respect to the disjunctive's quality and quantity. The author says 'so consider them' (fā'labirhā), meaning the reader should analogously construct the pairings using the same pattern, since the logical structure is similar. This completes the total of eight pairings mentioned earlier.

Compound Syllogism with Antecedent as Part of Disjunctive
Conditions and four pairings for compound syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two necessary conditions and construct the first two pairings of a compound syllogism where the antecedent is a part of the disjunctive premise.

Core Idea
When the antecedent is inside the disjunction, the conditional must be affirmative and the disjunction universal.

This lesson delves into a specific type of compound syllogism where the antecedent of a conditional premise is also one of the disjuncts of a disjunctive premise. The author sets two essential conditions and describes four productive pairings, detailing the first two with examples. Mastering these rules will allow you to derive correct conclusions in complex arguments, such as those found in kalam proofs about the world's creation.

Guided Path
1
Verify the two conditions
Confirm that the conditional (muttasila) is affirmative and the disjunctive (munfasila) is universal. The affirmation ensures the antecedent is present, and universality keeps the time of judgment consistent.
2
Classify the disjunction type
If the disjunctive is affirmative, it must be the 'man'at al-jam' duna al-khulu' type (preventing both together but allowing both false). This is exclusive disjunction.
3
Apply the first pairing: both universal affirmatives
Take a universal affirmative conditional (e.g., 'Everything that is B is C') and a universal affirmative disjunctive (e.g., 'Always either B is C or H is A'). The result is a particular affirmative conditional: 'It may be that when D is R, then it is not H A'.
Here 'D is R' replaces the original consequent? Actually the passage uses 'D is R' as a new variable. The result's antecedent is the consequent of the original conditional (C? unclear). For teaching, note that the conclusion's form is particular affirmative conditional with the consequent negating the unshared disjunct.
4
Apply the second pairing: affirmative conditional, negative disjunctive
Keep the same conditional, but use a universal negative disjunctive: 'Absolutely not either B is C or H is A'. The result is a particular negative disjunction: 'Not always either D is R or H A'.
The passage includes a brief justification: if H A were contrary to D R, it would be contrary to B C, which contradicts the lack of contrariety.
5
Observe the pattern for the remaining pairings
When the conditional is particular affirmative, two analogous pairings (third and fourth) follow on the same pattern as the first two. The author leaves these for the reader to derive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كان المقدم هو أحد أجزاء المنفصلة، فشرط إنتاجه إيجاب المتصلة، وأن تكون المنفصلة كليّة؛ حتى لا يختلف وقت الحكم. وإن كانت موجبة فإن تكون حقيقية مانعة الجمع دون الخلو، وقرائنه أربعة اقترانات، إلا أنه إذا كانت المنفصلة موجبة، فالنتيجة جزئية موجبة متصلة، مقدمها تالي المتصلة، وتاليها سلب الجزء الذي لا اشتراك فيه من أجزاء المنفصلة. وإن كانت المتصلة سالبة فالنتيجة جزئية سالبة منفصلة من تالي المتصلة والجزء الذي لا اشتراك فيه من أجزاء المنفصلة:
الاقتران الأول من كليتين موجبتين، كقولنا : كل ما كان "ب" "ج" فـ(؟) و(دائماً وإما "ب" "ج" وإما "هـ" "أ") فـ (قد يكون إذا كان "د" "ر" فلا يكون "هـ" "أ").
الاقتران الثاني: المتصلة بحالها، إلا أن المنفصلة كلية سالبة، كقولنا: (وليس البتة إما "ب" "ج" وإما "هـ" "أ") ونتيجته ليس دائماً إما "د" "ر" وإما "هـ" "أ") لأنه إذا كان ("د" "ر") فـ (قد يكون "ب" "ج") فلو كان ( "هـ" "أ") معانداً لـ ("د" "ر") لكان معانداً لـ "ب" "ج") وقد قيل لا عناد.
وإن كانت المتصلة جزئية موجبة، فقد يأتلف منها مع المنفصلة اقترانان آخران على نسق هذين الاقترانين فاعتبرها.
And if the antecedent is one of the parts of the disjunctive, then the condition for its productiveness is the affirmation of the conditional, and that the disjunctive be universal, so that the time of judgment does not differ. And if it (the disjunctive) is affirmative, then it must be true, [i.e.] preventing conjunction without [preventing] voidness. Its productive couplings are four pairings, except that when the disjunctive is affirmative, the result is a particular affirmative conditional whose antecedent is the consequent of the conditional and whose consequent is the negation of the part that has no participation in the parts of the disjunctive. And if the conditional is negative, the result is a particular negative disjunction from the consequent of the conditional and the part that has no participation in the parts of the disjunctive: The first coupling from two universal affirmatives, like our saying: 'Everything that is B is C, and (always either B is C or H is A)' so '(it may be that when D is R, then it is not H A).' The second coupling: the conditional remains as it is, but the disjunctive is universal negative, like our saying: '(Absolutely not either B is C or H is A)' and its result is 'Not always either D is R or H A' because when (D is R) then (it may be B is C), and if (H A) were contrary to (D R) it would be contrary to (B C), and it was said [that] there is no contrariety. And if the conditional is particular affirmative, then from it and the disjunctive there may be composed two other pairings on the pattern of these two pairings, so consider them.
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage discusses a different type of compound syllogism where the antecedent (muqaddam) of the conditional is itself one of the parts of the disjunctive premise. The author sets two conditions: the conditional must be affirmative, and the disjunctive must be universal to ensure uniformity in the time of judgment. If the disjunctive is affirmative, it must be a true disjunction that prevents conjunction (man' al-jam') but not voidness (without khulu), meaning it is exclusive disjunction (either one or the other, but not both). There are four productive pairings. When the disjunctive is affirmative, the result is a particular affirmative conditional: its antecedent is the consequent of the original conditional, and its consequent is the negation of the part of the disjunction not shared (i.e., the part that is not the antecedent). When the conditional is negative, the result is a particular negative disjunction between the consequent and the unshared part. The author then gives two example pairings: the first with two universal affirmatives, the second with a universal negative disjunctive. The second pairing includes a brief argument showing that the result follows because if the contrary relation held, it would contradict the lack of contrariety. Finally, if the conditional is particular affirmative, there are two more pairings analogous to the first two, left to the reader.

Structure and Conditions of Compound Syllogisms (Commonality in Antecedent)
Author introduces conditions for a compound syllogism with commonality in the antecedent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of a compound syllogism where commonality lies in a part of the antecedent and verify the conditions for a valid first-figure form.

Core Idea
When the common term is the antecedent's subject, the syllogism mirrors the first figure with strict conditions.

This passage delves into a specific type of compound syllogism combining a conditional and a disjunctive premise. The author focuses on the scenario where the common element resides in a part of the conditional's antecedent. By understanding the structural conditions—affirmation, negation, and universality—you will be able to dissect such arguments and assess their validity.

Guided Path
1
Locate the commonality
Determine whether the common term (al-ishtirak) occurs in the predicate or subject of the antecedent (muqaddam) of the conditional. The passage treats the case where it is in the predicate of the antecedent.
If it is in the subject, a different analysis applies, not covered here.
2
Identify the role of the common term in the disjunctive
Check whether the common term is the subject or predicate in the parts of the disjunction (afza' al-infisal). The passage focuses on the case where it is the subject in the disjunctive parts.
This mirrors the first figure (shakl awwal) where the middle term is subject in one premise and predicate in the other.
3
Verify the conditions for the first figure
Ensure (1) the antecedent of the conditional is affirmed if the conditional is affirmative, and negated if negative; (2) if the conditional is negative, it must be universal; (3) the parts of the disjunction are universal (judgment applies to every subject).
These conditions guarantee a valid inference in the first-figure style.
4
Recognize the form of the result
The conclusion (natija) is a conditional whose antecedent is the consequent (tali) of the original conditional, and whose consequent is the result of coupling the disjunctive with the antecedent. Note that the result may be particular even if all premises are universal.
This abstract form is key to generating the 24 pairings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كان الاشتراك بجزء المقدم فإما بمحموله أو بموضوعه، فإن كان بمحموله فإما أن يكون موضوعاً لأجزاء الانفصال أو محمولاً فيها، فإن كان موضوعاً لأجزاء الانفصال فهو على هيئة الشكل الأول، فشرطه إيجاب المقدم من المتصلة إن كانت موجبة وسلبه إن كانت سالبة، وأن تكون المتصلة كلية إن كانت سالبة، وأن تكون أجزاء الانفصال كلية - أي الحكم بها على كل الموضوع - وقرائنه المنتجة باعتبار كليّة المقدمتين وجزئيتهما، وسلبهما وإيجابهما، وإيجاب المقدم وسالبه أربعة وعشرون ،اقتراناً، وخاصته أن النتيجة فيه تكون متصلة مقدمها تالي المتصلة، وتاليها نتيجة اقتران المنفصلة بالمقدم ، وأنها قد تكون جزئية، وإن كانت المقدمات كليّة
الاقتران الأول من كليّة موجبة متصلة مقدمها كلي موجب، وكلية موجبة منفصلة كليّة الموضوع كقولنا: (كل) ما كان كل "ب" "ج" فـ "د" "ر") و(دائماً كل "ج" إما "هـ" وإما "أ") ونتيجته قد يكون إذا كان "د" "ر" فكل "ب" دائماً إما "هـ" وإما "أ")
الاقتران الثاني: المتصلة بحالها، إلا أن المنفصلة كلية سالبة، كقولنا: ودائماً كل "ج" ليس إما "هـ" وإما "أ") فـ (قد يكون إذا كان "د" "ر" فليس البتة إما كل "ر" "هـ" وإما "أ").
And if the commonality is in a part of the antecedent, then it is either in its predicate or its subject. If it is in its predicate, then it is either a subject for the parts of the disjunction or a predicate in them. If it is a subject for the parts of the disjunction, then it is in the form of the first figure. Its condition is the affirmation of the antecedent of the conditional if it is affirmative, and its negation if it is negative; and that the conditional be universal if it is negative; and that the parts of the disjunction be universal—that is, the judgment about them applies to every subject. Its productive couplings, considering the universality and particularity of the two premises, their negation and affirmation, and the affirmation and negation of the antecedent, are twenty-four pairings. Its characteristic is that the result in it is a conditional whose antecedent is the consequent of the conditional, and whose consequent is the result of coupling the disjunctive with the antecedent; and that it may be particular even if the premises are universal. The first pairing from a universal affirmative conditional whose antecedent is universal affirmative, and a universal affirmative disjunctive with universal subject, like our saying: 'Everything that is every B is C, then D is R' and 'Always every C is either H or A', and its result: 'It may be that when D is R, then every B is always either H or A.' The second pairing: the conditional remains as it is, but the disjunctive is universal negative, like our saying: 'And always every C is not either H or A', then '(it may be that when D is R, then absolutely not every R is either H or A).'
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage discusses the case where the commonality (al-ishtirāk) between the conditional and disjunctive premises lies in a part of the antecedent (muqaddam) of the conditional. The common part can be the predicate or subject of the antecedent. If it is the predicate, it can be subject or predicate in the disjunctive parts. When it is subject in the disjunctive, the syllogism takes the form of the first figure (al-shakl al-awwal). Conditions are given: the antecedent of the conditional must be affirmed if the conditional is affirmative, and negated if it is negative; if the conditional is negative, it must be universal; and the parts of the disjunction must be universal (the judgment applies to every subject). Considering all variations of quantity and quality of the premises, there are 24 productive pairings. The result is always a conditional whose antecedent is the consequent of the original conditional, and whose consequent is the result of applying the disjunctive to the antecedent. Notably, the result may be particular even if all premises are universal. The author provides two example pairings: the first with universal affirmative premises, the second with a universal negative disjunctive.
